THE RED PAGE.
This by request. (It appeared in a book oalled
' Among the Flowers and Other Poems,’ Belfast
1878. J. Churton Collins, reprinting in his recent
‘ Treasury of Minor British Poetry,’ writes : ‘ In
the original the last verse runs “when love is
done." I have taken the liberty to substitute
“gone” for “done,” feeling sure that “done”
must be a misprint ’ —a quite unnecessary feat of
editorial impudence.)
LIGHT.
The night has a thousand eyes
And the day but one ;
Yet the light of the bright world dies
With the dying sun
The mind has a thousand eyes,
And the heart but one ;
Yet the light of a whole life dies
When love is done.
F. W. Bourdillon.
» * * *
Some time ago it was pointed out that many
valuable books had been lost or stolen from Sydney
Publio Library. The other day the collection—
a national heirloom —was dispersed a little further
by the sale of about 5000 volumes for J>219—or a
little under Is. per volume. Many of these books
were comparatively worthless—old Parliamentary
papers, pamphlets, hand-books, and so on ; but a
number were among the most important examples
of literature relating to early Australia—‘the
eyes of the Library,’ in fact. (The phrase cer-
tainly applies to Surgeon White’s ‘ Journal ’—the
author’s own copy was sold; to Bennett’s ‘Wan-
derings’; to Kotzebue’s ‘Voyage’; to the 1809
edition of Cook’s ‘Voyages,’ in seven volumes;
and to many more rare books of the kind
which in a few score years, as population in-
creases, will be almost priceless to Australia. A
complete list of books sold should be obtained by
Parliament.) The scandal iB not diminished by
the fact that among the books sold were some
bequeathed by the late Mr. Justice Wise —a dis-
graceful breach of trust. These were deliberately
signed and stamped with the library stamp
authorising sale, so no pretext of accidental error
can hold —if accidental error were possible to men
with any book sense. The management of the
Sydney Public Library is attracting attention in
so many ways that a public enquiry into its
methods and results seems urgently needed.
* * * *
John Cordy Jeaffreson, whose ‘ Lady Hamilton
and Lord Nelson ” has been issued in a new and
considerably-altered edition, is a literary hobbyist
with no qualifications for historical enquiry. By
dint of studying the ghost of Lady Hamilton by
and large, he has been fascinated as every man
who studied her by and large was fascinated in
the flesh. So the whole duty of a biographer, as
announced by John Cordy, is to whitewash his
subject in accordance with conventional ideas;
and John Cordy has done his duty nobly. Occa-
sionally the whitewash slops over upon Nelson
—who occasionally needs it, even according to
unconventional ideas. Not that there was
anything particularly blameworthy in his
connection with Lady Hamilton — except
maybe his taste. But Nelson’s was a
sailor’s taste —he liked his women big and
blowsy, and (luckily for Lady H.) was not too
particular about the delicacy of their sentiments
or the size of their feet. Jeaffreson enquires how
many children his heroine had; and dubiously
decides on four—two by Nelson, and two from
alien sources; but ‘ no discreet biographer . . .
would like to state positively the precise number
of her children.’ Enquiry as to the number of
Lady Hamilton’s lovers is even less satisfactory.
WasNelson the forty-first? or the four hundred and
first ? The Lord knows ; but ‘ Pass ! ’ murmurs the
judicious John Cordy Jeaffreson. The valiant
biographer finds that in the transfer from Greville
to Hamilton—one of the most curious transactions
of the kind recorded—the lady was animated only
by the noblest notions.
Nobody who knew the space for sir william
lady intimately would
ever dream of suspecting
otherwise. And the in-
telligent biographer finds
that Sir William H anil-
ton, diplomatist and anti-
quarian, was convinced
(doubtless by his know-
ledge of the lady’s past)
that Nelson’s presence in
his household was due
merely to a pale-green Hamilton’s subtle smile.
Platonic affinity, or
thereabouts. If you have any acquaintance with
Sir William, and look carefully into the marginal
space, you will catch a fleeting glimpse of Sir
William’s subtle smile.
• • * *
‘ George Egerton’s ’ new book, * Symphonies,’ of
right belongs to the ‘Keynotes ’ series which she
commenced ; but the ‘ Keynotes ’ series is marked
at 4s., and ‘ George Egerton ’ has so much
evanescent reputation that her publisher judges
the public will pay for ‘ Symphonies ’ 5s. or 5s. 6d.
—according to the bookseller’s conscience. Any-
one beginning acquaintance with ‘ George Eger-
ton ’ had better buy ‘ Keynotes ’ or * Discords’ at
4s. The ‘Symphonies’ are played in precisely
the same key of woman’s sex-impulses, and the
playing seems to have less force—or it may be
only less novelty. The author’s faculty of keen
observation and brilliant picturing continues to
make her stories readable ; but somehow the
bloom is off the literary rye of the Woman Who
Talks About It. Curious, is it not? how few
Women really Do compared with the vast number
who courageously Talk About It.
Some little items—
Mrs. Oliphant, who died the other day, was a weaker
‘George Eliot.’ She had ‘George Eliot’s’ oatholio cul-
ture without her capaoity for philosophic generalisation:
her skill to tell a storj' without her power. Among Mrs
Oliphant’s many novels are two or three interesting, and
none memorable.
H. G. Wells' new collection of magazine tales, ‘The
Plattner Story and Others,’ is a very readable lot. For
weird imagination perhaps Wells’ most prominent con-
temporary rival is a young American—Robert W. Cham-
bers—who writes even more uncannily, though with
somewhat less invention.
A statue of Guy de Maupassant by Raoul Yerlet was to be
exhibited at this year’s Salon. ‘ Figaro ’ gives the follow-
ing description : —‘ Upon a circular bench of stone, abovB
which stands the bust of Maupassant, is seated a young
woman, whose left hand holds a half-open book, “Notre
Coeur.” Charmed by the story, the reader .... is
lost in a deep reverie. Would she like to have been the
heroine? Is she thinking of the author? One does not
know. But she dreams, and will make others dream.
Her right arm rests gently upon the back of the bench,
and one can almost hear the heart beating beneath the
admirably-modelled breast. The figure is very fine, the
drapery excellent. Below the skirt appear the crossed
feet.’ After the Salon, the statue will be placed in the
Parc Monceaux.
‘ Rolf Boldrewood ’ must prize money highly or reputa-
tion lowly to attach his name to suoh rubbish as ‘ My
Run Home ’ —just issued by Macmillan and Co. The
book, which hashes and elaborates dull diary mems.
hardly deserves journal print, let alone reprint.
P.A.: ‘A recent “Bulletin” insinuation re the unspeak-
able naughtiness of Shelley’s “Laon and Cythna ” is
hardly well founded, for a modern divoroe-case can give
the poem points and a beating. No doubt Shelley's
original plan of making the lovers brother and sister was
rather strong, but it took something pungent to appeal
to that diseased imagination of his, which, unlike his
“ Skylark,” knew so much of “ love’s sad satiety.” The
worst feature of the poem is that the lovers spent their
honeymoon in a cave, and for weeks never washed them-
selves or changed their clothes, not being able to carry
trunks on the one coal-black steed which bore them away.
I wonder how some of Shelley’s gushing maiden admirers
would like to spend the first four days of their honey-
moon on the rocky floor of a oave without food, and be
satisfied when Laon, returning from a foraging expedition
with some bread whioh be stole from a crazy woman’s
hut, fed them sumptuously on bread and water ? The
poet doesn’t say how stale the bread was, but I hardly
think the crazy woman could have baked that morning,
as she was too busy raving around the dead bodies of her
ohildren, which strewed the floor in various directions.’
* * * *
‘ The Well-Beloved,’Thomas Hardy’s recently-
published story, was serially issued in 1892. It is
almost as far from the palpitating romance of his
earlier method as from the tense realism of ‘ Tess ’
and ‘Jude.’ And it is a book which will hardly
help his reputation : it is too faint in treatment
and vague in impression—mere summer-lightning
of literature. The author hints knowledge of
this in his sub-title : ‘ A Sketch of a Tempera-
ment.’ Plot: a dreamer of elusive feminine
ideals meets her who nearly approximates his
most permanent ideal : engages to wed, and flies
to pursue another approximation who captures
his momentary fancy, yet cannot hold it. The re-
linquished A vice—is it not a pretty name?—
marries and dies, leaving in her stead another
Avice whom also, twenty years later, the Idealist
covets —for her mother’s grace he sees in
her. Disappointed, he returns after other
twenty years—a man of sixty—to find that
a third Avice has bloomed and grown to
charm him as her grandmother charmed. He
stretches his hand, and a third time the Ideal
eludes him. Nothing remains but to end in death
a lifetime of regrets. It is prettily, fancifully,
artistically told ; and the type-moral of the story
is rightly left as an emanation, an iucense-breath,
instead of being crushed into crude words. The
scene is the isle of Portland, and the intrusion of
local custom-traditions—as the ante-nuptial ‘ prov-
ing’ of a wife’s capacity, still common among
Lancashire operatives and others—adds to the
book’s quaint flavour.
» * * *
‘ The Well-Beloved ’ is for the elect of Henry
James and Meredith to dream over : a fantasy
rather than a solid fact of letters. If mere force
were desired, indeed, the theme would have
seemed more forcible in shorter clothes : Maupas-
sant has so dressed a not dissimilar idea in ‘Les
Soeurs Rondoli.’ The current cheap translations
of Maupassant are so eagerly gobbled in Austra-
lia that many people must have chuckled over
Pierre Jouvenet, Parisian, who yearned to cap his
culture by making acquaintance with the artistic
masterpieces of Italy, and journeyed hopefully
one spring-time ... as far as Genoa, where
Francesca Rondoli held him till his holiday ex-
pired. And a second time he set out to
realise his dreams of Venice, and Florence,
and Rome . . . and at Genoa Carlotta Rondoli
claimed him till the last moment before
return. ‘But,’ says the gallant fellow in one of
Maupassant’s inimitable endings, ‘I still reckon
on going to see Italy one of these days—always in
reflecting, with a certain anxiety not unmingled
with hope, that Madam Rondoli has two more
daughters.’
* * * *
Maupassant has ‘inquietude d’espoirs’;
and ‘ mingled ’ was first written above, and struck
out to get the effect of the double negative—one of
the loveliest effects in literary English. There is
a delicious example in the first two sentences of
‘Ginx’s Baby.’ ‘The father of Ginx’s baby was
Ginx. The mother, by a not unexceptional coinci-
dence, was Mrs. Ginx.’ That nectarine ‘not un-
exceptional ’ touches one almost as G. W. L.
Marshall-Hall is touched when he falls on her
‘quick-panting bosom scarce conscious,’ and sears
with burning lips her ‘emulous hungering mouth,’
and enthrals with strong limbs her ‘weak-yielding
frame that is shaken with shuddering bliss,’ and
so on, and so on, to adjectival infinity.
* * * *
Mr. Marshall-Hall’s ‘ Hymn to Sydney ’ is by
no means so bad as untutored Philistines allege.
There is not very much force in it, or very much
novelty, and the bard has occasionally the aspect
of an embodied hysteria bucked from a verbal
nightmare ; but phrases like ‘the azure effulgence
of high - storeyed heaven’ leave a reasonable
Svvinburnian taste in the mouth ; and verses like
For joy is for aye beauty’s follower, even as stars
By gleams on waste waters are followed, as blossoms by
fruit,
Spraying fountains by rainbows, and night by soft dews ;
nothing mars
The gentle ooncordanoe of Nature’s sweet low-voicM
lute
Whioh breathes through the heart as faint airs through a
forest of pines,
Till it quivers and throbs, like their leaves, with strange
melodies scarce it divines.
are good to see and remember for a little while.
The real scandal of the poem is that it purports to
hymn Sydney, and apparently (for it is too wild
and whirly to declare anything certainly regard-
ing it) darts suddenly away from the subject to
hymn another girl altogether—lrene, possibly;
and Irene has already had as much incense as any
mortal girl’s altar will hold. This is not fair to
Sydney—who can complain, besides, of the gum-
tree and the Chinaman’s garden which slander
her in the frontispiece. It would be better to con-
vert the picture of the poet playing the flute to
his goddess (or gnawing a bone and refusing to
give her any : she looks sulky, and these depres-
sionistio sketches are so vague) into a frontis-
piece for the ninth edition.
* * * •
The author of ‘ Ginx’s Baby,’ Edward Jenkins,
to whom reference was being made when a meteor
fell across the track, has just published ‘Pan-
talas,’ a satirical commentary on the way in
which England treats her poverty problem.
Jenkins’ criticism is futile enough, since he is
content merely to echo condemnation of existing
agencies for reform. Like ‘by-and-by,’ ‘ damn ! ’
is easily said. So the value of ‘ Pantalas ’isas a
mental stimulant, not as a mental food. Inciden-
tally Jenkins emphasises the truth that the Sal-
vation Army is a vast sweating-shop, where the
last atom of force is screwed out of mental or
physical weaklings employed at starvation wages
—in the name of a most merciful God.
* * * •
A rather hasty perusal finds no mention of the
Salvation Army in the new volume of Justin
McCarthy’s ‘History of Our Own Times’; and
such a remarkable excrescence on the corpus of
superstition and charity was surely worth a para-
graph. But the omission is characteristic. McCar-
thy, claiming to be a historian of England, has
little or nothing to say of the social condition of
the people of England during seventeen moment-
ous years. To judge from a passing reference to
‘those terrible problems of social life which so
many writers of later years have been thrusting
under our eyes with futile persistency, as if we
could possibly settle them in any case,’ it looks as
if McCarthy deliberately refused to bother his
head about social conditions. The philosophy
which consists in declaring that the worst
human ills can’t be mended, and that there-
fore it is a waste of time and energy to
try and mend them, is certainly a comfortable
philosophy for well-to-do people. As Mr. Jenkins
points out, ‘ the sublimity of egoism is to keep the
system free from shudders.’
• * * •
But in truth, McCarthy’s attitude to all ques-
tions of sociology is that of a languid dilettante.
In his earlier volumes he forced himself to take a
little thought for the state and prospects of the
less prosperous classes by whose blood and sweat
the communal edifice is cemented ; but his heart
is not in such matters. Omar Khayykm’s bread
and wine and verses sum the constituents of
Justin McCarthy’s felicity (there is no evidence
at hand to show that he wants a lady to sing
be-dde him). He is attracted by politics, but
rather by the personal and intellectual combat
than by the result—though all but all the value
of politics is in the result. For the rest,
McCarthy has a good deal of an Irishman’s
patriotism, and all a cultured Irishman’s love of
art and letters. Science does not interest him ;
and the new volume is blank as to scientific pro-
gress since 1880. So that as a ‘ history ’ it is mis-
named. It is really a gossiping chronicle of the
course of British politics as viewed by a clever
Irishman, with occasional remarks on literary
and artistic figures as they pass from the scene—
a pleasant commentary and pithless.
• * » *
Compared with the elder volumes, this is flatter
and duller. There is less vitality in the author,
and less vivacity in the book. In those elder
volumes McCarthy employs the method which
any brilliant journalist would employ in dealing
with contemporary life. His literary style,
modelled on Macaulay’s, is terse, clear, and vigor-
ous. It lacks a good deal of Macaulay’s epi-
grammatic glitter, and something of his strength
and fulness. Yet McCarthy’s history, less clever
than that of his great predecessor, is no less
entertaining. One’s interest never flags. If
there are wanting passages of transcendent power,
the book yet reaches a level height much above
mediocrity. The pictures of noted individuals, in
particular, are portrait gems. They are limned
with a few sentences that make the presentment
strikingly vivid ; and the panorama in which they
stand is displayed with a skill hardly less attrac-
tive.
* * * *
The new volume maintains the method, but
more feebly. It is less terse, less clear, less
vigorous, much nearer mediocrity. The author has
taken slighter pains, or possessed seanter energy.
His impressions are not so strong, his pictures
not so brilliant, his criticism not so valuable. The
artifices of style by which he holds or creates
interest were sometimes obvious: now, they are
occasionally irritating. Formerly the reader was
rejoiced by felicitous touches of wit or imagery.
It was clever, for example, to say that in 1834
China’s prayer to Europe was that of Diogenes to
Alexander, ‘ Stand out of my sunshine ! ’ It was
clever to compare Russia’s seemingly inconsistent
Turkish policy to the course of a sailing ship
which now tacks to this side and now to that, but
has a clear object in view and a port to reach all
the while. It was a pretty thing to say of
O’Connell’s varying eloquence that as a shadow
runs over a field so its impression ran over an
Irish assemblage. No such memorable allusions
give point and charm to the new volume.
» * * *
This comparison, of course, is inevitable, and
inevitably depreciatory to the fifth volume of ‘ A
History of Our Own Times.’ Positively, the
summary of English and Irish politics from 1880
to date (Jameson's raid is discussed with a
grave imitation of the best historical manner)
is often interesting, and rarely admirable.
(Ireland naturally gets more than its share of
space; and McCarthy is not concerned about
Scotland or Wales or ‘ the colonies and depen-
dencies’). The personal appreciations, too —
though colored by the author’s intimacies—are
readable. ‘ The dignity of History ’ has seldom
had a more agreeable mocker than Justin
McCarthy.
• * * *
The thirteenth volume of ‘The Yellow Book’
has two or three nice drawings—notably a glimpse
of ‘Winter Evening on the Clyde,’ by Muirhead
Bone; and the literature improves somewhat on
recent issues. A story by Ella D’Arcy closes
well; but this piece of verse, by Stephen Phillips,
is the most impressive item of the contents —
THE QUESTION.
Father, beneath the moonless night,
This heavy stillness without light,
There conies a thought which I must speak :
Why is my body then so weak ?
Why do I falter in the race,
And flag behind this mighty pace ?
Why is my strength so quickly flown?
And hark ! My mother sobs alone !
My son, when I was young and free,
When I was filled with sap and glee,
I squandered here and there my strength,
And to thy mother’s arms at length
Weary I came, and over-tired ;
With fever all my bones were fired.
Therefore so soon thy strength is flown.
Therefore thy mother sobs alone.
Father, since in your weaker thought,
And in your languor I was wrought,
Put me away, as creatures are ;
I am infirm and full of care.
Feebly you brought me to the light ;
Then softly hide me out of sight.
Now sooner will my strength be flown.
Nor will my mother sob alone.
My son, stir up the fire and pass
Quickly the comfortable glass !
The infirm and evil fly in vain
Is toiling up the window-pane. •
Fill up ! For life is so, nor sigh ;
We cannot run from destiny.
Then fire thy strength that’s quiokly flown . . .
Hark! how your mother sobs alone !

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EVOE BACCHE!
From pole to pole the wild Isaiahs cry
A curse upon the earth ; with yellow eye
They glare upon the wine-jars and the balm
That lifts our feet from paths of misery ;
And, furious that the sons of him enjoy
The Wine-God’s ecstacies that never cloy,
Shake in our eves the terrors of that pit
Where the fire b'urneth yet doth not destroy.
Still, though the crying of the saints endure
That hunt and harry us our ills to cure,
The rosy current of the grape runs yet,
We know the pleasant present is secure.
Just as the sea slides in upon the land
And smooths the furrows of the wrinkled sand,
So shall the God ye worship with this cup
Sooth all your sadness with a gracious hand.
The wisdom of an older day’s renown
Was wont with wine to wash its sorrows down,
Drink to the dregs the cup of each glad day,
Beneath the shadows of a roseleaf crown.
So let the ravings of the fools rebound
On their dull heads—great bombs of empty sound !
Lie still, and watch the gurgling amphora
With mellow laughter fill the air around.
MACHETE.
SONNET:
FOR THE KINGDOM OF HEAVEN'S SAKE.
“If thy hand offend thee, cut it off. It is better for
thee to enter into life maimed, than having two hands to
go into hell.” —Mark 9, 43.
“ In the resurrection they neither marry nor are given
in marriage.”—Matthew 23, 30.
Whole will I enter Heaven, or not at all;
I’ll keep my hands, though I should fail of
wings.
Is Heaven a place of maimed and sexless things,
Whose chiefest glory is—they cannot “ fall ” ?
No ; men and women, strong, majestical,
Full-facultied and free, shall taste the springs
Of life supreme—the larger life that brings
The higher joy, the mind more rational.
Maimed manhood, by the harsh Mosaic law,
Might not God’s earthly dwelling-place go in :
How, then, shall such in peerless glory dwell ?
Must fleshly pruning cure the spirit’s flaw ?
Then, rather than a Heaven of cripples win,
I’ll go, full-membered, cheerfully to hell !
M.L.
T. LIDDELL KELLY.
PELAGIA.
Glad captive, wooing while I win,
Across all borders let us float:
Come close, and let my lips drink in
The clinging moisture of your throat —
Light dew, that signals Love’s distress,
But drives away his burning drouth :
I lean above your face, and press
The aching corners of your mouth.
Your cheek against my temple crushed,
Your breath at riot on my eyes,
Dear dainty body hunger-flushed,
What are you under your disguise ? -
One sob struck from a silver bell,
One flaming star upon the night,
One perfect, passion-tinted shell,
One quivering rose of warm delight.
Once Aphrodite, love-distraught,
With sharp and sudden sigh was torn ;
Her scarlet lips a moment caught
The fragrance, dropped, and you were 1 c rn.
But this was—when ? Long ages sim e
Proud Alexandria knew your face ;
For you it was the stranger prince
Sold Gothic pride to Greek disgrace.
And even then you were not young,
When under porch and peristyle
The song that of yourself you sung
Went lilting down the lazy Nile.
What rivers have not echoed it ?
The ruined Rhine has often told :
Through Ganges valleys temple-lit
Its drowsy melody has rolled.
Or in some quaintly-carven ship
Where blue Assyrian helmets shone,
Its tune has trembled on your lip
Beneath the walls of Babylon.
And all those ancient lives of yours,
Those mystic lives, that came and went
Along the western battle-shores
Or in the poisoned Orient,
I know them flashing round us now
And firing you with subtle art,
I hear them beating on my brow,
I feel them crying in your heart.
I, even I, beneath their breath
Am backward borne : along with these
I tread the classic path of Death
That winds across the centuries.
Vic.
WYVIS.
DISCONTENT.
To shatter slumber with a stirring horn,
To goad the laggards on a long ascent,
To mate with man, till thunder-thoughts are born,
To shape the forces in a people pent—
Thy missions these, most noble Discontent!
The giant gyves of despots’ old constraint
Burst from thy frantic limbs. Thou dost present
Ever more fields to soldier, sage, and saint.
Thy gifts not always are the gifts we paint;
We seek a shining good through long unease,
Which jeering Fate forbids. What plea for
plaint ? , .
When hopes, fore-doomed, to vast and virgin
seas
Lure a Columbus, unsought lands arise ;
And phantom stars may tempt us to the skies !
N.S.W.
CECIL POOLE.

the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.

‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But w
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JULY 24, 1897.
The Sydney City Railway.
A few weeks ago the Sydney city railway
difficulty, after many years of delay and
conversation, seemed practically on the
verge of settlement. Since then some un-
expected opposition has arisen, and the
difficulty looks scarcely so near settlement
as it lately appeared. On two or three
matters connected with the trouble every-
body —or nearly everybody —is agreed ;
everything else is chaos. The Redfern ter-
minal station is too small, is eminently
dangerous because of its smallness, is obso-
lete and badly arranged, and is almost out of
the city in any case. Most of the great
cities of the world have railways running
right through them and one or more—some-
times a good many more—stations in the
city itself. Sydney has just one city station,
and it, by a paradox, is not in the city, and
lies a good way from the business centre.
Even though it can be shown that there are
other places as badly situated in this respect
as Sydney, that does not make the present
arrangement one whit less inefficient. And,
even supposing it should be demonstrated
that a city terminus isn’t essential now —
which is impossible—the fact remains that,
with increase of population, it must become
essential. Also, if it would be a costly work
to construct now, which is admitted, it is
certain to become more costly, through the
increased value of property arising from in-
creased population, later on. Consequently,
there is every reason why it should be made
and made at once, while property and ma-
terials are cheap, interest low, and work
urgently wanted, and before a huge butcher’s
bill, arising through some fatal accident in
the Redfern death-trap, conies along.
♦ * * *
The fact that a new terminus should be
constructed in the heart of Sydney is sup-
ported by so much convincing evidence that
nearly everybody is agreed so far. Almost
the only objection to the work is the wail of
the country politician to the effect that there
are many neglected regions in the backblocks
which want railway facilities much more than
the already favored metropolis. This state-
ment is perfectly true, but it is also wholly
irrelevant. It seems reasonably certain that
the city railway will pay working expenses
and interest on its cost, and that being so,
it should be constructed. If any backblocks
line can give equally good assurance to the
same effect it should be constructed also.
The fact of the city railway being con-
structed will not cause any other district,
which would otherwise have had a line, to go
without. The country can afford to build
every line which will pay working expenses
and interest on cost. The great difficulty,
approaching to impossibility, is to find dis-
tricts where a railway will do these things,
and there is a ghastly list of New South
Wales lines which don’t pay interest, and
very often don’t pay working expenses either.
• * * *
The site of the new central station is the
matter about which the real controversy
rages. The Government and the Depart-
ment desire to annex a portion of Hyde
Park, and plant the new structure about the
top of King-street. This will be almost the
exact centre of what is now the business por-
tion of Sydney. It will be adjacent to almost
everything that the Redfern station con-
spicuously lacks. The bill for compensation
between Redfern and the park will be mode-
rate, as there is comparatively little private
property in the way, therefore a great part
of the stupendous compensation swindle will
be avoided, and the local landlord will not
loot the State to anything like the extent he
usually does. It is only a part of Hyde Park
which will be required at present, and, as the
population of the city is constantly diminish-
ing, through the steady exodus to the
suburbs, the park, it is claimed, is by no
means so essential as it once was. More-
over, the Railway Department, in considera-
tion of getting the park gratis, undertakes to
provide, in lieu of it, a public pleasure-
ground in some other locality, where it is
possibly more urgently required. Finally,
as there will be few compensation questions
to settle, and very few buildings to pull
down, and hardly any engineering difficulties
to contend with, the new station could be
got ready sooner, if built on the proposed
site, than if it were placed in almost any
other part of Sydney. These are the main
features of the case for the Hyde Park loca-
tion, and, when they are all added up, they
make a very good case indeed.
* * * *
The case against the proposed King-street
terminus, however, looks quite as good or
better ; it greatly depends on the point of
view. The statement that it is only "necessary
to take a portion of Hyde Park is plausible
enough, but Sydney is still young, and there
is no possible reason why its 400,000 in-
habitants should not increase by-and-bye to
4,000,000 or even twice that number, and no
amount of provision will prevent a further
enlargement being necessary some day—
possibly before many years are past. If the
railway terminus once gets into Hyde Park,
it will assuredly spread over the whole of
that area sooner or later, and if the people
of Sydney are not prepared to sacrifice the
whole of it the scheme had better be dropped.
Also there seems to be no guarantee that the
new pleasure-ground to be provided in ex-
change will be anything like a fair equivalent
for the one sacrificed. And, in addition to
these considerations, the site itself, good as
it may be, is not altogether the ideal one
that it is represented to be.
* * * *
The most notable argument against it is
that the scheme now favoured apparently
implies the permanent abandonment of all
idea of an extension to North Shore. The
King-street site is too high to make a
continuation to North Shore possible
by tunnelling under the harbour—at least
as railway matters are now understood it
certainly seems so. To carry the line straight
across by means of a bridge would involve
crossing the harbor at a place where the
width is very great, and thereby incurring
enormous unnecessary expense, and it would
also mean that the access to Circular Quay
would be seriously blocked. Yet, in spite of
all the expert opinion that has been advanced
to the' contrary, the demand for a railway
connection with the other shore will almost
certainly be imperative—some day. Sooner
or later—probably a good deal later—North
Sydney will be a good-sized city in itself, and
for its large population the primitive method
of communication per ferry-boat will be en-
tirely obsolete, and so absurdly inadequate
as not to be worth mentioning. Then the
extension of the railway from King-street to
the only convenient crossing-place at Miller’s
Point would necessitate an enormously ex-
pensive resumption of one of the most ex-
pensive portions of the city—a portion which
would then, probably, be a great deal more
expensive than it is even now. If there is
ever to be a direct railway connection with
North Sydney, it is obvious that the line
should run between George-street and Dar-
ling Harbor, so as to point as straight as
possible for the only suitable crossing-place.
And, as there certainly will be need fox-
such a connection as soon as the population
has so increased as to completely outgrow the
existing means of communication, and as the
population has all the time there is to
increase in, The Bulletin regards this con-
nection as a thing that must come, even if a
host of railway experts held the contrary
view. The question is not one of railway
science, but solely of population, and a rail-
way expert is not necessarily any better an
authority on questions of population than
any other man. Therefore, the railway ex-
pert’s opinion in this matter is of no greater-
value than any one of several dozen or hun-
dred or thousand other opinions.
* * * •
To carry the railway between George-
street and Darling Harbor would involve
engineering difficulties from which the other
line would be free, but as the question of
communication with North Shore will cer-
tainly refuse to be buried, these difficul-
ties will have to be faced in any case—
some day. Perhaps by that time engin-
eering science may have so far advanced
that the difficulties will be much less
formidable than they are now; on the
other hand, it may not. Going on the west
side of George-street would mean the
destruction of much property—most of it the
ancient and mouldy kind of property which
much requires to be destroyed for the good
of the city’s incorporated soul. Also, unfor-
tunately, it would mean a good deal more
expense and more land-resumption swindle ;
on the other hand, the King-street idea almost
certainly means that the heaviest part of the
expense is only postponed ; for how long it
is postponed no one can say. The case for the
King-street route is based partly on a desire
for present economy ; partly on the yearning
of the property-holders about King, Eliza-
beth, Market, Castlereagh and other ad-
jacent streets for the unearned increment ;
partly on the desire of many people on the
line of route to sell out to the Government;
partly on the fact that the site is un-
doubtedly an admirable one at present ;
partly on the other fact that the matter has
been brought to its present advanced stage
only after infinite difficulty, and that to re-
open the whole question would mean another
indefinite postponement. The case for
the other route is based on a desire to
preserve the beautiful park from destruc-
tion ; on the desire of a different set
of property/- holders to sell out to the
Government; on the yearning of people in
the vicinity of Wynyard-square and York-
street after the unearned increment; on the
fact that that would also be an admirable
site ; and on the desire to keep in view that
extension to North Shore which will some
day be essential, and for which the King-
street scheme makes no provision. As nomem-
ber of The B olletin proprietary lives near the
railway, or has any property to be resumed
on either line, and as this paper has some
equally good enemies who will profit which-
ever scheme is adopted, it sums up the
case in an unbiassed spirit, and leaves it
there. If any method could be devised of
doing the extensive resumptions on a
decently honest basis, it would vote straight
for a site somewhere adjacent to Wynyard-
square. But, in the atmosphere of grease
and boodle, and tip and palm-oil, which now
surrounds the whole question, it is difficult
to imagine anyone even looking for an honest
basis.
Re Patents.
Mr. W. J. Spruson, a Sydney patent expert,
thus addresses The Bulletin :
“ Gambler’s ” reply to “ 0.” resurrects some old argu-
ments. It is not unfair that a patentee should pay fees
to reimburse the State the cost of affording protection for
his invention. The best method is to charge a small fee-
say, two or three pounds—on the grant of the patent, and
also periodical “renewal” fees during the term of the
patent. In that way, on the one hand, inventors would
be induced to exercise their ingenuity and to give the
public the advantage of their labors, by the prospect of
obtaining a share in the profitable results of them ;
whilst, on the other, unprofitable and “ dog-in-the-
manger” patents would be weeded out, for patentees do
not pay renewal fees when their patents have not been
worked, or when they have not received any profit from
them. Official records in all countries in which renewal
fees are payable show that only a small proportion of
patents survive their fifth year. Free patents would
involve an enormous cost in'working the Patents Office,
and there would be a tremendous number of speculative
and useless patents applied for. The writer knows three
men in Sydney who would each apply for a dozen patents
monthly if they had no fees to pay,‘and not one of their
inventions would be worth a copper to the community ;
still, the State would be at the expense of providing a
staff to deal with their applications. The worst method
is the N.S.W. one, where patentees are charged a single
application-fee and no renewal fees. Of course, if the
N.S.W. Patent Office published as it should do—the
vast store of valuable information now hidden away in its
manuscript records, it would not show its present annual
surplus of three to four thousand pounds. N.S.W.
patentees are virtually taxed to produce this surplus, and
get little or nothing for it. The primary service to be
rendered by the Patents Office is to give the public the
benefit of the knowledge brought to it by patentees. In
N.S.W. there has been no adequate attempt to do this
since 1884; upwards of 5000 specifications remain unpub-
lished.
* * • _* •
How can a patent be opposed to public policy in
the sense implied by “ Gambler ” ? A patent can only be
held for what did not previously exist—wherefore it takes
nothing from the public ; it does not Impose any obliga-
tion on the public to adopt the thing patented —where-
fore it does not tax them. If the public wish to use an
inventor’s creation, should he not share in the advantage
they reap? The “working” condition in patents is
ineffectual and absurd. It is retained in very few modern
laws, but is yet a hobby on the Continent. * Compliance
with the working condition in European patents is a
costly formality, and benefits the public not one whit.
What is “ working ” ? Working may be nominal or actual.
If nominal, it is a mere unpractical show. What is
actual working? How much must a patentee work
his invention before it can be said that he has
actually worked it? Take a few instances. An engine-
driver invents an improvement in loco, boilers. Should
the law force him to build a locomotive and buy a railway
to run it on ? Or should it let things slide until a rail-
way co. chooses to adopt the invention? If the former,
the condition can never be performed, and, therefore, the
law virtually would say that an engine-driver shall not
have a patent for an improvement in locos. As the rail-
way owner never does the inventing himself, the law
would, therefore, practically decree that patents could
not be held by the inventor of anv railway appliance, for
a patentee cannot work any railway appliance unless he
has a railway to work it on. Take the case of a mill for
rolling arnior-plates. How would a patentee proceed to
work that invention in N.S. Wales? Again, a man in-
vents a torpedo, or breech mechanism for ordnance, or
something in steamship construction. How is it possible
then for him to “work” anyone of these things? The
working must be done by the parties concerned in the
use of the thing, not by the inventor. It is the inventor’s
part to show the way. The public then have the oppor-
tunity to work his invention, or to do without it, as may
best please them. Let us go to the other extreme. A
man invents and patents an improvement in tobacco
pipes. He has one made and uses it himself. Is that a
‘‘working'?” If not, will “Gambler” say how many
pipes he should smoke, or how many persons should
smoke the one pipe in order to effect a sufficient working?
If he says that ten pipes should be made and sold to ten
smokers, then why not ten locomotives to ten rail-
way owners, or ten steamships to ten steamship
owners ? It is not practicable to require more than
nominal working, which, when possible at all, is a farce.
The compulsory-license clause is a very good thing to deal
with the “dog-in-the-manger” patentee. It is justified
by the argument that the ultimate object of granting
patents is to improve the arts, manufactures, and trades
of a country, by inducing persons of an inventive capacity
to exercise their ingenuity and produce novelties with a
view to introducing and developing trades and industries.
* • * *
" Gambler ” complains about “the roundabout rigma-
role of the English Act.” What is he complaining about ?
He does not tell us. When he says he w'ould tax profits of
inventions, he is again proposing something entirely im-
practicable. How can such profits be ascertained ? They
may be approximately reckoned in a very small percentage
of cases where the invention is a definite and separate
entity. But very few patents are of that kind. A large
proportion of patents in commercial use come to be
adopted because they make the article to which they are
applied more marketable, but no definite part of the
manufacturer’s profit can be called royalty. The State
had better let the exploitation of patents alone, for
patents are not a class of property which may be advan-
tageously nationalised. State interference in the direc-
tion of resuming patents would mean log-rolling extra-
ordinary. “ Gambler ” should confine himself to concrete
cases for a basis for his arguments. When “ some costless
force” of which he dreams is invented, it will
be time enough to legislate in view' of such an
extraordinary circumstance. But as we are yet some
distance from the Millennium, it is just as well to
postpone troubling ourselves about the details of such
legislation.' The mere production of motion seems an all-
embracing idea, but it really covers only a very small area
of the inventor’s and laborer’s fields of work, which are
more particularly occupied with the transformation and
utilisation of energy and the production of effects not re-
quiring the utilisation of motion. Even if we had a cost-
less method of producing- power, the occupations of only
a fraction of the world’s laborers would be affected, and
for most, if not all of these, new avenues w-ould be opened
up under the new conditions so created.
I cannot attempt to deal at length here with the great
economical problem which the use of machinery has
opened up. My own impression is that if the past
multitude of inventors and patentees had never existed,
and the Stone Age still survived, there would be just as
many discontents and unemployed amongst us as
we have to-day, if not more than we have, whilst we
would have none of the advantages and conveniences, all
flowing from inventive genius, which make decent and
civilised life possible. A diatribe against inventions is
logically applicable to the man who contrived the pick,
the spade, the knife, the hammer, the chisel, and the
needle; but this aspect of the matter is usually overlooked
by the narrow-brained economists who do not realise that
wants are created by the opportunities to fill them.
There are no wants just now for the things which
the next generation will invent, but when those
things are invented, the world will want them,
and men will be employed in supplying the new demand,
who otherwise might walk about idle. “Gambler”
instances the linotype, and quotes the immediate result
of the introduction of that wonderful machine, presum-
ably as a reason for its suppression. If he is logical hie
should also denounce the type-founding machine, the
paper-making machine, and the cylinder press. We must
look further afield than “ Gambler ” does to discover what
is the ultimate result of labor-saving devices. Surely he
will not attempt to tell us that more labor would be em-
ployed to-day if the three machines I have mentioned did
not exist. How many men were employed in the printing
trade when types were cut in wood and impressions pulled
in a lever press ? Have printers lost work by the intro-
duct ion of labor-saving devices? On the contrary, there
are now a thousand men employed in that trade for every
one there would be if we had only the appliances of three
hundred years ago. Every day we hear that if railway
freights could be cheapened, new industries might be
founded in the interior and labor employed which is now
idle. Would the cheapening of overland transit then
decrease the demand for labor? Lastly, what does
“ Gambler ” mean by a “rational system of patents ” ? I
do not say that we have in N.S. Wales even a moderately
good patent system, but “ Gambler ” seems to have a
grievance also against the English system. If it is as well
founded as the grievances he expresses, it is probably npt
very serious.—W. J. Spruson.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Land Tax Muddle.
The decision given by N.S.W. Supreme Court
in the recent Land-tax action puis the God-
fearing Ministry in several kinds of a tight
place. The law, as laid down by Judge Man-
ning, is that the Laud-tax Act requires the
assessment-books of the province to he com-
pleted before any money can be demanded from
the tax-payer ; and that, as they have not been
completed and are not likely to be for a long
while, no tax is due, or is likely to come due
until some very remote date. As a natural
corollary, the .£150,000 or so already collected
has been collected illegally, and unless Judge
Manning’s decision can be overruled, which is
eminently improbable, the only resource is to get
theassessment-books completed in great haste, or
to pass an amending Act with all possible speed.
Failing that, it will not only be practically im-
possible to collect any more tax, but the people
who have already paid will raise a howl for a
refund, which bowl will be practically irresist-
ible. There should be very little difficulty in
passing the amending Act, but meanwhile the
deadlock is a beautiful advertisement for tlie
aggregate intelligence of the Ministry. There
are four lawyers in it, yet not one of them
dropped to this ghastly mistake when the
measure was under consideration. The error
was so conspicuous that quite a number of
lawyers and laymen and various papers, in-
cluding The Bulletin, pointed it out over six
months ago, and it was also so conspicuous that
the Equity Court found it at the very first
attempt. Yet apparently all the legal talent
in the Ministry couldn’t see the flaw even when
it was pointed out, or else the Ministry in
general lacked the foresight to set about
remedying it before the deadlock took place.
This episode should make the tax-commis-
sioners feel a ’ittle better. A few weeks ago
they were heavily sat upon by the Attorney-
General for employing outside legal talent
while he was available, and considering where
the outside talent led them in the matter of
the 'income-tax arrears, all they could do was
to take the remarks that were made as peace-
fully as possible. Now, considering the much
worse hole which the Attorney-General and
the other legal members of the Ministry have
got into without any outside assistance, the
Commissioners probably feel that things are
about square. The present situation is some-
thing unique in the way of blank stupidity.
Low Prices, Depression, and Borrowing.
Premier Turner, in one of his London
speeches, ascribed the present depression in
Australia largely to borrowing. Whereupon his
brother-Premier, Reid, of N.S.W., took an
early opportunity to contradict him, and
alleged that it wasn’t borrowing, but “ the fall


of 25 per cent, in the value of products ”
within the last few years which caused all the
trouble. G. H. Reid, whose political economy
js weak, and his finance awful, and his arith-
metic beneath derision, apparently didn’t see
that he was saying in other words the same
thing as Turner said, while alleging that the
thing which Turner said was incorrect. There
has been, roughly speaking, a fall of 25 to 50
per cent, within the last quarter century in the
values of the goods produced by Australia, and
there has been, through various causes, a very
similar fall in the values of the goods which
Australia doesn’t produce, but buys from other
people. If this country didn’t owe any money
to any foreign creditor the fall would matter
nothing, or next to nothing. Wages, rent,
and interest would probably be 25 per cent,
less on a rough average, but as the reduced
sum' of money would buy the same amount
of goods as the larger sum did before
the reduction, nobody would be any the
poorer. The exported goods which formerly
fetched, say, 10 millions would now fetch only
7\ millions, and the imported goods which
formerly cost 10 millions, would only cost 7£
millions, and barring some temporary disloca-
tion of trade no one would be any the worse.
The borrowing curse, however, puts things in
quite a different aspect. When Australia sends
to England £30,000,000 worth of goods per
annum, and gets back £15,000,000, the differ-
ence goes to pay interest at the rate of £15,000,000
per annum on public and private borrowings.
A drop of 25 per cent, all round in values
means that the exports only fetch £22,500,000,
while the cost of the imports goes down to
£11,250,000, consequently, instead of there
being 15 millions left to cover interest there is
only 11£ millions. This means that Australia
must pay up the other 3f millions, which is
dead loss created by the decline in prices.
The rate of interest on public borrowings
has also gone down since 1870 or ’75, but
as the loans floated then were at a fixed
rate of interest for a fixed number of years
this country as yet has gained no benefit worth
speaking of from the fall. Reid’s statement
that the “ depression ” arises through the fall
in prices is true; the other man’s state-
ment that it arises through borrowing is equally
true, for the fall in prices would have made no
real difference but for the borrowing. Reid,
being himself a persistent borrower, didn’t like
the way Turner put it, but though lie put it
differently he said exactly the same thing.

The Theory of Mine Accidents.
Judge Murray has been appointed a Commis-
sion to inquire into and report upon the whole
subject of the frequency of accidents in Broken
Hill mines and open cuts ; and he has already
begun his work. He is to ascertain what class
of operations, if any, are particularly unsafe ;
wherein the present regulations fail ; what
regulations, if any, ought to be substituted or
added ; whether more inspectors are required ;
and to travel into various other byways.
About the most feasible-looking theory sub-
mitted so far to account for the frequency of
accidents is the simple one that, all round, the
mines are working at too high a pressure,
Mining could be made as free from danger as
ordinary pick-and-shovel work—if you could
get a mine rich enough to stand it, and a pro-
prietary philanthropic enough to allow it.
Bub in order to make bigger profits
out of rich ore, and to bring poor
ores within the pay - limit, operations
are pushed further and further in the
direction of danger. Deep shot-holes are
put down instead of shallow ones ; wide stopes
are exposed instead of narrow ones ; sides of
open-cut quarries are undermined so that the
overburden will fall away instead of having to
be blasted ; ends of long steel-wire winding-
ropes are changed while the bight hangs down
the shaft, supported on timber, instead of the
whole thing being hauled out of the shaft;
“ bottoms ” of holes from which the explosive
is supposed to have blown out are used as the
beginnings of new holes ; and a thousand other
labor-saving devices are resorted to. Theoreti-
cally, there is hardly such a thing as a non-pre-
ventible accident. Ask a mine expert—manager
or workman, it doesn’t matter which—whether
a given operation is safe, and he will probably
reply, “Yes, from a mining point of view.”
Which means that it really isn’t safe, but
that for the sake or in the hope of getting
profits the co. allows it, and for the sake of
getting or keeping in work the sub-
mits to it. It is a matter of extreme diffi-
culty to say where what may be called
legitimate risk ends, and the illegitimate
begins: no Commission, whatever valuable
light it may be able to shed upon more or less
local details, can be expected to satisfactorily
dispose of this larger problem. The miner may
claim that as he has to accept big and still
bigger risks he ought to be paid higher wages ;
but the owner has the reply that if the big risks
are not taken at the ordinary wage the ore
will not pay to handle at all. His reply has
been so convincing that at length practi-
cally all risks are, by the inevitable levelling
process, now taken without special payment.
At one time working miners sought to get some
few special risks specially compensated—work-
ing in wet shafts, for instance. But even this
provision is now generally disregarded on most
mining fields. If the miner did, as a rule, get
compensation for extra or special risks, he
might be reasonably expected to make special
provision against accident, fatal or otherwise
but as he doesn’t get special compensation he
doesn’t, as a rule, make special provision.
In fact, following what seems to be a
pretty general rule, the riskier his work
the more prodigal he is of his pence. And
because profits are largely dependent upon
risks, and because the miner is not generally
compensated for the special risks of the calling,
it is arguable that mine-owners should be re-
quired to at least co-operate in maintaining a
fund for the relief of those who suffer from the
special risks of the industry. There undoubt-
edly are many mines which could not be worked
were not large risks accepted. As it is, these
mines do not return enough or more than enough
to pay the ordinary wages. But, on the other
hand, there are mines which, while still paying
only the same rate of wages, might be profitably
worked with far less risk ; and, seeing that the
miners swell the profits by accepting risks
without extra payment (unwritten industrial
laws preventing any differentiation), it doesn’t
seem utterly outrageous that some of these
extra profits should be taken to help provide
something like an equivalent in cash to pay the
price in flesh and blood which the industry’s
special risks demand.
Put It Down to the Drink.
Butler was duly butchered last week, in
Sydney, by the noseless minion of the law, and
he died a penitent member of the Wesleyan
persuasion, with the hope of a blessed resurrec-
tion in his soul. According to his ghostly com-
forter, the Rev. George Lane, he left an
affecting message behind him, in which he
“ put it all down to the drink ” :
The next time you see your Sunday-school children, Mr.
Lane, tell them from me that I, too, was once a Sunday-
school scholar in a little English village, and drink has
brought me to this. ,
This is the voice of warning which rises from
the grave of the Glenbrook murderer, and, on
the strength of it, Rev. Mr. Lane invites
everybody, with renewed fervor, to swear
off the beer when it is brown and be a new
man. Still, the voice isn’t quite so convincing
as it sounds. Very few men, looking back on a
moderately long life, can tell exactly what it
was which made them become murderers, or
accountants, or burglars, or preachers, or ham-
shop keepers, or teetotallers, or Buddhists, or
hangmen, or anything else ; a man generally
becomes what he is through countlesa causes,
direct and remote, working together, many of
which causes he is wholly unaware of. Not
one man in 1000 can say honestly that his whole
life turned on any one cause. Butler possibly
thought drink was the reason of his downfall
because he was attended by a parson who held
forth to him on the horrors of alcohol ; if he
had been attended in his last hours by a wine-
and-spirit merchant he would quite as pro-
bably have ascribed his downfall to the Sunday-
school. Simply because all criminals are
attended in their last hours by parsons,
and none of them by wine-and-spirit merchants,
the Drink doesn’t get a fair show in their
dying speeches; if the condemned were
habitually visited and comforted by publicans
probably a large proportion of them would die
stating that they had gone down-hill through
the gloom and Calvinism and sulphur and
brimstone of their early Christian teaching, and
had been kept away from the right path by the
dreariness of pastors in general. Moreover, it
was easier and pleasanter for Butler to ascribe
his crimes to drink than to mere greed and cal-
lousness and indisposition to honest labor;
every man naturally passes on the blame, and
Drink is as handy a thing to pass it on to as
anything else. Butler, so far as all the evi-
dence goes, was distinctly a sober criminal.
The drunken criminal does his crime suddenly
while he is.drunk. Butler beguiled his vic-
tims away with the ingenuity of a clear, cool
head, and shot them with a steady hand
—not a hand that wobbled through the
fumes of alcohol. A drinky murderer
would have muddled the elaborate plans
which Butler had to carry out. Almost all
great and skilful criminals are strictly sober
men, because it needs a much clearer head to
compose an elaborate crime than to compose a
sermon. Peace, Wainwright, the poisoner
Bertrand, and Deeming are a few casual
examples ; so are Matthias Larkin and the
other pious Melbourne swindlers who ran the
boom ; so is almost every other reasonably suc-
cessful criminal. The theory that it was Drink
which made Butler the hardy ruffian he
was, which made his hand steady and
his eye reliable so that he potted his
victims with absolute accuracy every time,
is wild absurdity, according to all parsonical
theories as to the effect of liquor. Butler’s
story is simply this : he was a Sunday-school
scholar, a sailor, a soldier, a drinker, a miner,
and many other things, and either the Sunday-
school or t he drink, or the sailoring, or heredity,
or something else led him to become Lee
Weller’s murderer. But the Rev. George
Lane, as a good teetotaller, suggested to him
that it was the Drink, or Butler thought it
would fit the mental bias of his ghostly com-
forter to say it was the Drink, and in his last
attempt to shunt the responsibility on to some-
one else he told the Rev. George Lane it was
the Drink 5 so it stands at that.
A Kerristian Exemplar.
A Melb. newspaper discussion on “The Sign
of the Cross ” was opened last week by one
Rev. E. Isaac, president of the local Baptist
Union. The Rev. Isaac merely referred to the
Firm’s show as something which the writer
wouldn’t go to see, and his epistle was, for the
most part, a hardshell Baptist advt. of parsonic
self-righteousness and conceit. In the beautiful
words of this holy man :
The question for the Christian minister, and, indeed,
all who profess and call themselves Christians, is not can
I ko to the theatre and receive no harm, but will others
who are perhaps more prone to temptation than I follow
my example and escape unscathed? Will not the sanction
and approval which my presence gives act as a con-
venient opiate to the consciences of some who are anxious
to justify theatre-going from a Christian standpoint? It
seems to me that if I attend the theatre at all I throw the
weight of my example in the scale of theatre-going
generally.
A question for all who don’t profess and call
themselves conscientious opponentsof William-
son and Musorove, penny nap, intoxicating
liquors, dancing, Sunday excursions, and horse-
races is—what proportion of the civilised world
objects to theatres on principle ? The number
of redeemed mortals who shrink from the
thought of setting foot in a play-house can only
be guessed at through church-going statistics.
Religion of the Baptistioal and Wesleyanic
order is represented by “ regular attendants at
Divine Worship,” and these—according to the
shepherd’s own lamentations—are a small per-
centage of the population, growing smaller
every year. The grand total of regular theatre-
goers, occasional visitors, and adults who would
go there sometimes if they were not in a chronic
state of hard-uppislmess, is probably 50 times
greater than the snuffling minority which
suffers from a conscience on the subject. A
portion of the public stops religiously
away from wliat is called frivolous en-
tertainment at the theatres. “ The Sign of
the Cross ” was specially designed to catch
the “ serious-minded ” crowd—but probably
not more than two per cent, will absolutely
refuse to sample any kind of stage-play what-
soevei*. In short, Rev. Isaac, living and
preaching in a small world, magnifies his own
importance to a ridiculous degree, after the
manner of petty local potentates in every line
of business. As for his pride in his particular
ability to walk unharmed where other angels
might fear to tread, it is such a hideous illu-
stration of Baptist faith that The Bulletin
suspects some artful theatrical manager (other
than the Firm) of having secretly incited Isaac
to give himself away. Frank Thornton or
Bland Holt incited him by circuitous means
to write his delectable letter, in order that the
community at large might begin to turn against
“ The Sign of the Cross ” and despise the
Early Christianity which . has resulted in
Baptist Unions and the Nonconformist con-
science,
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent, “J,”
argues quite logically re the cyanide patent
question, but, unfortunately, when the pre-
mises are unsound the more logical the argu-
ment based thereon the more false the
conclusion. If the cyanide people were seeking
simply to correct a “slip in their specifica-
tions” “ J’s ” contention would he unanswer-
able, as it was to enable patentees to do tin's
that the Act was passed. But the cyanide
people did not make any “slip ” —they calmly
claimedgwhat they didnof invent—what has been
known for 30 years, viz., the mode of extrac-
tion of gold from powdered ore bj 7 any solu-
tion containing cyanogen. This claim the High
Court of Appeal in England held to be bad
on the ground I have Btated, and the object of
the proposed amendment is to substitute for
the present invalid, drag-net claim a definite
one for a certain specified solution. Should
this amendment be permitted, in lieu of a
patent which has been held by the highest court
of law in the Empire to be bad, the cyanide
people would be granted what is practically a
new patent, affecting the treatment of the
whole of our low-grade ores. Under these cir-
cumstances the N.S.W. Minister for Mines is
doing nothing more than his duty in instruct-
ing the law officers of the Crown to oppose
the application.—Yours, Arthur Griffith,
Sydney, 18th July.
Premier Seddon comes out of the Jubilee, so
far as the cabled information shows, about the
worst of all the seven Premiers. They all
grabbed hungrily and shamelessly at whatever
title was going, and in this one matter Reid
showed to the worst advantage as the man who
had declared most distinctly that lie would
never take a gewgaw under any possible cir-
cumstances whatever, while Kingston and Sed-
don, as two other avowed Democrats, were very
close up. Turner, who never advertised him-
self as a Democrat or a man who scorned gauds
with a lofty scorn, took his decoration with a
better grace, and the other three were avowed
Tories, to whom titles came as a matter
of course. Also, the three Tory Premiers
didn’t lose caste when they drivelled about
Imperial Federation, because everyone knew
they were built that way and they never made
any secret of their Imperialistic leanings. As
regards the four to whom the democratic party
pinned its faith, Reid, Turner, and Kingston
appear to have given neither grovel nor rash
promises nor professions of abject loyalty in
return for their gauds, and Seddon was the
only one who showed signs of a tendency
to wobble after strange gods, and to truckle
to Joseph Chamberlain and the Imperial
Federation party generally. Possibly the cable
did him an injustice, but he certainly looks
like a man who lost his head badly, and grovelled
foolishly ; and the Maoriland National Ass has
already begun to speak kindly of him, which is
valuable corroborative evidence. Turner would
have come through the business more respect-
ably than anyone else, but for the pathetic tale
of how he shaved off his quaint, crescent-
shaped billygoat in order to mash or otherwise
impress Queen Victoria, and took drilling-
exercise on board ship, and “ waved his hand ”
in the Jubilee procession. The story of that
stout, elderly, spectacled solicitor incessantly
waving his hand to the Empire in general will
stick to Turner for a long, long time.
Melb. Argus says: “ The singular delusion is
often encountered that Freetraders cannot pos-
sibly regard with favor manufacturing growth.
No allegation can be more preposterous. Free-
traders fully recognise that a multiplicity of
occupations is essential in a well-organised com-
munity, and that the aspiration after a strong
manufacturing element in the national economy
is in every way laudable.” The Freetrader in
a new country has no desire to crush native in-
dustries, it is comforting to hear. He simply
dumps mountainous masses of imported mer-
chandise in the path of the local manufac-
turers, and says : “If you can step across those
obstacles without assistance, bless you, my
children ! If you can’t, you must go upon the
land and earn profits for the importer by the
sweat of your brows.”
Canterbury Times (M.L.): —
In Her Majesty’s Privy Council, as now constituted,
there may be discerned the eerm of that Imperial Council
which Federationists see to be the necessary concomitant
of a closer and more formal union between England and
her colonies. The Premiers of Canada, of Cape Colony,
and of the seven colonies of Australasia have been called
to the councils of Her Majesty, and have thus been
granted a position of honor and responsibility in the
affairs of the Empire. The Premiers, and the colonies
whom their elevation is meant to honor, have every
reason to feel highly gratified that the Queen’s advisers
have chosen this method of recognition, rather th;in the
bestowal of meaningless titles and tinsel decorations,
which confer no honor, typify no dignity, and are by the
bulk of colonists regarded as worthy only of ridicule! As
Privy Councillors, sworn to advise and assist the
Sovereign, &c., &c.
This is probably as good a fashion of explaining
away Dick Seddon’s title as any other. But
the seven Premiers haven’t been “ called to the
councils of Her Majesty,” for the Privy Council
doesn’t give any good advice to Her Majesty
nowadays. Despite their being “ sworn ” they
don’t either advise or assist the Sovereign.
Their title is as meaningless and as tinselly as
a*K.C.M.G-ship, or any other slab of orna-
mental woe. It doesn’t typify any dignity now,
though it once did. There is no “ responsi-
bility ” about the position, and its honor is a
matter of opinion ; also there is no “ germ ” of
any Imperial Council, or any imported microbe
whatsoever about the business, for the F.C. is
an old institution that is dying out, not a new
one that is growing and developing. The title
of Right Hon. is as strictly an ornamental
honorary cheese-plate hung on the owner’s
stomach as any other gewgaw, and no amount
of explanation can get away from the fact that
all the seven Premiers grabbed unanimously for
all the meaningless title that was going. And
may the Lord have mercy upon their political
souls.
Minister Gould, speaking for Minister Car-
ruthers in N.S.W, Parliament on dummy-
informers, said that it is the practice of the
Lands Department, when a man discloses that
he has played the dummy, to forward his state-
ments to the Crown Law officers, with a view
of prosecuting him for perjury if those officers
so advise. Later on the Minister said he should
not feel inclined to shield self-confessed per-
jurers. The informer is the ne plus infra of
civilisation. In barbarism he is chopped up if
his one-time friends catch him, and sometimes
he fares as badly among the new friends he
tries to serve. A Minister is on pretty safe
ground, therefore, when he girds scornfully at
the pariah. Nine out of ten hearers will
applaud. The tenth may, however, ask
why, if the Law shield informers of
one class, it should not shield those of
another ? If a man who has done his share
of a murder or robbery turns Queen’s evidence,
the Law shields him. If he has conspired
under oath with others to perpetrate a felony
of the ordinary brand, and he chooses to give
his associates away, the Law not only protects
him, but often actually rewards him. Why,
then, is this minatory Ministerial message
issued against the land-dummying informer?
It is well-known that the foul-smelling pool of
land-dummying has been lately stirred, and
that, to the surprise of many, pseudo-selectors,
north, south, and west, have shown a willing-
ness to “ give away ” their squatter bosses. Is
the Minister’s message, then, a menace ? Any-
way, these potential witnesses are admonished
thereby that they may be prosecuted for their
initial perjury.

In the N.S.W. Mines Dept.’s annual report
there is every year included an elaborate state-
ment of the number of men killed in the mines
of the province. Upon these figures are based
tables which purport to show the percentage of
men killed and injured : and these figures again
are accepted universally for purposes of com-
parison with the statistics of other industries.
The fatal accidents have hitherto been thought
to be pretty correctly stated, though the few
people on the inside have known that when a
miner dies some time after the occurrence of
his accident, the circumstance is often allowed
to pass officially unnoted. But even this sec-
tion of the statistics is now more seriously dis-
credited. Replying in the Assembly the other
night to a questioner, the Minister for Mines,
said that there had been eight fatal accidents
in B.H. mines since Sept., 1896 ; whereas local
authorities insist that there are 14 more or less
mangled corpses to be accounted for in that
district. The Minister continued that there had
been 27 other accidents in the same period ;
whereas the Miners’ Association, which includes
about half the total number of workmen,
declares that its books alone show that in the
period referred to 80 men have received acci-
dent pay at Broken Hill, and 13 more at the
neighboring Tarrawingee. Officers of this body
further assert that'not above one-half of the
accidents have ever been officially listed. In
the metalliferous mines of the United Kingdom
33,200 persons are employed ; there were 37
fatal accidents last year, involving 40 deaths.
The proportion of fatalities to persons employed
there was thus one in 830. In the open-cuts
and quarries of the U.K. the proportion was
124 in 112,829 (one in 910) ; in the collieries,
notwithstanding some big disasters during the
year, it was 1025 in 692,684 (one in 686). In
the Broken Hill district for the last eight
months alone the proportion has been one
in 410. The figures prove two unpleasant
things—one that the proportion of accidents at
Broken Hill is excessive ; the other the unre-
liability of the official figures of the N.S.W.
Mines Department.
“ Le Juste Milieu ” addresses The Bulletin
re a Police Court incident lately referred to in
these columns:—
At Melb. Police Court a man was sentenced to three
months for theft, or fraud of some kind, and Panton,
P.M. ,took upon himself to endorse the prisoner’s business
references to that effect— -i.e., the magistrate cancelled
his certificates of character up to date. Was Mr. Panton’s
act an injustice to a dishonest man, or a performance of
duty to the commercial community? The spoiled refer
ences were calculated to land the bearer in a billet where
a character for trustworthiness would be essential to his
engagement; therefore, they were now false in the light
of his proved dishone-ty. The magistrate had a right to
protect society from imposition. On the other hand, it may-
be said that the prisoner was entitled to the benefit of
any- good character he had acquired previous to convic-
tion. But was he entitled to capture, on the strength of
his bygone trustworthiness, real or supposed, a billet
which other men, who have never gone wrong at all, are
hungrily seeking ? There are the unemployed, as well as
the employers, to be considered in a case like this. Where
honesty is asked for, unsullied honesty should take pre-
cedence of the tarnished article. In the competition for
precedence among clerks in distress, all anxious to enter
the office that offers a means of living, sympathy
for the poor devil who has brought himself to grief
should surely be set aside in favor of sympathy for
the poor devils whose trouble is undeserved. And this
apart from the question as it affects employers. Then
have a right to get what they' consider the most desirable
clerk, according to the references submitted, therefore
the reference which suggests unimpeachable honesty in
him who has subsequently been impeached and sent to
gaol, is a fraud upon the employer, as well as an injustice
to the rival candidates. Yet, how is a clerk, stripped of
his references, to rise again from the dust if, as seems
probable, his former employers refuse to repeat their good
opinion of him in writing? Must he starve? Panton,
P.M., might reply that better men are in dire straits,
and their erring brother must go for just what he is
worth in the clerkly labor-market.
8 By making all the seven Premiers Privy-
Councillors, Chamberlain simply divided them
into two classes—the weak men who were pro-
foundly grateful because they got the same title.-
as the strong men, and the strong men who were
mad because they only got the same titles as th*
weak men.
