Rotunda Prof. Norton B. Smith.
Colonel Olcott, one of the boss apostles of
Theosophy, dropped in casually upon Sydney last
week, and lectured very affably on his faith. The
Colonel revealed himself as a very stout gentleman
with the most benevolent countenance on record,
a smooth, waxen skin, a creamy-white beard
streaming like a cataract of beneficence down his
vasty chest, a pair of dreamy blue eyes, and a
gentle voice. He is the sort of man that any lost
dog would go home with at first sight. As an ex-
pounder of the large, soft, overwhelming creed of
the Theosophists, the Colonel is a reasonable suc-
cess. There is nothing bony or angular about
that creed. It falls softly on the hearer like 2ewt.
of boiled sago pudding, and adapts itself to cir-
cumstances. It is so comprehensive that almost
any kind of religionist can get into it without
much conversion, and it is generally understood to
have come from Asia, where all the gentlemanly
creeds do come from. Anyhow, no creed that
came from any other place ever amounted to any-
thing, and no prophet, from Moses to Malachi,
Micah and Mahomet, who wasn’t an Asiatic
prophet, ever prophesied worth tuppence.
All the same, the Colonel’s religion hasn’t
come to these parts to stay. It is too
mild, and soft, and benevolent to catch on per-
manently. The Anglo-Saxon likes a creed that
bites all the way down ; something that he can
procesh about now and then, and throw bricks
about, and over which he can do some tall shoot-
ing at the heathen in his blindness. Also he
wants good, solid, well-defined damnation in it.
A creed which is mainly a fat, mild, brotherly
muddling with the Infinite isn’t in his line.
* • • •
The present writer has heard a good many
Theosophists on the platform, and is personally
acquainted with one—a long-haired dreamer, who
always has eight dogs walking behind him (it is
only a man with a kindly soul who is ever followed
by eight dogs, and when eight dogs go after a man
it generally means that he is too kind to wholly
damn anyone, and then he has to take to Theoso-
phy or go without a religion). And, despite all
these lecturers, and much Theosophical literature,
and the eight dogs, his attempts to grasp the big,
undefmable faith with Karma in it have been as
ineffectual as an attempt to flatten out soup with
a hammer. But he likes the Colonel all the same,
and regards him as a pleasant, honest, kindly
fentleman, with both hands full of Chaos and
nfinity and Soul, and things like that, and, at his
worst, he is a great deal more consoling than most
preachers that one meets. And when he gets on
a platform and dreams affably about Buddha and
various other bygone prophets, and about the old
faiths which sprawl loosely among the old Asiatic
temples with the snake in the basement, he is
very agreeable company for an idle hour, and
quite as good as being asleep.
** * *
On Saturday night “My Friend from
India ” is to be produced at Her Majesty’s, with
Alma Stanley, Paulton and George Lauri in the
cast. The “ Friend,” which did a rather successful
season in Melbourne, is on for one week only, as
another new co. is due on the 31st With the “ Gay
Parisienne.” In the meantime the “ Night Out ”
is getting along to excellent business, and
Mathieu’s four daughters make a noble effort
every evening to go to bed publicly on the stage,
till the eruption of the inebriated architect breaks
in on their innocent repose.
* * *
Maggie Moore has decided to cut short
“ Mrs. Quinn’s Twins,” after a two weeks’season,
and a new comedy-drama with the gruesome title
of “ The Pauper ” is to be staged at Sydney Royal
on Saturday. This paper parts with the twins in
a rather regretful spirit. They were two bright
scraps of draggled humanity, and Mrs. Quinn pur-
sued them among some very interesting scenery;
moreover, the play, despite its thinness, was a
pleasant little thing with a nice, large dog in it,
and at a more prosperous time should have paid
the gas-bill and left a decent margin over. But
another hard frost appears to be settling on
theatrical matters in Sydney, and the necessity for
a continual hustle probably makes the untimely
sacrifice of the twins unavoidable.
** * *
Signs have lately been accumulating that
the Farley co. will not be of much longer duration
at Sydney Opera House, and now its demise is
announced for Friday night. Meanwhile, the
rather attenuated audiences don’t grow any
larger. The management has certainly given
very good value for the very moderate amount
charged at the door, but there appears to be
no great demand for old-fashioned operas and the
melodies of bygone days. So good old Mephis-
topheles lifts up his bass note of denunciation in
the presence of many empty seats, and Jules
Simonsen is damned very unobtrusively. There
are rumors about a new management trying
its fortunes in the luckless cavern above the
public-house in - Y ork-street.
** * *
The Flying Jordans give their last per-
formance at the Lyceum to-night, and then
that theatre relapses into darkness. The Cri-
terion also continues closed, and the luckless Elsie
Adair Co. has scattered. An effort is being made
to raise a benefit to Miss Adair, who deserves one,
if only for the bright smile with which she faced a
vast number of difficulties, and the dazzling set of
teeth which she revealed in the presence of very
hard circumstances. Unluokily, she wasn’t long
enough in Australia to become generally known,
and Sydney has very little to donate just now
except its land-tax paper.
** * *
The three Hayters, who created a good
Impression in Melbourne, as mentioned in sundry
back numbers of this paper, join the Tivoli crowd
on Saturday, the “loose-legged comedian” moving
on to make room for them. Jimmy MacMahon’s
reliable cinematographe is alive and prospering at
the old address, and the Polytechnic show in the
same line of business appears to be doing well in.
King-street. Which ends the chronicle of a
rather barren week. A week when the only
novelty in the show line is a benevolent colonel
from ’Murka lecturing on a hazy Buddha who died
25 centuries ago is not exactly one of dazzling
thrill.
** * *
The return of “ Charley’s Aunt ” caused a
Senuine rush to Melb. Bijou on Saturday, for the
rst time since long, long ago. Seeing that the
first Australian appearance of the same spurious
relative, in the same theatre, didn’t excite as
much rush as was expected at tne time, the crowd
last Saturday may be largely attributed to popular
prices. “ Charley’s Aunt ”is now on offer at an
alarming sacrifice, and the winter sale will
probably continue for several weeks. The lively
demand for this durable article, which has achieved
a longer run than any other comedy of French or
British manufacture, reminds The Bulletin that
it was made in Germany. Frank Thornton is
less fleshy and more unmanageable than the Aunt
of three or four years back, and the performance
altogether seems madder than ever. The company
that kept the old joke going in Sydney recently has
taken to itself a new Kitty, in place of Miss Sea-
broke (who seems to have retired for the purpose
of issuing writs), but all the other people are as
before. Also, the aforesaid change in the cast
makes no difference to the general effect. There
is a fresh young air of juvenility about Miss Bessie
Rignold, Herbert Terry, Miss Poole, and the
damsel whose deceased father left her all the
money he had won from Thornton at cards—and
juvenility is the principal qualification expected of
them in this case. The cheerful colonel and the
bona-fide aunt from Brazil are distinctly valuable
items, and even old Spettigue has the negative
virtue of seeming to feel some of the pain that he
inflicts upon his fellow man.
** * *
“ The Sign of the Cross ” is in the third
week of an unquestionable triumph, &c., at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, and the management truly
states that the entertainment “ makes you think !
think ! think ! ” A Bible-preacher or two, and
several other equally absurd persons, have been
writing what they think, think, think to the ac-
commodating daily press, and the thoughtful
advt. was doubtless a good thing for the Firm’s
handsome show up to a certain point. But at time
of writing it seemed rather advisable for the
correspondence to cease. An uncalled-for
individual had chipped in with the sugges-
tion that symbols of the Cross were unknown
to Early Christianity, and the uncalled-for
testifier seems in accordance with facts as far as
they can be arrived at. When the “Sign of the
Cross ” came into the religion, early Christianity
went bung, arid Mahomet presently rose to protest
against any further embellishments of the original
faith. Wilson Barrett, of course, took his Cross
situations from “Faust,” where Valentine is in
the habit of stiffening the Devil by showing him
a sword-handle. No matter. There will be another
matinee massacre of the saints on Saturday.
** * *
Howard Vernon persists in carrying on a
spirited comic opera season at the Alexandra,
although it is clear to every kind of understand-
ing that the enterprise can't prosper. The Bul-
letin would say some kind things of “Madame
An got ” and the new tenor, J. E. Shepherd, if
this journal’s good word could possibly affect the
decrees of Fate. It couldn’t, however. Argument
with Fate is a mere waste of time.
*# * *
The last of Bland Holt’s dramas has
grown more acceptable on acquaintance, as the
result of increased exertions by the company and
an impressive improvement in the behavior of
the hydraulic lift. After eight solid months of
uninterrupted struggle between Walter Baker
and the powers of darkness, during which period
the hero has disproved half-a-dozen false accusa-
tions of undue familiarity with the spinster who
went astray, it is difficult for “ The Cotton King ”
to strike the weary newspaper chronicler in
a fresh place. Everything that happens
in this drama is an attenuated reminiscence of
some previous atrocity, as it were ; but for the
very occasional visitor “ The Cotton King ” is
possibly a thrifty novelty. Anyway, Melbourne
Royal will be crowded to-night (Thursday) and
to-morrow, when Bland Holt’s record reign must
reluctantly terminate.
** * *
Allan and Co.’s experiment with Wednes-
day afternoon Chamber Music Concerts, at Melb.
Athenaeum Hall, promises to lead to a perennial
entertainment on the lines of “Monday Pops,”
which have long been a feature of the London
season. The Allan concerts (directed by clever
little Benno Scherek) are just a little too long and
classical for philistinic taste; an instrumental
trio, even by the tuneful Schubert, contains
rather too much padding for the concert world in
general, and it seems a pity that the works of
the great masters can’t be edited by a more
vulgar-minded musician, such as Arthur Sullivan.
But these programmes, light in their way r are
likely to grow upon people who have been accus-
tomed to taking their music in smaller doses. The
good stuff is appreciated when it gets good treat-
ment. Scherek, George Weston (violin) and
Hattenbach with his ’cello, are the principal
artists engaged on instrumental piecework, and a
couple of vocalists, selected from Maggie Sterling,
Gladstone Wright, James Wade, Madame Wieder-
mann, Miss May Brookes, and others, contribute
songs at each concert. But why are “ high-class ”
songs so very unexciting ?
** * *
On Saturday the Flying Jordans, together
with the lovely vision who trips upon a cobweb,
and the rest of the show from Sydney will appear
at Melbourne Royal.
* * *
Mark Hambourg and his younger brother
Jakoff, who plays a violin almost as effectively as
Mark punishes a piano, will appear at Melb.
Town Hall on Thursday (22nd), and for a few
nights thereafter. John Lemmone is managing
artist of the Hambourg Concerts, and the show
promises to boom.
** * *
The Haytors and Phil Newbury are retir-
ing from the Opera House programme forthwith,
but the Selbinis will continue their performance of
impossible deeds upon various bicycles, and bits
of bicycle, until further notice. Carl Hertz is
dividing his time this week between Geelong and
the Athenaeum Hall, where he will take two sor-
rowful farewells of Melbourne on Saturday. After
which the wizard is to tour in a sou’-westerly
direction and reach Perth sometime before the
middle of August. Professor Norton B. Smith
appears to have settled down to a successful horsey
season at the Rotunda, in Bourke-street. The
supply of untamed steeds is increasing in fury, so
the publio may one night be treated to a sudden
death of the low comedian ostler. This bullet-
headed myrmidon is required to stir up the
(quadrupeds’ feelings by walloping a drum, and
if, perchance, he were to meet with a fatal acci-
dent in the discharge of his awful duty, a good
many people would congratulate themselves upon
his peaceful end.

Annoyed by plaj'-going priests in Westminster, Cardinal

Miss Alma Stanley desires it to be known, with reference
to a recent Bulletin paragraph, in regard to the mono-
logue “ How it Happened,” recited by her in “My Friend
from India,” that the monologue in question was
specially written for her by the late George Fawcett Rowe,'
in 1883, under the name “ Our First Kiss,” under which
title it has been played by her in England and America
from time to time ever since.

Captain Fred. Thomas, of Maoriland, the only whistling
ventriloquist in these parts who can lay over YVinton, has
left the land of the awful Scotch Sabbath, and is looking
for a wider field for his talents in Australia. The captain
—this paper doesn’t exactly know what he is captain of —
called in at The Bulletin office on arrival, and did some
hair-raising varieties of sample whistle, so the writer
speaks with authority.
A matinee performance at Melb. Princess’s for the bene*
fit of Vic. Infant Asylum yields £l6O, and the all-wool
pietists of the city and suburbs gnash their gums over
“The Sign of the Cross” as a Satanic manipulation of
Keristianity intended to beguile souls from the churches.
Charity doesn’t cover the other fellow’s sins.
One Melbourne parson complains of a diminution in
“the plate” since the outbreak of “The Sign of the
Cross.” The sanctified indulge in a bob seat in the Prin-
cess’s gallery, and pay for four sermons with shirt-buttons.
This narrows down the issue between church and stage to
the vital point, the fight for the standard—coin.
“The Sign of the Cross” cost the Firm at least £2500
to put on the stage.

Royal, and that was to an amateur performance.

French music that has doubtless been cribbed from by

pioneer comic opera ?

Lucia Harwood, one - time an ornament of George
Rignold’s co., was divorced by her husband (King-Hall) in
London, the other week. Lucia must really be getting
quite old enough to know better. She played one of the
young princes in the original Lyceum production of
“ Charles 1.,” about four-and-twenty years ago.
Mrs. Bland Holt’s part in “The Cotton King” was
created in London by gigantic Alma Stanley, which ex-
plains certain allusions to the physical strength of the
character. Mrs. Holt’s appearance (on the programme) as
a “ Female Hercules ” is mystifying.
Norton Smith, the horse-trainer now' at Melb. Rotunda,
has been running his show around the world these last six
years, or more. At Glasgow, on one occasion, he drew
the record “ gate ” for an open-air entertainment. 11,000
odd people paid to see him tackle a notorious stallion
named Charlie that was said to have killed four men.
After a few attempts to add Smith to its list of victims,
the animal was subdued—for that evening, anyway.
“Skipped by the Light of the Moon,” as played in
America and Australia, was a pirated show. G. R. Sims
wrote it, under the heading of “ The Gay City,” without
musical embellishments, and one night, when “The Gay
City” was being performed in an English provincial
theatre, a young American took it down in shorthand.
The pirate interpolated sundry ditties, changed the title,
and sold the little piece to a ’Murkan actor, from whom
John Gourlay, so the story goes, derived the Australian
rights. Years afterwards the pirate went back to Eng-
land and had to make a full confession to G. R. Sims.
They settled the trouble amicably. The original piece
had been worth very little to the author ; the alterations
had improved it, so he took possession of “Skipped,” as
it stands now, on payment of a modest sum.
Bland Holt’s only terror is lest the supply of horse-
drama should run out.
At Melb. Exhibition Building, the occasional license to
sell strong drink expires at 7 p.m., seemingly. This sort
of law is made and enforced chiefly in the interests ol
somebody who isn’t General Public. By what right does
a privately-owned theatre run its own grog-shop at all
times, whilst a national building has its value discounted
by being compelled to conduct the evening shows on
lemonade principles?

Melbourne friends of Moulton, the musical constructor
of “ Lelamine,” are announcing that the young man in-
tends to take his opera to England straightway, and put
it on first at Manchester, because Manchester is more
critical than other places, and will therefore be quicker to
appreciate a great work. Seemingly, the theatrical
managers in every city are waiting for an unknown com-
poser to come along and “put his opera on”—at their
risk. And Moulton, with his forefinger to his brow, finally
decides to give Manchester the first treat.
Two rival attaches of a stage lady, and not a very youth-
ful goddess at that, waged a gory war on her account
some short time since. The trouble originated on the
Melbourne “Block,” where A. (connected with
the profeshun) struck attache 8., a long-haired actor
of slender build, for daring to resent an insult to
their mutual friend. 8., in preference to retaliating on
the spot, with a fair chance of being top dog in a brief
rough-and-tumble, imprudently challenged his foe to a
“ scrap ” on the Yarra bank, behind the boat-sheds
There the warriors met, two hours later, accompanied by
seconds, referee, and timekeeper. They squared-up with
due regard for melodramatic effect, and exchanged sicken-
ing thuds upon sundry places for nearly 10 minutes.
After which the battered champion threw up the sponge
and went into close retirement for a fortnight. It was
hardly a case for sympathy, except from a purely sporting
point of view. Had the wife of the defeated one been
present she probably would not have washed away his
blood-stains with her tears.

Royal Conservatoire. He is now 18 years old, and has
been three years in Brussels, where he is a pupil of Pro-

veteran Poussard.
Dear Bulletin,—There is another and quite „ different
way of looking at Marcus’s offer of marriage to Mercia in
the “ Sign of the Cross.” It isn't funny. It doesn t pre-
sent the least resemblance to Bland Holt proposing to
Mrs. B. in a melodrama. Also the lady doesn't make a
gratuitous confession that she loved Marcus “ from the
first moment that she saw his legs.” The situation is per-
fectly reasonable. Mercia is a highly-strung woman with
an absolute belief in heaven and hell and eternity and
various other things. As a woman she loves the hand-
some Prefect with his clanking armor, and his jewels, and
his fame, and, among other attractions, his courtesy and
his kindliness of heart. Marcus makes love to her, but
the idea of the Prefect of Rome —the second man of the
empire—desiring to marry a plebeian girl, and a criminal
to boot, is about as incredible as if the Duke of Norfolk,
or some other British patrician, offered to marry any
attractive little Cockney seamstress who was doing three
months in gaol. Therefore Marcus’s love-making only
stamps him in her mind as a libertine of a very ordinary
sort, and Mercia’s hero, with his pomp and his jewelled
mail and his-other glitter, gets down into the dust.
According to her creed he is Lost, and will fry
on the bars of the hereafter for billions of ages. And
when he makes that incredible offer of marriage, which
is the only really ridiculous thing in the play, Mercia
gives way to an outburst of perfectly natural joy. The
man she loves with her illogical womanly soul
isn’t the coarse libertine she took him for,
therefore, according to her own simple, Early Christian
logic, he probably, or at least possibly, won’t be eternally
damned after all. And, whereas she was going, a moment
before, to start for the heaven that she believes in so ab-
solutely (in her own foolish, Early Christian way) with a
conviction that her hero will never get there, she now
starts with a knowledge that he is much more of a hero
than she imagined, and she may meet him in that
legendary region, after all. To the person who recognises
Mercia as a highly-strung visionary the situation is per-
fectly logical and intelligible; to the person whose sole
philosophy is leg, of course, it isn’t. But it is an ab-
surdity to imagine that legs were the attraction then that
they would be now. In those days the male legs of all
the vast Roman Empire were more or less available for
public inspection, and legs were probably too common to
excite any-interest worth mentioning. And the attempt
to look at everything from the undressed-leg point of
view, and to consider everything in a leggy frame of mind,
is a dreadful weariness.—T.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Queensland National Bank reports that the
Aalf-year ended June 30 shows a loss of £25,425, com-
pared with a loss of £1607 last half and a profit of £10,021
for the half ended June, 1896. The loss is partly
apologised for on the ground that the reduction
of deposit interest did not come into force till
31st March, and partly ascribed to certain rearrange-
ments which caused nine months’ interest on the Govt.
London deposits to be crowded into one half-year. Still it
would appear that the Q.N.B. has already saved nearly
enough by three months reduction of interest to the local
depositor" to cover this* extra payment to the Government,
and that, reckonirig.things on the same basis as last half-
year, its profits are something like £20,000 less, which
means a sudden drop at the rate of £40,000 a year. The
bank expects to save £112,000 a year by reduction of in-
terest, so it appears, on the present basis, that a very
considerable part of that beautiful margin is gone
already. Out of the available £70,000, or so, per annum-
assuming things get neither better nor worse—the
Q.N.B. has to accumulate enough to pay off nearly
£1,600,000, which is nominally written off the amount of
the deposits, but which is to be liquidated gradually out
of profits, so by present appearances it has a long road to
travel, and its prospects are considerably worse than they
were.
Capital has been written down from £928,527 to
£453,886, but otherwise the balance-sheet is practically
unaltered. Said balance-sheet promises to be rather
a difficult affair to manage. Nominally, the bank writes
about £1,600,000 off its deposits, and apparently it wants
that amount badly to write down its securities to present
values. But as it undertakes to pay the amount all the
same—only out of profits instead of assets —the £1,600,000
still remains some kind of a liability, and can’t well be
dropped out of the balance-sheet. If it isn’t dropped and
the assets are written down, the sheet will show an ap-
palling shortage, and, if the assets are not written down,
the sheet will look square, but will be admittedly quite
unreliable. On the other hand, if the assets are written
down and the balance-sheet is squared by leaving out the
aforesaid liability, it will again be quite unreliable. So
far, things appear to be made evep by leaving the securi-
ties at very nearly their old value. The cash and
other liquid assets have improved somewhat during
the half-year, but the writing-down leaves the Q.N.B. a
very small concern, with £454,000 capital, of which no less
than £287,000 consists of “ premises, furniture, and
stationery.” This leaves it with £167,000 of its own
money to trade upon, which is remarkably little for a
concern that has about £1,600,000 of liabilities to pay out
of profits some day or other.
Next balance-sheet should give some distinct
idea of the Q.N.B.’s prospects ; the present one is too
confused to show anything very definite, except the loss
of £25,000. Six months ago manager Ralston remarked,
proudly, that the then loss was £1607, “ but if the new
scheme had been in operation there would have been a
profit of £54,611.” It now looks as if there would be
nothing even distantly resembling that figure. Several
other banks have made similar statements in the past,
but in every case the unaccountable slump in profits rose
up voraciously just at the wrong moment and swallowed
the apparent saving, or most of it. On present appear-
ances it really looks as if the Q.N.8., after saving
£112,000 a year interest, might not have a very much
larger margin of profit than it had, on paper, three years
ago.
m * * *
The Bank of North Queensland keeps moving
slowly but steadily in the right direction. The half-year
just ended shows a profit of £2003, as compared with
£1717, £1702, £llB2, and £1736 for the four previous half-
years. There is no dividend—the profits only amount to
2 per cent, per annum, despite the improvement—but
added to previous balance they make £9368 to credit of
profit and loss. Since the 1893 trouble the B.N.Q. has
written off 150,000 capital and about £12,000 of reserves
to cover losses, and if there is no reasonable prospect of
further depreciation it is about time it started a new
reserve-fund to replace the very humble one of £3OOO which
went down in that historic crash. Considering that the
North Queensland’s entire ourrent liabilities to the public
are only £269,000, its cash reserve of £99,ooo—equal to 37
per cent. —looks needlessly solid. If it invested £20,000
of this in Government securities, the dividends would
make a very valuable addition to profits. There is a con-
siderable increase both in deposits and cash reserve, but
still the B.N.Q. is a very limited affair, and though the
exceeding smallness of its liabilities as compared with
capital makes it eminently safe it doesn’t look like being
a gaudy source of wealth to its shareholders for a long
while to come. Also, in view of the continued low rates
of interest, the experiment of taking £50,000 of deposits
for 10 years at 4J4 per cent, looks about as dubious as it
did a year ago.
* * » •
The Bank of Victoria, one of the few recon-
constructs which pays its 4J4 per cent, to the bitter end,
has a reasonably prosperous result to show. The half-
year’s profits are £17,877, as compared with £17,928,
£15,068, £20,631, and £19,863 for four previous half-years.
A dividend of 5 per cent, to preference shareholders
absorbs £10,419, and the remaining £7458, added to pre-
vious balance, leaves £46,378 at credit of profit and loss.
If the profits were spread over the whole capital, they
would equal rather over 2J per cent. —not much, com-
pared to the regular 10 per cent, of a few years ago, but
very good as matters go nowadays. The Victoria has now
paid off 60 per cent, of its reconstructed deposits,
and hopes to wipe off the rest within a few months.
The large saving in interest thus effected, how-
ever, hasn’t increased the net profits to any ex-
tent worth mentioning; apparently, “depreciation”
has swallowed up the saving as fast as effected.
The cash and liquid assets (61,670,000) equal 28 per cent,
of the liabilities to the public, and the business show’s a
steady improvement all round, so the position is good
enough, though not quite all it might be.
One notable fact is that the Victoria has just
forfeited a large batch of shares for arrears of calls, and the
sum paid on them (£61,238) has been promptly swallowed
up in writing off depreciation. This reveals the ominous
fact that, despite the writing-off of £250,000 reserve fund,
the depreciation was not entirely provided for after all,
and seeing the board has been going along peacefully
paying dividends to preference shareholders, and saying
nothing about this £61,000 of losses unprovided for, thei 8
is evidently just a possibility that there are other losses
still unprovided for about which it is also saying nothing.
Anyhow, considering the still precarious condition of
business, these 5 per cent, dividends seem very doubtful
poliev. Since 1893 the Bank of Victoria has earned
£174(031, and has paid away £127,653 of this amount in
dividends, leaving £46,378 as the entire accumulation.
It even paid three dividends to ordinary shareholders.
There is no reserve fund as a provision against any new
losses, and, in view of the £61,238 of further depreciation
abovementioned, this cheerful lavishness looks wholly
unjustifiable. The system followed by the Royal of
Queensland, under which a quarter of the profits must
be placed regularly to the reserve, is one which a good
many other banks would do well to follow.
* * * *
The Queensland Deposit Bank, which got into
difficulties in ’92, and was in some further trouble in ’94,
is again reconstructing. This time it isn’t nearly so en-
thusiastic about its absolute solvency, and bursts (if that
is the correct word) in a minor key. The Deposit concern
wrote off half its capital some time ago and called up ss.
a share —the whole amount available. It now states that
it owes £80,257 to depositors and other creditors, and has
£69,000 of assets in hand, so, apparently, the other half
of the capital is lost also. The call is expected to make
good the deficiency and pay everybody except the miser-
able shareholders, who will get their reward Hereaft ir.
Meanwhile, the Deposit Bank wants “ more time,” and
proposes to pay the depositors (£74,000 worth of them)
in 19 instalments between Feb., 1898, and Feb., 1917. Also,
it desires that interest shall be reduced from 41 to 3 per
cent, as from last Feb., that payment of interest shall be
suspended for 22 years, the whole amount to fall due in a
lump in Feb., 1919, and that no compound interest shall
be payable. This sort of proposal would have only led to
derision and road-metal a few years ago, but the Aus-
tralian depositor is a perfectly tame animal now, and will
feed out of the hand. The news has been broken to him
in so many different instalments that he can stand almost
anything by this time. The Q.D. Bank, by the way, “ re-
serves the right ” to pay off all the deposits at any time.
This is mentioned, presumably, lest it should rush at its
depositors with the money at an inopportune moment,
when they hadn’t anything handy to put it in.

The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. (Melb.),
comes out only moderately well with a half-year’s profit
£1756 compared with £1779 last half, and £1702, at
June, ’96. The usual 5 per cent, dividend absorbs £l5OO,
and the balance (£256) added to amount previously on
hand, makes £4770 to carry forward. This, as usual, is
cutting things very fine, especially in view of the fact that
the Od. still puts in its £14,000 of preference shares,
and £lB,OOO deposits on the dilapidated Australian
Deposit and Mortgage Bank, at their face value.
Whatever they may dev elope into ultimately, the fact
remains that, just now, these alleged £32,000 worth of
assets are only worth about 618,000, and they may never
be worth any more—they may even be worth a great deal
less. If the difference has to be written off, it will make
a huge gap in the reserve of £40,000, and if it is proposed
to cover any possible loss by accumulating £256 per half-
year out of profits, the co. has a long road to travel.
Meanwhile, the balance-sheet, by ignoring this large de-
preciation, doesn’t represent the actual state of affairs
any too accurately.

Reader: By way of further information about
the N.S.W. Distillery Co., The Bulletin now discovers
that Mr. E. Viles’ connection with it ceased on November
14, 1895, Mr. F. E. Fox being appointed secretary in
his place. At that early date—nearly two years ago—
there was talk of winding-up and other troubles. The
cash collected up to then was £451, and as £350 had
already gone in expenses, there seems to have been
mighty little left to start any distillery with. After that
meeting the N.S.W. Distillery Co. goes right oyer The
Bulletin’s horizon, and where it evaporated to this paper
is as vet unable to discover.
.* * * *
In a tired sort of way Hobart citizens rise to
protest against Premier Braddon’s syndicate-mongering.
What started them was a cable stating that Braddon had
arranged with Siemens Bros, to give them certain water
powers for the generation of electricity in Tas., and that
he had made some other Jubilee promises regarding rail-
ways. These things are looked on as a sort of an infringe-
ment of Tasmania’s bargain with the Great Western
gridiron syndicate ; hence the howl. Braddon is certainly
a firm believer in private enterprise : Director of the
Anchor mine, proposed ditto of Waratah-Zeehan railway,
local director in the Hobart Tram Co. monopoly, and pro-
bable ditto of the Great Western Railway syndicate, is a
fairly big load for a Premier to carry.
* ♦ * *
Be Melbourne’s emptiness : Despite the number
of houses vacant, it is hard to find a sanitary one for a
small family, almost all having been built regardless of
health considerations—everything has been sacrificed to
economy.

And they must be well-dressed, well-educated, and

* * * *
Launceston (Tas.) is showing the way to other
Australasian cities in the matter of electric light. A most
complete and up-to-date plant has been erected at a total
cost, including all initial expenses, of £75,000. Water-
power is the agency used to generate the electricity. At
present only 500 h.p. is developed, but the water is
capable of generating 1500 h.p. in the summer and 2000
h.p. in winter, and even this can be increased by a further
expenditure on weirs. Cost of maintenance, including
interest on money borrowed, provision for sinking fund,
and all reasonable allowances for wear and tear, is
£6OOO per annum. Against this, £3OOO is received from
private lighting, and a book allowance of £lBOO made for
street lighting, which was the amount formerly paid the
Gas Co. The difference, £l2OO, is made up by a slight
increase in the general rate. Electricity is rapidly dis-
placing gas both for light and power purposes, and the
likelihood is that in less than a year the new system will
be entirely self-supporting, and thereafter will be a sure
source of revenue to the council. The light traverses an
area of 3J4 square miles, whereon a population of 18,000 is
distributed—a population and area akin to North Shore
(N.S.W.). If a scheme of this kind can be a success in a
comparatively small place like Launceston, it should be a
splendid thing for Sydney. In the Cole and Grose rivers,
on the other side of Richmond, there is ample water-
power to generate electric light for every establishment
and house in the city and suburban area (population over
400,000), and power for every factory and workshop in the
same locality. This was practically the scheme of the
notorious Australian Rights Purchase crowd, and if the
Sydney civic powers don’t take hold of the matter the
shindykite boomster will be again working it for all it is
worth.

Broken Hill exports for the half-year included
£320,439 worth of concentrates, as against £237,148 in the
preceding half-year. But in the export of crude ore
there was a fall from £284,895 to £211,967—due to the
collapse in the Junction recently ; the Junction had, up
to that time, been by far the largest exporter of crude
ore. The total value of B.H. exports for the half-year
was £1,022,692, against £893,964 in the corresponding
half of ’96.
• * * *
Ballarat of N.S.W. Substantial Govt, aid has
enabled this show to bore into what looks like a payable
run of wash, and a main shaft has been decided on. Is it
a condition of the contract that the co. return the Govt,
money out of the first gold won ?
* * * *
The public has long wearied of the monotony of
reading of the sales of so many tons of copper from the
Great Cobar mine, N.S.W. At last the returns of the
last six months’ run are published : 33,400 tons of ore pro-
duced 1307 tons of copper, 65270 z. gold, and 15,5160 z.
silver. Approximate value, £90,000. Working cost not
given, but it may be taken that it is less than half the
proceeds. Averages : Copper, a shade below 4 per cent.;
gold, a little under 4dwt.; silver, about Joz. to the ton of
ore. The copper average during the last six months has
been brought down about 1 per cent, by the quantity of
poor ore left in the old stopes and now run through to
cheapen smelting. Great as has been the enterprise of
the tributors, the mine has been proved quite big enough
to stand more than double this output, and the chances
are it will not be long ere this is accomplished. It is
asked why the market value of Mount LyelJ is about ten
times that of the Cobar mine. That is quite unanswer-
able.
* • * *
Another N.S.W. mine sold and got the money
—most others reported sold never get it. The Witney
Green, a few-miles from Lucknow, is supposed to be on
the Lucknow line of lode. Every mine within many
miles of Lucknow is supposed to be similarly situated.
Cash £14,000 and some 5000 shares in the new co.—an
English one. A rich surface show ; some 20 tons crushed
for 200 oz. gold. Impossible to say from amount of work
done how long the mine will last.
• * • *
Another Bull-owned mine in N.S.W. closed
down—the Golden Gully—for reconstruction. Many
do that, but few reconstruct. When this mine and plant
were sold to clear off debts some of the shareholders were
furious, and, when the new co. was formed in London,
many nasty insinuations were made. It has cost the Eng-
lish co. upwards of £20,000.
* * * *
A few weeks since, when it was learned that
Govt, intended cutting a road from the Beacon gold-mine
(Upper Bucca, near Coramba, N.S.W.) to the main Coff’s
Harbor-Coramba road, many predicted that as the side-
cutting (nearly three miles) progressed a reef was almost
sure to be cut. Many fairly good reefs have been located
within a few miles of the Beacon, and as the, surveyed
route followed the side of the ridge across the creek from
where Tajdor discovered the Reward —now Beacon —the
road-work looked like a good prospecting venture. How-
ever, in that three miles of cutting not a leader was cut!
The same holds good on the miles of cutting around the
range between Coff’s Harbor and Coramba. Can any
Bulletin reader say whether, in the history of the con-
struction of the hundreds of miles of roads across ranges
in N.S.W., any payable reef has been discovered? If so,
where? Between" Laura and May town (Q.), on the old
Palmer field, the road for miles passes through what were
once alluvial claims; but there the claims were worked
first—the road followed.
* * * *
Terrible were the experiences of the recent Ger-
man exploring party led by Ehlers into the hinterland of
German New Guinea. The explorers had to cut their way;
were drenched by almost continual tropical rain ; were bled
and wounded by countless leeches, and had then the
pleasure of finding their wounds invaded by insects whose
eggs in a few days turned into red maggots ; grass and
leaves formed their food for days together, until dysentery
supervened ; and, to crown all, the hapless leaders, while
floating down-stream on a raft, were drowned. And now,
who’s for Papua ?
* • » *
Latest Papuan joke. At Woodlark the gold-
bearing country is mostly clay, and consequently very
boggy. Also, the Woodlark miners are a thirsty race . . .
wherefore all the way to Busai empty gin-cases have been
placed six feet apart, and the traveller bound thither-
wards has to jump from one to the other.
* * * *
A special correspondent has furnished Sydney
D.T. with an excellent account of the gold prospects of
New Guinea, which those still contemplating going
might well read : “ Large mining syndicates may be able
to find a field for superfluous energy and cash, remunera-
tive, perhaps, at some future date, and now and then
some lucky individual might drop across a golden pocket
—who can tell ? But, though I cannot of course form an
opinion of the actual mineral wealth of Papua, I can
judge the difficulties of looking for it, and the small man,
I honestly believe, will at present not stand the least
chance of rewarding himself for the many inevitable
hardships and losses and terrible risks he runs. It is
easier for a camel to walk through a needle’s eye than for
a poor man to get into Papua.”
* * * *
And this is how the “ little ” man fares in
Papua. 34 miners returned per s.s. Merrie England,
three-fourths of them sick ; #nly six paid their fares back,
the rest being destitute. All came from Port Moresby
—whence, they say, there is no chance of getting into
the Mambare country. The Merrie England brings news
that the bodies of two men were found on the Vanappa
track. Evidently one tried to bury the other, and while
in the act fell dead.
* • • *
The joys of latter-day squatting. A well-known
Northern pastoralist despatched a mob of 311 bullocks to
Townsville a month ago. Ninety beasts arrived safely in
that sultry city, the remainder fertilising the roadside
with tick-burrowed carcasses. Yet out in the West the
pest is comparatively innocuous and bids fair to dis-
appear.

* » * *
Warden M‘Donald, of Charters Towers, reports
that over 40 cyanide-plants are now at work there.
Others are being erected and the industry is giving work
to several hundred men. Although nearly all the tailings-
heaps have been appropriated there are persons still eager
to acquire the balance. There must be many heaps in
other parts of Queensland where cyanide could be used
profitably, though in some cases the heaps are too small
and isolated to warrant the first outlay.
* * * *
The reefs that are payable on the world’s gold-
fields have hitherto averaged 3 per cent., but it is a
question if the Coolgardie and Murchison fields will be
able to maintain that average through the extraordinary
number of leases taken up during the boom, numbering
altogether over 14,000. If 1 per cent, of that number pay
it should be a record.
• * * *
Some W. A. miners, to be on the safe side, won’t
work at a mine unless there is machinery, or a big stack
of wood, on the mine—so many mines have, of late, been
shut down, without enough money to pay the men off.

The Australian Mail, London, offers a warm
welcome to a Westralian mining expert who, it under-
stands, is coming to London to float more W.A. mines,
and to obtain appointments as consulting-engineer to cos.
already working mines there. “It would be interesting
to know how’ many of the properties this gentleman has
recommended have turned out duffers.” It gives a few
mines he reported on which have been abandoned at a
heavy loss, and, as it believes the list could be very much
extended, it invites readers to send along the names of
other worthless properties which w’ere floated mainly on
statements made by him.

The directors of the Ivanhoe Co., W.A., have
circularised the shareholders, setting out the terms of the
proposed sale to the London and Globe Financial Cor-
poration. The new co. is to provide an Australian register;
a Melb. Board to transfer shares, issue scrip, and distri-
bute divs. in Australia ; also provided in the contract that
scrip is to be issued simultaneously in London and Mel-
bourne. “ The terms obtained appear to your directors
to be so satisfactory that they cannot but think it will
meet with your unanimous approval, and be ratified by
the requisite majority of shareholders.” We should
think so. £lO,OOO has been paid, another £15,000 to be
paid on the ratification of the agreement, both to be for-
feited if the purchasers fail to compete.

What has become of Bayley’s No. 2 South,
floated in London for £lOO,OOO, of which vendor took

reefs running right through the lease and 400 tons at grass
estimated to go at least 4oz. to the ton. What became of
this stone? There is much gall in reading Westralian
prospectuses of two and three years ago by the peep"'’' ho
took up shares on the strength of the statemSii’i. de
therein.
* * * *
It now transpires that it was the enterprising
W. R. Wilson who started the deal of turning over the
Lake View mine, W.A., to a bigger co., and for his trouble
he lately recovered, in London, a judgment (£48,750 and
costs) against the London and Globe and Finance Cor-
poration, which took the matter over from him. Wilson
withdrew, and for so doing was to receive 7500 shares in
the.new co. It was argued by the L.G.F. Co. that he was
only entitled to the value of the shares on the day they
w’ere allotted, but Justice Wills ruled that Wilson, not
knowing till October that defendants were going to re-
pudiate, was entitled to the price of the shares at that
time, and gave judgment accordingly.
• * • •
T. A. Rickard, the well-known mining engineer,
arrived in Sydney, from America, this week, on his way to
Westralia, where he proposes concerning himself w’ith the
occurrence and treatment of tellurides. He is accom-
panied by Mr. Bancroft, as assayer and metallurgist, and
is recently from Cripple Creek, Colorado, where tellurides
are now v'orked to a depth of 700 ft. Before this, the
presence of tellurides in the form of either gold or silver
was regarded as anything but evidence of permanancy of
the ore-bodies in which they are found. Their occurrence
in American mines has never been welcomed. Mr.
Rickard is emphatic on that point. How it was that Mr.
Maryanski, coming from America and seeing tellurides in
W.A., should say that they tended to establish the rich-
ness and permanency of the fields, Mr. Rickard doesn’t
understand. M.M. may prove right as far as Hannan’s is
concerned, but on the basis of American experience it only
looks like a good guess anyhow. With the exception of
Cripple Creek, it has alw’ays been the other W'ay about
with tellurides in Yankeeland.

Had the representative of the Hampton Plains
Syndicate carried out his original idea of making Mt.
Burgess the N.E. corner of the co’s freehold it would have
included the whole of Coolgardie and a portion of Kal-
goorlie. And all for half-a-crown per acre. As it is, the
western boundary is mile east of Coolgardie, and the
northern boundary 11 miles south of Kalgoorlie. By the
way, had Bayley and Ford not found Coolgardie for an-
other six months, the H. Plains land crowd would have
forfeited its deposit.
* * * *
Over 10,000 leases have been taken up on Cool-
gardie goldfields, and if the results of assays, reports of
experts, and newspaper boosts had been realised, they all
should have been worth from soz. to 5000 oz. to the ton.
Let us sum up : 10,000 leases, running 250,000 stampers,
crushing 100,000,000 tons of quartz annually for
500,000,0000 z., equal to £1,750,000,000, which would enable
the owners to send Mayor M'Donald, of Coolgardie, ’Ome
to make the record present of the record reign, and for
all time, by handing a draft to the Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer to wipe out the British national debt. He could
also have a draft for a trifle of £80,000,000 for a fleet of
battle-ships to guard the coast of the modern Ophir, ex-
cept for a few weeks when Melbourne Cup carnival was
on. But alas !

SOCIETY
4 butchered to save a cabman’s extra fare :
“.One, of the most piteous of Sydney hospital
sights” (writes a Bulletin correspondent) “is
that of a, sinking patient trying in vain to get
sleep amidst the rumble of cabs rolling along
Macquarie-street when there is a ball at Govt.
House. What chance will such victims have if
the railway ever comes to King-street ? ”
Macquarie-street is laid with “open” blocks;
in the interests of common humanity these blocks
should be pulled up and re-laid “ close,” so as to
minimise the noise. Either that, or tan should
be put down and changed occasionally. The noise
now made under the windows of the big hospital
is simply slai'vhterous.
* * * *
At Perth, the other day, the cross-
examination of a Frenchman who had been
stabbed by a compatriot elicited the fact that he
was himself a convicted murderer who had escaped
from New Caledonia—whe'reupon his wife, who
was in court and to whom the revelation was a
thunderstroke, had to be carried out insensible.
The Australienne who contemplates espousing the
fascinating foreigner would do well in most cases
to make previous inquiry at the proper consulate.
• * • •
Apropos of the two well-connected Mel-
bourne lady shoplifters, whom Panton, P.M., in
a burst of righteous indignation, sentenced to a
weeks gaol apiece for wholesale thieving—the
shoplifter who isn’t well-connected gets from six
months to three years—a London paper publishes
a strange story about shoplifters in general. The
fashionable lady-thief is numerous in London,
and, as a rule, prosecuting her involves much
waste of time and gives very little satisfaction.
Therefore, one swell draper, it is alleged, has the
detected kleptomaniac led into his back premises
where she is offered the alternative of being
arrested or being flogged by the proprietor’s sister,
in which latter case the painful subject will there-
after be dropped. It is asserted that a tolerable
large and. influential section of society has been
spanked in a room behind that drapery man’s
premises.
» « * *
Sydney (in which the God-fearing Ministry
says there are only 200 unemployed) is a beautiful
place to wander in by night. There are long rows
of rubbish-boxes along the edge of the pavement,
and at nearly every box there is a hurried,
anxious, shivering ghoul turning over the con-
tents in search of something eatable. Where the
midnight ghoul gets to by daylight is a mystery ;
the kind you see at night is even a more hairy,
and frayed, and tattered edition than the species
which hang out in the Domain by day. Probably
the one who skims the dirt-kegs after closing-up
time is so far gone about the clothes that he can’t
even enjoy the run of the park, lest he should be
arrested for indecency, and he gets into the cellar
of an empty house about sunrise, and lies low
t ere till night comes on again. Mostly, he has a
sort of black halo about him, the halo on his head
being hair and his body-halo being rags.
* * * *
Morose prisoners, like the late Butler, have
the days leading to the guillotine more rationally
ordered in France than are those leading to the
gallows in Australia. Australia treats its con damn 4
like a caged beast. Nay, worse; for keepers,
knowing their lion or tiger is securely caged,
observe him, from time to time, with a little
human interest. Australia, however, provides
keepers with a stony, official glare in both near
and off eye, which, to a man of moods, like Butler,
must be more maddening than insults. In France,
the Government furnishes the prisoner with a
compagnon, or mate, chosen from among other
prisoners, who shares the condainnd’s delicacies,
sleeps in the same cell, and plays cards, chess, or
draughts as the condamn4 desires. Every eatable
or drinkable the condemned orders, in reason, is
given to him in such measure as enables him to
treat his compagnon hospitably, so that a morose
subject is almost unknown in French prisons.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin, lie front-names : a
clergyman’s objection to any praenomen is of no
consequence, provided the registrar accepts it;
and he, by rejecting any designation that is not
“ blasphemous or obscene,” would simply relieve
the parents of responsibility for non-registration.
Mere absurdity he has nothing to do with. Also,
whatever preference R.C. priests may have for
saints’ names, there is no incumbency on them to
refuse any others. And, finally, there is no neces-
sary inseparability between a person and a baptis-
mal or registered name. A man’s virtual name is
simply whatever he will answer to.—A.M.
• • *
More bush justice. In a Tas. township a
man was arrested for “drunk and dis.” The
Super, of Police, wishing to get rid of the incubus,
went to the Warden’s house and asked his attend-
ance to hear the case. The head beak was at that
moment engrossed in cabbage-planting, but was
equal to the occasion. “ I’m too' busy to come
just now,” he said ; “ fine the beggar five shillings
and let him go.” The Super, complied. Justice
tempered with—vegetable production.

other thoroughfares. Apparently, the Brunker
Sabbath-keeping spasm lasted just one Sunday.

From a London private letter: —
“There are plenty of the Australian ex-soldiers knock-
ing about already. I saw one of those fellows from New

* » • *
Dr. Strong’s Australian Church held a
meeting, the other day, to consider its lamentable
financial condition. Apparently every variety of
Protestantism in this country has gaunt In-
solvency howling at the door of its tabernacle,
and Dr. Strong’s fane is no exception to the
general rule. The pastor explained in tones of
regret that the accumulated debt was .£11,722
10s. Bd., which seems a tolerably robust shortage ;
£9467 and some coppers of this being due to the
Savings Bank. How the Savings Bank will feel
when it forecloses that mortgage and has a £9400
church among its assets it is hard to say ; a big
stone temple should be about the most unsaleable
thing in Melbourne just now. The congregation
on Sunday nights, it appears, averages 900, but
such a very large pro|>ortion of these are seized
with catalepsy at the sight of the plate that the
profits are not worth mentioning. It was finally
resolved that the Australian Church should gird
up its _ offertory and make another hard struggle
for existence, and if it fails there will be one more
eligible tabernacle for sale down South, and the
all-devouring Salvation Army will probably take
over the business.

A Bulletin correspondent :
Incident of the recent Port Darwin cyclone. A China-
man was found apparently dead, among the ruins of his
house. He was placed in a coffin, and surprised the
mourners by beginning to show signs of life. Soon after
he called for rice, and when it was brought ate a fair
rneal (still in the coffin), and then pegged out in earnest,
full of years and rice.

A private letter (date. June 4th) from
’Ome to a Melbourne resident, says :
You would be surprised to see London now, it is like a
carpenter’s shop. The carpenters’ men have been earn-
ing 2s. per hour, and now they will have 2s. 6d. The
price of most things is up, and they tell us we shall have
to pay at least eighteenpence a pound for our meat. '

ters and butchers, anyhow.
* • * *
Much to the delight of the local vegetable
heathen, Launceston (Tas.) Christian young ladies
—most of them plump and prepossessing, by the
way—have lately been filled with a zealous yearn-
ing to save the souls of their yellow brethren. To
this end—or some other—prayer and social meet-
ings are held for the benefit of the alien. The
other night a tea-fight happened, at which the
Chows delivered little “testimonies.” presumably
arranged to convince the audience of the exuberant
spontaneity of the heathens’ conversion. One
particularly greasy idolator stopped short in the
middle of his oration and calmly announced that
he had “forket um ” the rest; but a compatriot
came to the rescue by handing up a dirty slip of
paper from which Ah Gup read his testimony right
from the start in most approved Sunday-school
English. “ This sort of thing, though amusing
must involve a good deal of self-sacrifice,” said a
lady visitor to one of the girls. “Oh, yes,” was
the reply; “ indeed it does. For instance, my
cousin, Miss So-and-so, has to sit between two of
the Chinese at every prayer-meeting, and ’’—well
perhaps it would be unwise to print the rest of her
remarks.
NOT AS THE SEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not as the sea, when wanton day has found -her,
And she, rich-smiled,
Draws her white fleecing laces closer round her,
Yet is beguiled;
But as the sea, when her salacious lover
Is gone, and she
Lies quiet-bosomed to the stars above her—
So thou to me.
Tic.
WYVIS.

* * * *
The first settlers in Westralia were mostly
English, and their descendants through long
isolation from the other portions of Australia re-
tain many of the manners and customs of their
forefathers. For instance, they call their employer
“the master,” a creek a “brook,” an overseer a
“ foreman,” the manager of a gold-mine a „ cap-
tain ; ” and use the expressions “up south and
“down north.”
* * * *
J.D. : “ How many Bulletin readers
have ever seen a ticket-of-leave ? Picked one up
the other dav, and for the first time understood
why a ticket-man so rarely exhibits his ‘ certifi-
cate of freedom ’ even to his most intimate
cronies. The System provided a fine outlet for
the surplus humor of its gaolers. On the back of
the ticket the description of the ‘freed prisoner
is set forth under the, following headings: Name,
grade, native place, height without shoes, age,
complexion, head, hair, whiskers, visage, forehead,
eyebrows, eyes, nose, mouth, chin, remarks.
Given those headings and a little savage humor
and what sort of a picture could be painted ? Ihe
one I found is simply unpublishable.”

* * * *
In Sydney, if an attempted suicide requires
hospital-treatment, the Police Department has to
supply a constable to watch him till he is dis-
charged, as the hospital authorities very properly
refuse to take any risks of injury to himself or the
other patients. The constables don’t like the
duty, as nurses “can’t abear coppers.”
* * * *
A search for precedents in a recent Melb.
fortune-telling prosecution revealed the fact that
m N.S.W. a case (R. v. Colan) decided that the
long-since-repealed Act of George 11. imposing
the pillory and a sentence of not less than 12
month’s gaol for sorcery, witchcraft and fortune-
telling was in force in N.S.W. in 1878 and is still,
apparently, above ground. The reasoning of the
J udges was most peculiar—and more so.

“ It is not generally known,” writes a Bul-
letin correspondent, “ that Melb...Hospital has
paid subjects, male and female, but such is the
fact. They live on their disease, and are always
un hand ready to be strung up (in the nude) for
Dr. Sawbones and gang to pull about.
One of Melb.’s least conventional press-
lydies told the Austral Salon at a recent meeting
“The cardinal virtues are not those with which
you paint the town red.” Quite so; when ver-
milion’s the order of the evening, the world punts
its pocket-money on the Scarlet Woman.

Dear Bulletin, —This is for
spiritualistic and metaphysical
readers. Last week I bought a
cheap (of course) W.B. house in
Northcote, Melb., which I wanted
moved to Oakleigh. A house-
contractor asked d£4o, but I knew
too much for that/ With three
serious-minded friends I sawed
away all the supports of the house,
then —closing all doors, windows,
fire-places, &c., to an air-tight
degree—we pumped the house full
of compressed air. Then each stood
at one corner, placed one finger
under the house, drew a deep
breath, and lifted. The house rose
as easily as a feather, and, still
holding our breath, we carried it
some few yards. This operation
we repeated for four hours, until
we finally reached Oakleigh. We
nearly had an accident on the
Caulfield railway - crossing the
youngest of the party laughed,
and the houseimmediatelydropped
on the line ; but the matter was
then much too serious for laughter,
and we soon got clear. I think
I see a fortune ahead in the house-
shifting profession. —F.C. J.
* * *
The eternal woman questi
Half the trouble among the na-
tives up in the Straits is caused
by the unfortunate custom the
niggers have of selling their gins
to colored aliens for a gun, am-
munition, rum, etc. Within the
course of a few months the abori-
ginal repents his bargain, endea-
vors to seduce his quondam spouse
from her new allegiance, and very
often gets a split head or a mouth-
ful of meat-axe in the operation.
And police begin to curse and wish
the gaol was bigger.
A Melb. solicitor lately found himself
lodging at a hotel with five other men, four of
whom had been divorced, whilst the fifth had had
divorce papers served upon him. The learned
fashion in which the four expounded the law of
divorce to the fifth, and explained processes,
papers and formalities to him, filled the profes-
sional lawyer with amazement.

Sydney School of Arts charges ss. 6d. a
quarter for male members and 2s. 9d, for their
wives and sisters. But the periods must be con-
current. It appears that, before this latter rule
was thought of, members who entered their wives
dropped out themselves and used the wives as
agents, thus obtaining the use of the library at
half-rates.

Necessary amendment of Yic. Factories
Act: The application of the minimum-wage pro-
visions and the definition of “factory” to the
Salvation Army wood-and-coal yards. However
laudable this body’s exertions to directly help the
poor and indirectly the snufflebuster, it’s coming
it too strong when it enters into keen competition,
at a barest pittance wage, with one of the hardest-
worked callings in the country.

LOVE MUSIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Heard through the trees, voluptuous strains
Of violins in concert blended.
He takes her hand (which still remains
In his, although the tune is ended)
And Maud is not at all offended.
Says he : “All love-born music seems
To bear me to a world of dreams
By Jove 1 it’s splendid.
“ Love music, on a sultry night,
Performed, of course, with real fee ling,
Gives strange, unspeakable delight
To such as I.” Then Gussie, kneeling,
With many vows and much appealing,
Persuades the silent girl to speak
“ One little word ’’—her manner meek
The truth revealing.
Next summer-time the lusty twins
Distract him with their cries nocturnal.
A year ago two violins
Had told a tale of joy eternal,
And life was like—the London Joubnal.
These strains of love-born music keep
Poor Gus from getting off to sleep—
O, d d infernal!
A CONFESSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They say, dear heart, that love of wealth
And love of fame, and love of wife,
And lover’s love will never stand
Against that test—the love of Life.
Yes 1 let me whisper it in shame
That this poor clay has found it true :
But may I add the saving clause,
That Life, my dearest one, is you.
W.P.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed in,
and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

A WOMAN’S LETTER .
My ItaMT- Sydney, July 20, 1897.
Three local artists and two ladies of the recently-
arrived Gay Parisienne ” eo., acted as judges at
the Juvenile Fancy Dress Ball, at the Town Hall,,
on 1 nursday night. The newcomers, sternly at-
tired m masculine-looking coats and skirts, enteredl
into the awarding business with much seriousness,
yet the entire lack of originality about the majority
ot the gowns made the decisions very easy. The
interpretation of the words “ fancy dress ” seems,
to most folk to be something cheap and eccentric
with swans-down or artificial flowers on it,
which accounts for the lavish scattering of
queens,” “ Flower girls,” “ Nights,” and'
oprings, at Thursday evening’s dance. One-
unusually pretty get-up, which managed to es-
cape . the eyes of the judges, represented a lily,
but the dainty maiden who wore it seemed anxious;
(marvellously enough) to conceal herself from view.
Ihe handsomest girl present—one, by the way, who-
had left childhood a good many years behind her,
and reached the age when knee-short skirts only
suggest the ballet —was smartly attired as a naval
officer; she wandered about with a Robin Hood,,
whose face, beplastered with a “spirit-gum ” yel-
a W << se^ rellJ inded one of a w T eek-old gosling.
i Military officer ”in scarlet mess jacket,
looked like a Dudley Hardy poster. The ’37
costumes were exquisitely dainty, and suited the
wearers well, but the incroyable suits of their
partners proved that the figure of the modern boy
is wholly unadapted to tight pantaloons and tri-
angular hats, and that the manners which go with
the dress of a courtly period are mere foolishness;
to him, Ihe galleries of the Town Hall were
packed with spectators, who struggled with each
er craned their necks to catch a glimpse of
the motley-attired crowd on the floor, and most of
the onlookers found the spectacle entertaining
enough to keep them chained to their seats till
after midnight. From a spectacular point of
view, however, the affair was really about as
amusing as a Methodist tea-meeting.
The girl’s dress which took the gold and
pearl bangle represented “Federated Australia,”'
in refulgent satin and many-colored ribbon
streamers. It was worn by little Zelma Aron-
son, and her brother also bore off a prize for
his historical make-up. Young Marks, as a China-
man, trotted into the prize-list with his cabbage
baskets. Among the unrewarded drapery, the
Hussar uniform on a tall blonde was conspicuous.
At 10 p.m. the grown ups crowded the floor and
pushed aside the small dancers, A tall man and
his partner collided with a tiny couple and brushed
them away, saying it was time for little boys and
girls to be in bed. “ Look here ! is this your ball
or ours ? ’ cried the six-year-old, squaring up
manfully to the six-footer.
Tfie pension of <£lso a year granted to Lady
Ihurston, widow of the late Go.v. Thurston, of
Fiji, will, if that lady’s friends in Suva be success-
ful, be augmented by a vote on the Fijian esti-
mates. The official salary of the Gov. of Fiji is
«2<ioo, which does not leave much margin, after
all expenses have been deducted, for providing for
the support of a widow and five children. Lady
Thurston is at present living in Sydney. Some of
her children are being educated in England. The
path of the wife of the Governor of a Crown
colony like Fiji is not altogether strewn with
roses. A great part of the inevitable expense
conies from keeping a free hotel for every globe-
trotter who arrives armed with a letter of intro-
duction “ tortile Governor,” and before the “ cir-
cular-letter trick was shut down upon, every
traveller who could scrape an acquaintance with a
Colonial Office hireling used to get free board and
lodging at all the Govt. Houses in the British
dominions on the strength of the missive which
he presented to each of H.M.’s representatives in
turn.
.4.1 in a Stanley looms much larger as Mme.
Paillard in “A Night Out” than the original
.trench lady, but hasn’t nearly as much to do.
Much of the latter’s “business ” is cut out of the
English version, and she plays the part standing
around. The defect of the farce is that it has had
much of the fun eliminated with the Frenchiness ;
some of the characters have a forlorn look, as if
they had nothing to do with the case, whereas in
the Parisian play every character was provided
with a neatly-fitting and quick-acting part. As
the waiter, Whelan can only rush up and down
stairs noisily through the second act; of course if
he carried on as in the original the Brunker-bossed
police would interfere. Several detached bits of
business, such as the collapsing chair and Pinglet’s
blackened face, should all come into the scheme of
things and make the farce rush rapidly along its
noisy, naughty career—instead of being, as they
are, solitary independent facts standing baldly out
of the piece and accentuating the world of dif-
ference between Gallic and Anglo-Saxon ideas of
taste and humor.
Although the Jubilee is off, Jubilee appeals still
go on. The charities now being drummed for in
ydney would take a good half-column of The
Bulletin to enumerate. And now it is decided
to add another to the list by establishing an
keep green the memory of the late
N.S.W. Chief Commissioner for Railways. The
proposal is full of good feeling, and the members
of the associations who met at the Chamber of
Commerce to arrange for it hope for a “suc-
cessful issue ” of the new idea. But when
the money has to be raked in with a steady, con-
tinuous stroke, the enthusiasm will probably ooze
off somewhat; though 11,000 railway employes
with influential outside backing should be able to
accomplish wonders. Just at present there are
far too many charities to the square inch, and
about 50 good causes are ready to snatch at each
available coin.
A new song, “ Thou Art Mine,” by Alfred Hill
(Paling and Co.), is very tuneful, with a stirring
military refrain. The sentiment of the words, by
T. Warner, describing the warlike hero going to
“love and laughter’’ in the south, while the
“maid’s fa,ir face grows pale and old” in the
north waiting for him, is all very well from a
man’s point of view, but a woman could hardly be
expected to give three cheers for it. Wiegand’s
“ Marche Francaise,” which appropriately adorned
music-shop windows on 14th July, weds effec-
tively with crashing chords the Russian and
French, national hymns. A waltz, “ Shel-
bourne,” by John Lee (Paling), is suggestive of
‘lhree Blind Mice” with variations. M. E.
Price has arranged “ On the Benches in the Park”
for dancing, and waltzes that rather weary motif
through several pages with some well-harmonised
padding. De Groen’s band has made this version
popular in ball-rooms during the season.
The exhibition by lady students at the Art
Society’s rooms, Pitt-street, was brightened by
one head and a couple of landscapes, otherwise the
show was average. This was the first “At Home ”
given by the students, and many guests assembled
to drink their cup of tea in the cause of Art. Mr.
Coffey’s lightning sketches and Mr. Alfred Hill’s
song helped the reception along. One girl reci-
tross came out strong, and gave “ Mary, Queen of
Scots to the bitter end with Potteresque ges-
tures, and there were some other events on the
programme. 1 his little party will probably be-
come an annual one.
Some lively charges and counter-charges are
made by the members of the Thornton co., now at
loggerheads. The co., while on tour, was a most
unhappy family. Noney Seabrooke, who issued a
writ against her former manager, is now staying
in Sydney, and intends, as soon as possible, to go
to her friends in her native Edinboro’. The
common report of an engagement between Miss
Seabrooke and the other writ-issuer, Herbert Terry,
is denied by both Terry, by the way, has not
left the co., and must find it hard work to nightly
act up to a man with whom he is not “ playing
speaks ”in private ; but he does it, all the same.
Theosophy has been boomed by the lectures
given in the Protestant Hall by Col. Olcott and
Miss L. Edger, who have been listened to atten-
tively by large numbers of the followers of the
mystic cult. rhe Colonel’s twang vibrates above
a whiter and more flowing beard than when he
was here six years ago ; otherwise he is unchanged.
M-iss Mger, who is obviously understudying
Annie Bes&nt in matter and style, wears flowing
neutral-tinted garments, clinging round her like a
grey sea fog on a mud-bank. Evidently the
astral body has no waist-line. The lady-lecturer,
in a, fluent, soothing voice, discoursed on re-incar-
nation as a cure for all ills. Also, on the irrever-
ence of Australian youth, an opinion which the
audience warmly applauded in a way suggesting
that they would be blessed if young Australia
•could be worried to death by wild Mahatmas,
and never come to earth again for a millennium
■or so.
llorence Schmidt’s second concert, on Saturday
night, in the Town Hall, showed that the plucky
■Queensland songstress is justified in her rather
arduous undertaking of drawing a Town Hall
audience “on her own.” The soprano’s own songs
were some which she has sung before, and for en-
cores she had prepared various ballads. There
was a Scotch flavor about the entertainment.
Mrs. Lance wailed that dreary dirge, “ Auld
Robin Grey,” in contralto, and Woodhouse and
Miss Schmidt both warbled of the kail-
yard. A feature of the evening was the piano-
playing of the minute Freda Miller, who used her
tiny fists effectively, and staggered off the plat-
form under a cartload of flowers. As an encore
she played the “Carnival of Venice;” in so
young a child the habit could surely be checked
before it became confirmed. H. Staeli’s artistic
violin-playing pleased people immensely. Miss
Ooutts-Duvalli was last performer, and her gilt
harp on the platform was a signal for half the audi-
ence to rise and stream noisily out; having
heard the soprano, they allowed their frantic desire
to catch trams and ’buses ten minutes ahead of
time to quite overpower their manners.
In “ Mrs. Quinn’s Twins ” at the Royal the jig
•danced by Maggie Moore and H. Roberts is one of
the best things in the show. It looks like the
real and not the stage Irish j ig. These old dances
are giving place to new. The very latest whirl
was danced for the first time in Sydney at a chil-
dren’s party in a private house a week ago. After
this it may spread in sassiety. The man dancer
trips behind his partner to the strains of “ Wash-
ington Post,” catching her hands, which she holds
backwards above her shoulders, and they kick
simultaneously in a kind of barn-dance hop.
Gush about the coming Ex. of Maoriland is
measured off by the yard in a daily which clips it
from a ’Ome paper for the benefit of readers un-
blessed with aristocratic acquaintances. The en-
raptured interviewer spills flattering adjectives
freely about the “elegant figure” of Lady Ran-
furly, which the severe lines of the tailor-made
gowns she habitually wears only serve to accentuate
he is “too nervous to ride,” but is “a thorough
sportswoman, being devoted to toboganning. ”
Ladyß. won’t get much of thatsportin Wellington
unless she tries sliding down Govt. House stairs on
a tea-tray. Her “ little spaniel frisks constantly
her feet as she walks,” which conveys the
idea of her ladyship tripping over doggie and
barking her Norman nose at every step. Then the
enthusiastic penwoman screams with delight over
some “ glorious ” diamonds possessed by this
paragon. After all the gush, the hard fact
that the eldest Uchter Knox olive-branch is 6ft.
high rather discounts the youthful Diana theory.
The “perfect gentleman” biz. evidently can’t
be run as a going financial concern in Sydney. In
answer to an advt. requiring services of “ agentle-
xnan (perfect) as companion to an invalid ; salary
«sBSO, 45 applicants rolled up in less than 15
minutes. Most of them looked like remittance-
men waiting for next mail, and some were of the
