Unemployed men are now applying for billets
as housemaids and indoor servants. A lady who
runs a large boarding-house advertised for a ser-
vant ; three men applied for the job, but she
explained that she wanted a housemaid. She
engaged a girl who didn’t suit, and the lady adver-
tised afresh. Two men answered the first day,
and so impressed was she at the persistence of the
appeal of one that he was engaged. The boarders
were astonished next day to find a fine-looking
young man established as a regular indoor slavey,
attending to the bedrooms, sweeping and dusting,
&e., and his mistress says her “new man” gives
her every satisfaction.
Spooks are said to flourish better in Melbourne
than Sydney, Most of the folk who run eerie,
other-world shows eventually settle on Yarra’s
banks, where, among other disciples of the occult,
one of the most successful and original of business
men governs all his actions by expensive weekly
advice obtained per medium. The controller of
Cissy ” and “ Geordie ” intends to practise for
the future down South, where interest in astral
paraphernalia is strong, and believers’ faith has
not yet received the rude shock which befel local
confidence two years ago.
A Burwood (Sydney) man, who is at one with
Rev. Harry Bryant, of St. Paul’s-a recent
importation—upon the stinginess of congregations
in general, has just returned from America and is
about to suggest a Yankee church expedient. He
says it is a practical impossibility, in a N. Y. fane,
to refrain from “parting.” The contribution-box
used contains a small bell which rings only when
a contribution is made. The collection is taken up
during the silence ensuing on the delivery of the
sermon.
Among new dance-music published by Paling &
Co., “Leura Falls” mazurka, by A. Nolan, is
tinkly ; “ Bunyip ” schottische, by Geo. Gardner,
is evidentlj r a fancied echo of the wail of that
fabulous animal ; “ Isatis ” waltz isn’t strikingly
original; “ Tandem ” waltz has a gaudily-printed
cover, showing a clever trick bicycling feat by a
girl on a tandem, who is “ coasting ” and at the
same time turning her head and apparently kiss-
ing the man behind her. Only a Selbini could do
that without falling off.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The latest vice-regal fad is prison - reform.
Lady Brassey and a coterie of Melbourne’s Nicest
visited the female section of Pentridge Prison the
other day, when special prisoners, who are always
kept on show for visitors, were lined-up, and several
were vigorously pump-handled by her ladyship.
One gaunt female, who had ‘five more left to do,’
shook the proffered hand warmly, and observed
blandly, in response to a Sunday-school common-
place : ‘ An’ I kin sye, yer lydyship, ez ’ow we’ll
all be glad ter see yer ’ere soon agen. ’ Queer that
the Governor’s wife should shake hands with
prisoners, but after a year’s pump-handling at
Government House Her Ex. probably doesn’t care
who it is.
“A down South Governor goes his own canny
way, and business people still regretfully recall his
predecessors comparative openhandedness. In
those palmy times, a vice-regal valet who took a
cab home from town was good for half a-crown
and a glass of ale. Nowadays the Governor him-
self is only a shilling fare, and a lackey who re-
freshes a cabby with household beer does so with
his heart in his mouth.
“ In the same province, under a former regime,
the aides took a wonderfully serious view of their
duties and responsibilities, considering to how
lenient and lighthearted a young couple they
owed allegiance. The present staff kicks up to an
extent that surprises, when one remembers that
there is an elderly eagle eye ever upon it and that
family prayers and home influences go on all the
time. A parson, visiting the big House one day
to get his Ex. to open his new church, was inter-
viewed by a frivolous A.D.G. and quite grieved
by the latter’s off-hand references to his em-
ployers. ‘ Gov. won’t give you a cent! P’r’aps
the missus ’! he chirped airily, disregarding the
apostolic protestations that gubernatorial ‘presence
and patronage ’ only were asked for.
“ Guests grumble because a Southern Governor,
laying in stores to be guzzled by his loyal subjects,
evidently never forgets that his yearly salary is
less by <£3ooo than his predecessor’s, and gives his
orders accordingly. Her otherwise popular lady-
ship is blamed on the score of the fewness of the
rooms thrown open for inspection on those
days when the gates of the big house lift up
their heads to admit general company. But,
bless you ! there’s not a mayoress or M.L.A.’s
wife but knows better than to leave her keys lying
around on reception days. Haven’t they all
heard of the Sydney hostess who ran upstairs for
her fan on her ‘ at-home ’ afternoon and found her
bedroom full of women trying on her gowns ?
“ A story is current, on the Melb. cab-ranks,
that a local hansom-driver was lately commis-
sioned by an Exalted Personage to drive him to
his home in South Yarra. Cabby, taking the
nearest route, cut in one of the side gates, and
pulled up at the illustrious back door of the
exalted one, whereupon the latter hopped out and
handed up a shilling. ‘ What’s this ? ’ con-
temptuously demanded Jehu, holding out a flat
palm, with the coin shining like a bull’s-eye in the
centre. ‘ Uin-um,’ said the E.P., ‘ashilling—urn
' plenty um. And, why—um—didn’t you come
in the front gate ?’ ‘ The front gate ! ’ said the
cabby, wilfully mistaking the identity of his fare.
Why. the front gate’s shut. They do say that
old Gassey lost a thrummer last week, and they’re
a-keepm’ the gates shut till ’e finds it ’
“ Melbourne actually has a lady student of
veterinary surgery. She has put up the record in
the shed of the biggest vet. in the city, and gives
promise, too, of shaping well at the work. What
next ? Lovely woman at the abattoirs.
Westralia’s high death-rate provides a con-
venient loop-hole for anyone inclined to the
vanishing trick, as a bright Melb. society girl with
a tuneful singing-voice learned to her cost. Her
fiance left her to seek fortune for them both in the
West. Soon, word of his death came to the girl
he left behind him. She put on mourning, cried
her eyes nearly out, and gave up dancing until her
tears were dried by the news that not only was
her lamented lover still alive, but married and
thriving in business in an inland Western town.
“ The last Liedertafel concert emboldened two
novices to make their first splash. The school-
girl pianiste with hair down her back, and stout,
warm-tinted hands, wrestled conscientiously with
a sonata, and looked young and bashful enough to
have left the public in peace for a few years longer.
The debutante vocaliste, on the other hand
dowagerish in a solid black gown, had evidently
staved off her initial appearance considerably past
the orthodox moment. The Liedertafel members
and associated ladies’ choir gave a vocal version of
the doings of shepherds and water-nymphs. Not
that a single one of the singers was a scrap like
the traditional dryad with red or golden tresses
and rudimentary green raiment. The Liedertafel
mermaidens were black-polled without exception,
and warded off draughts by fagged-out opera-
wraps, originally cream-colored, with only two
crude pink wool ‘ clouds ’ to break the whitey-
grey monotony.
“ The audience, for its part, was as dowdy a
crowd as ever refused to quit the family fireside
until too late to don its finery. Now that side-
combs and suchlike are fashionable, tortoise-shell
teeth and carbon daggers predominate coiffures
rather than hair. One damsel had surmounted
her plump Madonna-like face by a smooth
cushioney top-knot. Above this towered a tall
jet comb in the shape of a cross. The whole
structure loomed so funereally that onlookers
studied it as rather a good suggestion for an effec-
tive tombstone.

“St. Kilda Yacht Club ball, which in way-back
times claimed to be the subscription dance of the
season, was this year a tawdry ghost of its own
self. The girls, in many instances, had laid on
the make-up with a trowel, giving them a ghastly
effect in the full glare of light, and every
other kind of ballroom decoration showed a
sad falling away from the club’s traditions.
None of the Nicest ventured into this ques-
tionable mob save the commandant and his
buxom better half, who wandered around
for an hour or so, like sheep minus a shepherd, and
then departed in a hurry when they found Govt.
House was not expected. Later in the evening a
little wild excitement was caused by the arrival of
Beauteous Mrs. Maesmore Morris in a black dress
with white bead over-bodice. Several men accom-
panied the worn and weary vision, including the
painfully elegant Ferris, who used to look so
young in ‘A Woman of No Importance.’
“The Potter boom still rages among St. Kilda
females, and fantastic coiffures, plastered with
large poppies or roses, would have spoiled the ap-
pearance of many ordinarily pretty girls, even if
they hadn’t been painted an inch thick to begin
with.
“Prof. Marshall Hall’s University Conserva-
torium of Music gave a concert at the Wilson Hall
last week. The bouquet nuisance was particularly
objectionable on this occasion. As soon as a
young lady had warbled or thumped the keys an
old party from the back of the hall would start on
a violent sprint up the centre, armed with a big
bunch of flowers, which the fair singer would
stand awkwardly smiling to receive. It adds a
flavor of romance and an air of charm to these
floral offerings to know that the old party who
carried the bouquets is the University officer who
pickles the corpses for the medical school.
Nearly every music-teacher in Melb. turned up
at the concert, and a decidedly inharmonious
commingling of biassed critics, complacent rela-
tives, and free-show supporters in general crowded
the Wilson Hall to the doors. As a display of
Conservatorium talent the concert was quite satis-
factory to —the Ormond Professor’s professional
rivals. One or two girls sang with good expres-
sion and pure tone, but the vocalising of the
majority had faults on its surface. The contralto
aspirants were wanting in good low notes, a twang
(throaty and nasal) was common to all the
singers, and their pronunciation of English words
was decidedly off. The instrumentalists shone
brightly by comparison, yet without reflecting
particular glory on the Conservatorium, seeing
that several of the best have been playing at
pupils’ concerts and amateur shows for years past,
and ranked as rising artists ere Prof. Marshall-
Hall got hold of them. His Ex., who had said
much the same thing at the ‘Howard ’ pupils’
concert, a few weeks ago, gave a short burst of
lame oratory to the effect that Prof. Marshall-Hall
is ‘ the right man in the right place,’ whilst ad-
mitting his own vice-regal ignorance of the matter
he was talking about. Lady B. faced the music
in a vivid cabbage-green cloak somewhat dazzling
to beholders.

-N.S.VV. Assembly carried, by 59 votes
against only four, a resolution that the states
should not be equally represented in the Federal
Senate. There wasn’t even one influential politi-
cian or one solid old political hack among the four.
Considering that equal representation in the Senate
is the one condition which Tasmania, S.A., and
Westralia absolutely insist on, this shows what a
very wide discrepancy there is in the views of the
large and small provinces.
A great crowd, attracted by a mysterious
handbill, turned up at Brunswick, Vic. (a sort of
head-centre of Irish feeling down South), on Sun-
day the 11th, expecting an Orange demonstration
and consequent devilment, but no one demon-
strated, and the people dispersed in great disgust,
anxiously inquiring for the author of the leaflet.
There is a suspicion that some tram co. share-
holder “faked ” the whole business, inspired, no
doubt, by the fact that the conductors were raking
in money with both hands all the afternoon. On
the next Sunday there was an Orange processioti
and a row.
Some of the preparations for the Melb.
Orange march possessed a certain element of
humor. Two days previously, police-chief Chom-
ley ukased that no constables would be allowed to
leave home or barracks during the day of trouble
except in uniform. (Last year, several “ cops ”in
plain clothes were rumored to have taken a hand
in the fight.) Another preparation was the re-
moval of all loose road-metal within a half-mile
radius of the “ battlefield.” However, the wet
weather spoiled any “ saypous divarshun ” which
might have taken place—was it not the French
general who remarked, “ It rains ; there will be
no revolution to-day”? The best thing of the
afternoon was the charge of Johanna M‘Mahon, a
green-bonneted female, who rushed through the
cordon of police and smote the procession hip and
thigh. When overpowered and run-in, Johanna
was not contrite, and, smiling to the crowd, said
that she had come to Brunswick on purpose to
deal with thim Orangemen,’’and she had done it.
Miss Garran, secretary of the Queen’s
Jubilee Fund for Distressed Women, writes
t ? ditor c Bulletin,— ln a paragraph in the Woman’s
Letter of your issue of sth June, re the Queen’s Jubilee
Fund for Distressed Women, the writer states that this
charity has been “ kept so very select that it gradually
dwindled and pined from amemia.” There has been ho
dwindling at all; on the contrary, the capital fund has
been increased from £16,204 to £17,603. The society’s
income does not depend on annual subscriptions, but on
the interest of an endowment fund, vested in trustees
which has been made up by contributions from all classes
° f ru th e.P eo P le m sums ranging from one shilling to £l5O
What is now desired is to increase this endowment, as the
available income, amounting to £lOOO per annum, is quite
unequal to the claims made upon it. The present request
for additional donations is, like the original request, made
to all classes, and the council hopes that rit-will he
responded to by all. Your contributor ’states that this'
charity is now called by a different name. ’ This is an
entire mistake, as it has never been known by anv other
than the Queen’s Jubilee Fund for Distressed
Women. Nor has there been any change whatever in the
lines on which the income is distributed. The original
committee framed the regulations under which the money
is spent, and the present council, on which all olasses are
regulations* a on nes down by those

An Epoch of Her Majesty’s Reign.
There are many epochs of Her Majesty’s Reign
which are recalled with feelings of gratitude and
thankfulness, but perhaps no one event has
touched the homes and hearts of her people-
caused as much heartfelt praise; brought as
much comfort and joy ; saved, in fact, as many
lives—as the discovery and introduction, some
twenty-five years ago, of Warner’s “ Safe ” Cura.
Until more recently, kidney disease—mora es-
pecially Bright’s Disease—was considered incur-
able, and now we have examples of cures from
these dread diseases on every hand. The most
eminent scientists on the Continent now claim
Bright’s Disease is curable, and many admit that
they have cured it by the use of this medicine.
Dr. Fisher, Govt. Physician, Neuenberg, Ger-
many, says : “I effected a cure in a patient suf-
fering from Bright’s disease with Warner’s “Safe"
Cure after other therapeutic agencies and frequent
consultation with eminent colleagues had failed.”
In the early days of Her Majesty’ reign many ail-
ments and diseases, such as nervousness, insomnia,
dyspepsia, and so-called “ impure blood ” ailments,
puzzled practitioners, but now it is generally ad-
mitted that these ailments result from a torpid or
diseased liver, and Warner’s “ Safe” Cure, acting
as it does directly upon the liver, cures promptly
and permanently these secondary liver ailments.
Examples of its efficacy occur almost daily in onr
midst, and the reputation of this medicine has in-
creased and extended from town to town, city to
city, country to country, until to-day there is no*
a civilised country in the world where Warner’s
“ Safe ” Cure is not obtainable. Mr. W. H.
Mitchell, J.P., of Casterton, speaks strongly in
favor of this medicine in a letter dated 3/3/97. He
writes : “I deem it just to my fellow creatures
who may be victims to flatulency and indigestion,
and the many ailments resulting therefrom, to tell
them I received infinitely more benefit from a few
bottles of Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure than I did from
the numerous prescriptions prepared for me by the
medical faculty for years.”*

The Bulletin notices an increasing ten-
dency on the part of the Hebrews to give their
children Scotch front names—to wit, “ Colin Kor-
minsky,” “Roderick Aronheim.” Painting the
lily, with a vengeance.
Major-General Andrew Clarke, Agent-
General for Victoria, will be 73 on July 27. He
was aide-de-camp to Denison, Governor,
V.D.L., from 1846 to ’4B, then went to fight the
Maoris, and returned to Hobart in ’49, going
thence four years later to become Surveyor-
General and Commissioner of Crown Lands in
Victoria. He was a member of the first Vic.
Ministry, being elected to the Assembly to re-
present the working men of South Melbourne
because of his vigorous railway policy and the
liberality of his views generally. He left Mel-
bourne nearly 40 years ago, and during
the whole of that period has drawn a pen-
sion of ,£BOO a year from the province, ex-
cept when acting as Agent-General.
Zoology-Professor W. A. Haswell,
M.A., D.Se., F.L.S., of Sydney Univer-
sity, is among the 15 candidates nominated
by the council as fit and proper persons to
be elected Fellows of the Royal Society of
London, one of the highest of scientific
distinctions. From these 15, will be
elected by votes of the present Fellows.
Prof. Haswell is author of scores of pub-
lished papers on zoology and compara-
tive anatomy. Also, he is a trustee of
the Australian Museum, vice-presi-
dent of the Linnean Society of N.S.W.,
a corresponding member of the Royal
Society of Tasmania, and of the Royal
German Academy at Halle. His particular
brand of recreation is promenading the bot-
tom of the Beautiful Harbor in search of sea-
things rich and strange.
Joshua Beard, the Streaky Bay (S.A.)
murderer, was hanged at Adelaide gaol on July
10. He is waiting on the banks of the Styx for
Butler, and the two will be prospecting-mates.
Majah Neild seems to have taken a far-
back seat at this year’s big N.S. W. Orange pow-
wow. Because, owing to electioneering exigen-
cies, he publicly prayed for the Pope last year ?
Ghastly, unconscious humor of the cor-
respondent-creature ! “ Beard had recovered his
appetite, and was in splendid health at the time
of his execution.” Splendid health —for the worm!
Australasian, July 10, has an interview
with the yet-living John Bourke, who carried the
first overland mail, Melb. to Sydney, 1838. He
calls the Murray the Hume, because Hume first
saw it, and thinks it is a case of Amerigo and
Columbus.
A Jubilee Mounted Rifleman, writing to
Melb. Argus about the death of Private Sneller
on the voyage ’Ome, said : —“The deceased, who
was 24 years of age, died in full uniform.” One
may have much to be thankful for, even in a case
of accidental death. Full uniform !
The author who wanted the Melb. City
Police Magistrates to let him off, and be remem-
bered in his book, makes one think of Southey.
When he applied to a noble lord for financial help,
the lord said : “ I believe literature is able to take
care of itself.” Southey : “Yes, and of you too.’
At the Maoriland kick-up in London, the
chairman, to Premier Seddon : “We look to you,
sir, materially to increase the population of Maori-
iand ! ” A screecher, which recalls Bucking-
ham’s sotto voce comment upon an appeal to
Charles 11. as the Father of his subjects—“ Of a
good many of them ! ”

THREE WEEKS A WIDOW.
MRS. M‘GEE : “ Good mornin’ to ye, Widdy
Nolan. I hardly knew ye widout your
black eye.”
No practising butcher of whatever civic
dignity should allow his name to appear in print
as having witnessed a hanging. The meat-eating
public is not yet utterly devoid of imagination.
An obviously inspired Sydney D.T. para-
graph states that M.L. Agent-General W P.
Reeves has refused a K.G.M.G.-ship, although
pressed to accept by Chamberlain.
Inventive talent runs in the blood.
Tagell (of Hildyard and Tagel, Melb.), the author
of an improved patent axle, is the son of the man
who, whilst managing Hiram Maxim’s factory at
Erith, perfected the wondrous Maxim gun for the
destruction of mankind at large, and Hingland’g
heathen enemies in particular.
Ex-Premier Jennings was an excellent
Italian scholar and indulged the somewhat pecu-
liar taste of keeping himself au fait with current
Italian literature. W. B. Dailey, who whiled
away his closing years studying Italian, said it
was worth an Australian’s while to learn the
language if only to enjoy an evening with the jovial
Jennings.
“ Reefer ” to The Bulletin :
Your correspondent is mistaken in asserting- that
“ Clancy of the Overflow ” is on White Cliffs opal fields.
A brother of his—John Clancy—is at present on the field
and has been here for nearly five years ; he is the father
of the Booligal “ boys” of that ilk. It is years since Tom
Clancy, (“ Banjo’s ” original) was at “ Overflow ” Station ;
he is now gold-hunting at Mt. Morgan.
Poor Ruskin is said to be in nearly the
last stage of brain-softening. He spends most of
his time planting things in his garden, and pulling
them up again next day to see how the roots have
progressed. But that is not an unquestionable
proof of lunacy. Lots of women who “ take an
interest in gardening ” conduct their floriculture in
much the same way.
Good old attorney Cuthbert, M.L.C., pro-
bably got his knighthood because he is a generally
useful person. He has been in four Ministries—
those of Berry, the then Republican ; Service, the
Conservative merchant; Gillies, the Tory spend-
thrift ; and Turner, the semi-Demoerat and hard
economist. A man who can serve four such
masters is pretty versatile.
One of Attorney-General Want’s best ser-
vices to decency and humanity in his dealings
with the Butler business was his order that the
name of the felon’s people should not be disclosed.
Almost all the near relatives of another well-
advertised assassin, a quiet, law-abiding crowd
till newspaper murder notoriety overtook them,
have since gone to the bad.
The wages of sin is -. “ All the
articles found in Butler’s possession that the police
have not already shown to have belonged to Lee
Weller and his wife are of very little value. They
consist of about half-a-dozen shirts, a suit of
clothes, a leather bag, a couple of handkerchiefs,
and a number of other paltry articles, probably
not worth more than <£l or £2. ”
John Murray, of Patee (S.A.), is set down
as the champion clumsy suicide of the province.
First of all, he blew away his nose and part of
the roof of his mouth with a gun. Then he put
his head in some blasting powder and fired it off,
and blew away all his hair and eyebrows. Next
he pulled out to the middle of the river and stepped
overboard, but he floated ashore, and then, before
he had time to do anything more, he was arrested
by policeman Shanks and carefully run in. Evi-
dently suicide on the instalment plan is a failure.
One Sun Johnson has protested to the
N.S.W. Acting-Premier against what he calls the
“ infamous insult ” of tying one Chinese prisoner’s
pig-tail to the handcuffs of another. But the
writer has seen, in Melbourne, a good two-score of
Chinese gamblers hitched together by their tails,
and, as soon as their fines were paid, they went
away, grinning, and apparently not insulted worth
a cent. All the same, the constable was wrong.
If beauty draws us with a single hair, a large flat-
footed policeman has no business to take the
whole headful.
Dear Bulletin, —As a person under whose
patronymic several homicides have been hanged,
let me laugh at the self-important individual who,
because he happens to be called Butler, writes to
the press complaining that official reticence about
the mountain-murderer’s real name allows hi*
reputed one to “ figure in the criminal annals of
the colony.” Thanks to conspirator-Butler and
others, it’s pretty prominent therein ; and, any-
how, what’s the odds ! “ John Smith ” has
probably committed more crimes—and more good
actions —than anybody else. —A.M.
The knighting of Cuthbert, Vic. Solicitor-
General, is easily understood. Attorney-General
Isaacs refused the “ honor ” because he felt him-
self too young, or too modest, for it “at present,”
and, with his refusal, he bracketed the suggestion
that Cuthbert would be a suitable person to brand.
Result, a titled Cuthbert and an astonished com-
munity remarking, “ If he has earned a K.C.M.G.
to-day what will his senior officer, Isaacs, deserve
in a few years time ? ” Artful Ikey—it is rather
superficial artfulness, though—has established a
comparative claim to an earldom at least.
Sydney’s and Brisbane’s, and Temora’s and
Cooktown’s, and Adelaide’s and various other
places’ old friend, W. H. L. Bailey, now of
London, got very near to death, the other week,
through trying to enter a moving train. While
the little man was laid up for repairs, he took the
first opportunity, so goes the story, to write »
characteristic letter to the railway co., threaten-
ing to sue for fabulous damages, yet expressing a
desire to settle amicably. Whereupon the railway
manager sent his compliments to Bailey, and
stated that, in consideration of his sufferings, th#
co. had decided not to prosecute him with th#
utmost rigor of the law for stepping into a moving
train, unless he aggravated his offence by agaia
applying for compensation. Which settled the
matter quite amicably.

Joshua Beard, Butler’s under-studv, should a*
least be hanged by “ a Christian man,” there is
some doubt whether the S. A. executioner has been
“ saved ” or not ; he has been taken to task by
S. A. Register for his “irreverence” in wearing
his hat during the prayer and pinioning. Judicial
THOMAS BROUGHTON: one of the early
Mayors of Sydney. Was first elected as Alderman
to the City Council in 1837—60 years ago. H[?]
now retired, at a ripe old age, from active [?]
life, but is still hale and hearty.
slaughter is so rare in Adelaide that the too®)
man-butcher may be pardoned for his ignorance of
gallows etiquette. The present scribe has seen
many hangings, and no incident of the proceed-
ings ever revolted him more than the obviously-
sheriff-promoted “ respectability” of the chief
ghoul’s broadcloth, boiled shirt, and—ugh ! —re
verently-bared head. To this very hour, he can
see cold-blooded murder writ in the face of every
smug tradesman in his Sabbath suit of black.

T&E POPE'S LATIN POEM ON
FRUGALITY.
•■»«. • - •»—:
Haling base gluttony will} anxious air.
lie thus laid down the laws of Frugal Fare:
Neatness comes first! Be thy spare table bright
With shining dishes and with napkins white, ,
Be thy Chianti unadulterate,
To cheer the heart, and raise the spirit s weight.
Yet trust not-much the rosy god—in fine,
Be sure that you put water to your wine,
picked be thy grain, and pure thy. home made
bread, . *
Thy meats be delicate and dairy fed;
Tender, not highly spiced, thy food; nor tease
Thy taste with sauces from Aegean seas.
■Fresh be thine eggs, hard boiled or nearly raw,
Or deftly poached, or simply served au plat,
“There’s wit in poaching eggs,” the proverb £3y».-
And you may do them in a hundred ways.
Nor shun the bowl of foaming milk that feeds
The infant, and may serve the senior’s needs* «
Next on the board be heaven’s gift, honey,, placcgi
And, sparing, of llyblasan nectar taste;
Pulses and salads on thy guests bestow—
Even in suburban gardens salads grow—
And chosen fruits, whate’er the times afford;
Let rose-red apples crown the rustic board.
Last comes the beverage of the Orient shore.
MoCca, far off, the fragrant berries bore.
Taste tlio dark Huid, with a dainty lip.
Digestion waits on pleasure as you sip.
On e of the deputation then complained,
■of the insufficiency of the food supplied to i
men who went to the Leongatha labour
colony. They only got porridge, drv bread. '
and tea for breakfast, and were supposed'
to do a good morning's work on such a meal*
as that.

My title’s clear—l’m free from

From Sydney straight to Heaven’s gate
They passed me on. You pass me in.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The S.A. Assembly is quite beyond Treas.
Holder’s control when party feeling gets excited,
and he will be very glad to see big brother King-
ston back again.
Judging from the messes into which his
colleagues have been drifting, Reid was wise in
his endeavor to keep N.S.W. Parliament closed
during his own absence abroad.
“You can’t beat Right,” exclaimed a
pious N.S.W. Minister lately when threatened
with a vote of censure. “We and Right stand
together ! We and God ! ” he hoarsely bawled.
Which beats “ Me and Parkes ! ”
The true philosophy of the Sunday-closing
question is simply that the existing law should be
rigidly enforced until the community is goaded
into smashing it. It is only the spasmodic and
half-hearted administration of such enactments
that keeps them on the statute-book. When the
shoe pinches hard enough it must come off !
THE GOOD DIE YOUNG.
MR. CASEY TURNUP: “Pwhat the divil is all this fuss about the
Quane’s Jewbillay?”
MR. KEW KUMBER : “It manes that the Quane’s rayned sixty years."
MR. C. T. : “ Begor, there’s nothin’ in that, at all, at all. Why,
shure, wouldn’t the other Quanes have rayned that long if
they’d stayed alive? ”
The Vic. police force and public service
generally is said—on very good authority—to be
“honeycombed with Orangeism.” If it be true
that men are pressed into the accursed faction by
motives of self-interest, the Orange tree needs a
Government axe at its roots. Who are the roots ?
It is estimated that the average income of
every person in N.S.W. is a little below £5O a
year. Therefore when the State borrows and
spends £1,500,000 per annum, which is rather
below the average, it means that 30,000 people in
N.S.W. (or one inhabitant out of every 40) live by
State borrowings.
That benign but tottering person, Acting-
Premier and cow-dealer Brunker, of N.S.W., now
says that the police won’t prosecute all Sunday-
traders, but only “ fhe most flagrant cases.” The
law, which Brunker approves of and doesn’t pro-
pose to repeal, says that Sunday-trading is illegal,
and Sunday-traders, flagrant or otherwise, should be
prosecuted, and Brunker thus announces that he is
going to look on in a state of mild approval while
the law is set at defiance by the very people who
are paid to enforce it. The reason for Brunker’s
existence grows more problematical every day.
Victoria finds that, out of 160,000 children
dependent on the State schools for education,
something like 60,000 hardly attend at all and are
getting no education worth mentioning.
The recurrence of St. Boyne’s Day re-
minds The Bulletin that the foremost supporters
of Orange ascendancy nowadays are the successors
of the Nonconformist parsons who suffered most
from Orange ascendancy in the past.
Similia similibus curantur. The (paid)
N.S.W. Public Works Committee is appointed to
check log-rolling and corruption. But where is
there more log-rolling than in connection with the
election of that very committee itkelf ?
Under the terms of Caldwell’s Bill in
S.A. Parliament for the election of all J’sP. by
the municipal ratepayers, the maximum number
of honorary justices is fixed at 1 per cent, of the
population instead of about i per cent, as at
present, which is surely quite enough.
The N.S.W. Ministry, The Bulletin pre-
dicts, won’t resume Hyde Park, Sydney, or any
part of it, for a railway-station. The public feel-
ing against the move is too strong ; besides, the
Labor-party opposes the thing—and that settles
the matter so far as Premier Reid is concerned.
By formal enactment to recognise God
Is the mint-tithing, lip-serving Pharisee’s plan.
Humbler teachings enjoin Duty’s path to be trod
In the hope that God’s goodness may recognise man.
The twopenny postage-rate is to continue
in Victoria for at least another year. To revert
to the penny city rate, which was abandoned after
the bcom exploded, would mean a sacrifice of
£40,000, and until things improve a lot more Isaacs
wisely says that he is going to hold on to every
penny with both hand«.
S.M. Herald : “If the
(N.S. W.) revenue is £96,000
deficient it is so much in the
pockets of the people.” This
is about Reid’s deficiency.
Wh enDibbs had a de ticiency,
the logic was that if the ex-
penditure was £96,000 be-
yond the revenue it was so
much that had to come out
of the pockets of the people
later on. So much depends
on the way you put it.
Caldwell, M.P., is
moving in S.A. Assembly
for the election of Justices
of the Peace. Even that
would be better than the
present system of obtaining
the position by Parliamen-
tary influence. The pre-
sent scribe once saw a letter
in which the writer anathe-
matised a M.P. for not
having had him appointed,
and threatened to expose
certain illicit practices by
which the M.P. was elec-
ted, in which practices, the
writer was apparently the
prominent figure. He was,
however, duly J.P.’d and
still adorns the Bench.
That good old Radical,
Longmore, pegs away with
his scheme for making the
great Victorian landlords
cut up and sell their estates.
But the plan of merely or
dering them by Act of Parlia-
ment to cut up and sell is
not very hopeful. A gradu-
ated tax, under which land
that, in 500-aere holdings,
is worth £3 an acre, is only
worth £2 or 30s. in 50,000-
acre holdings by reason of
the extra liability upon it,
is the most effectual remedy.
When cutting up big estates
is the only way to escape a
heavy tax, they would rush
into the market of their own
accord.
It is now pretty plain
that, but for certain tests
which limit the selection of
the Cabinet law-officer,
“ Jack ” Want’s official shoes would be filled by
a saint prominent in Little Bethel and Orange
Lodge circles.
“Boss” Ewi-ng, of N.S.W. Public Works
Committee, displayed questionable taste in writing
to the press to defend the by no means unanimous
decision of that body re the proposed metropolitan
railway centre. Why this ultra-judicial zeal ?
Parson Dill-Macky, at Sydney 12fch July
screech :
I would ask you to rise in all your strength as a body,
and strike for the vindication of your God !
The humor of it! The “ vindication ”of the Om-
nipotent by the Yellow Pup !
A communication reached Sydney one day
lately breaking the news gently that the N.S.W.
Freetrade congratulations to President Cleveland
a few years ago, and the Protectionist congratula-
tions to President M‘Kinley later, were treated
as waste paper by the officers of the U.S., on
the principle that that country repudiates in its
elective or administrative affairs the interference
by praise or blame of foreign Governments or
foreign organised political parties.
“Nick” Willis, M.P., is threatening to
oust Lyne from the N.S W. Opposition leader-
ship in favor of “Jarge” Dibbs, now recruiting
his health in a trip to the Islands. Those in the
know, however, say it’s only a case of “Tom
doing splendidly ” again. They aver that
“ Jaige’s ” name is being trotted out at the present
juncture in the hope that when Reid returns he’ll
be only too glad to rid himself of a rival by pack-
ing him off to London, vice Saul Samuel, retired.
IN PALLIATION.
“ Sir, you are misinformed. lam NOT 'chucking
you ’ for any gray-haired old man of sixty.
He is quite bald, and seventy-five if he is
a day!”
King O’Malley, M P., fathers a measure
in S.A. Assembly for the legitimation of children
born out of wedlock. The ’Murkan Lightnin’
Streak says “the child born illegitimate has to
carry a ten-stone handicap when it keern’t win
with a postage-stamp.” S.A. politics have been
dreary of late, and this latest move of the
O’Malley’s will be a godsend to the press.
The chairman of Brisbane Chamber
of Commerce: “Colonial politics consisted
mainly in borrowing money from England and
then spending it wastefully on dubious rail-
ways and bridges. ”
A South Brisbane (Q.) man was fined,
the other day, for neglecting to supply
proper and necessary treatment for a suffer-
ing horse. The man pleaded that he himself
was suffering from want of employment.
Question : Who ought to be fined for neglect-
ing to supply proper and necessary treatment
to the suffering man ? Don’t all speak at
once, political economists.
The bill of Daniel O’Connor, M.L.C.,
providing for the admission to the legal
trades-union of anyone who had served 10
years in a solicitor’s office and could pass the
needful examination, was thrown out by
N.S.W. Leg. Council so conelusi vely that
Daniel himself was the only man who gave a
vote in its favor. Or rather, Daniel wanted
to give a vote and couldn’t, because no one
would act as teller and count the solitary Dan.
How enthusiastically the Leg. Council curses
the trades-union of bricklayers and demands
free labor in tailoring and bootmaking ! And
how enthusiastically it curses the man who
even suggests comparatively free labor in legal
matters or wants to lay a finger on the
sacred trades-union of law sharks !
The Tasmanian Government announces
that its policy includes the centralisation of
the police force—a much-needed reform; the
encouragement of local manufactures, and the
establishment of a State Land Bank—which
latter is now an essential feature in every-
body’s policy. Also, it promises the reduction
of the police rate, the abolition of the tax on
landowners’ improvements, and the reduction
of the income-tax by 25 per cent. Tasmania
has had two surpluses—it made up the last
one by getting through <£113,000 of borrowed
money in one year, of which sum .£llO,OOO
was for works which yield no return and
which should have been charged to ordinary
revenue. And it has still an accumulated
shortage equal to a whole year’s revenue-
which is the same thing as if N.S.W. had
an accumulated shortage of 9| millions. This
makes the present a really beautiful time to
sacrifice revenue for the benefit of the local
land-owner.
The working man is a hard taskmaster.
One labor man returned to an Australian Parlia-
ment was unpopular at the following general elec-
tion, because he was a toff and a dandy ; another
was objected to because, instead of “ keepingup his
position,” he moved about among his old mates
and used “ langwidge ” ; a third got in by com-
promising—he never bathed and liberally greased
his hair.
Bennett, M.P., Vic., an alleged Democrat,
has been “ moving Government ” to appoint the
Mayors of all municipalities J’sP. “in com-
memoration of the Queen’s Diamond Jubilee.”
J.P. is the municipal C.M.G.-ship. It is a pity
that the suburban ham-fat man and the paunchy
soap-boiler cannot have their lust for an earmark
satisfied without adding to the bourgeois block-
heads who swarm in the courts of Justice.
Re running a coach and
horses through acts of Parlia-
ment. At a recent school-com-
mittee election at a country
district outside Nelson, M.L.,
a fracas occurred owing to
rivalry between different
teacher factions. The minority
took possession of the building
(with the help of pick-handles
and other aids to argument).
Result: ©lection declared void
—fresh election. Pinal act:
arrival of minority with nearly
a score of miners’ rights taken
out by young girls, andreturn
of their candidates by a good
majority. The Act reads :
Any person over the age of 14
years in a declared eoldfleld dis-
trict, holding a miner’s right, is en-
titled to vote at a school-committee
election.
Is N.S.W. Colonial Secretary Brunker
acting-Premier or acting-Deity, or only acting the
Goat ? To the batch of pietists who came
to pat him on the back over the “ good work ” of
robbing the Sunday urchin of his pen’orth o’
bananas, he said :
I take an independent course, regardless of any expres-
sion of opinion from the press.
He claims to be accountable to neither press nor
public, but to “ a higher Power,” forgetting that
his £2OOO a-year is paid, not by “ a higher
Power,” but by the public. And the publio,
which pays the piper, has the usual privilege of
calling the tune, and does not hanker after too
much hymn in business hours. Perhaps, how-
ever, “ higher Power ” means the Labor-party.
The second Federal Convention is to meet
on September 2at Sydney. There are now about
24 working days left for the various Parliaments
to get through the Federation Bill. N.S.W.
Assembly has been at it for over two months, and
is still bogged among the earliest clauses, and is
making no progress worth mentioning, while the
Council is still further behind. The Victorian
Assembly is only in the preliminary stages of the
business, and is further behind than the N.S.W.
one. Moreover, as the Vic. Parliament dissolves
at the end of August, it is also necessary to get
the financial statement, the estimates, Appropria-
tion Bill, and other matters of the kind into the
brief period available. S.A. Parliament is also
just starting, and the Tasmanian Assembly is
only thinking of doing so. Queensland has solved
the difficulty by dropping Federation entirely.
Altogether it is a picturesque situation.

GENUINE TESTIMONIALS.
“ Orroroo, Adelaide. —I hereby acknowledge receipt of
cheque, my share in estate, with thanks. I also thank
you for prompt manner you have conducted the business.
—AMY NUTT.”
“ Port Darwin Camp, South Australia. —I purchased
your ‘ Register,’ and found my uncle’s name as having
funds in Chancery. I thereupon instructed you to in-
vestigate the matter, the result of which was I recovered
my share, and advise all to procure your ‘ Register,’ and
have their claims properly investigated by Dougal and
Co., London.—A. WARLAND.”
“ Queen-street, Auckland.—The ‘ Register’ compiled by
you is of very great value. In my own case my mother
was entitled to money lying unclaimed for 50 years in
Chancery. Seeing your book, I noticed my mother’s
name, and was successful in obtaining what should have
been paid more than half a century ago. It is to be re-
gretted your book is not more generally known. —Yours
Faithfully, E. T. DUFAUR, solicitor.”

thanks.—LUKE FARROW.”

unknown to me. I thank you for obtaining money from
court. —LOUISA BAXTER.”

Colonel Olcott, the Pope of Theosophy, is
. 65-year-old, round man with a patriarchal
white beard, a blue eye, and a Yankee business
address and drawl. His Sthula (Theosophic for
fighting weight) is 13st. Doubtless this Sthula
superiority, combined with a copious Kama, con-
stant Prana, and systematic Linga Surira enable the
Manas of Olcott to nurture his Buddhi, to keep in
touch with Atma, and to avoid Wesleyanism.
Colonel O. (he is just that letter) was not theoso-
phised until ’72. When going, in the news-
paper interest, to a certain ’Murkan farm
to investigate an alleged apparition, he was
COLONEL OLCOTT , the Pope of Theosophy.
Photo., A. W. A. [?] Colombo.
confronted by a tremendously real and over-
whelmingly convincing phenomenon in the
loveable and aggregate avoirdupois of Madame
Blavatsky. Their eyes met ! the eyes of the
fattest Pope and the fattest priestess ; henceforth
their Sthula, Linga Surira, Kama, and Prana
would work harmoniously together in the interests
of their Atma, Manas, and Buddhi. Or, in other
words, Olcott was (Platonically) a gone coon. In
’79 the two fat ones sat together on their trunks
and fastened them down, and, with the aid of a
derrick, embarked together for the sunny isle 1
of Ceylon. After some years of Theosophy,
the Colonel lost his Blavvy, that is, her Sthula,
by natural causes, but the round old boy has kept
pegging at it ever since; grinding out the
Theosophio journal from the head mill
at Madras, and mashing the world so con-
stantly that to-day Theosophic centres number 400.
“Women” says the Colonel, “ask me questions
on every conceivable subject.” How nice ! And
the Colonel does like it when he lays down the
law to the gentle creatures, lording it over them
the while as a shaggy bull terrier would lord it
over half a dozen mice. Following the Colonel’s
generalisation of questions, here is The Bulletin’s
notion of what the answers might be :
“ Yours is an undeveloped spiritual sight;
Swedenborg’s undeveloped spiritual sight caused
him to see St. Paul in frock coat and top hat.”
“ A little gruel with whisky on retiring.”
“Follow your own intuitions.”
“ Bullock’s liver cut up fine will assist chickens
wonderfully.”
“ Analyse everything by common sense; do not
bow down to Christ, Paul, Mahomet, or any other
man.”
“ This is not my ‘ baby ’ day.”
“ Frills are Foolishness.”
“ There is no religion higher than truth.”
“ Lace looks lovely.”
“You are a part of Atma.”
“ Silk stockings are more beautiful than babies’
bibs, and simple faith than Royal blood.”
“ I am out to ‘indigestion’ and ‘tea’questions.”
“ You are immortal.”
“ Hot brine for chilblains.”
Theosophy, to The Bulletin’s mind, is an occult
religion obviously founded on beef, bread, beer
and Blavatsky, and from the social science stand-
point, it is valuable only as exemplifying the trite
fact that that incomprehensible ass, a human
being, can “ kid ” himself and his fellows into be-
lieving anything about anything.
Lormer, the godly Melbourne be ak and
boss teetotaller, still adjudicates in licensing cases,
despite the dictum of his own board that he
suffered from “unconscious bias.” With due
respect for this decision, the average defendant
always tries to keep his case over till the after-
noon, hoping that the cold-waterman will by that
time have departed. But barrister Eagleson,
defending the Cafe Denat against the hideous
charge of supplying cafe royal to its customers,
hit upon a far more effectual method—he coolly
subpoenaed Lormer to appear as a witness for the
defence ! The wail of a strong teetotaller in his
agony, as Lormer unavailingly pleaded his total
ignorance of the whole affair, was the most
pathetic thing heard in the court for years.

Charters Towers (N.Q.) P.M. says that
0 Inebriates Act has not yet become law. Wrong.
It is law, but it is not being administered. The
Diamaiitina Orphanage, now being slowly vacated,
is supposed fictionally to be an Inebriates’ Asylum
and a Home for Epileptics combined.
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
veil tors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eve-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection-
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
Brisbane Y.M.C.A. has practically ad-
mitted the utter hopelessness of endeavoring to
Christianise the local youth by any of the recog-
nised methods, and, being in a hopeless financial
slough, has decided to sell its hymn-books and other
little things, and spread the proceeds over as
much of its liabilities as they will cover. There-
after, it will lead a new life wherein going slow
will be a prominent feature, and from which en-
thusiastic orgies on ginger-pop will be rigorously
excluded.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centi®
of the block in Bourke-stree* to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb a distance of 060 ft.,
and contains the following 1". departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ;6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11 a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13 a Smiling Gallery i 4, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
The smallest prosecutor on record turned
up recently in Melbourne. He was aged s£, and
his head just appeared over the solicitor’s table,
nevertheless he conducted his own case against a
man who snatched sd. from him in the street, and
got the marauder a month in gaol. The belligerent
mite, after being robbed, had gone in pursuit, and
finally ran down his prey in the Salvation Army
dining-room, where he identified him and gave
him in custody. Plainly the Australian small
boy isn’t a person to be plundered with impunity.
In New Guinea the rubber industry is be-
coming a b'g thing for the foreseeing, who are
planting and growing, and “ milking ” by stripping
the leaves, instead of wastefully and ruinously
“milking” the trunk. Sponge-growing and
sponge-gathering already prove remunerative to
a very keen-eyed man in the Conflict Group.
Also, Melbourne regularly ships abroad loads of
arsenic, which comes back again as “ best
European.” Vic. grape-brandy steadily comes
back as the best product of the “Cognac ” country.
With Maoriland sitting, as it were, on its door-
step, Australian sulphur is “ best Sicilian.” Quan-
tities of treated shark’s liver regularly go London-
wards, and return as authentic “ cod-liver oil.”
Boiled-down penguin-juice miraculously becomes
whale-oil.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Melb. and Sydney race-goers accustomed to
getting about 10 or, at most, 12 to 1 against a rank out-
sider, must find it hard to realise Maid of the Mist’s divi-
dend of £lO5 Is. A few such practical lessons will do
more to encourage the totalisator agitation than all the
talk in the world. The machine must come and the
bookmaker must go. There can be no compromise.
The Millers had an exciting time at Caulfield
on Saturday. They won with Homespun (unbacked), and
ran a couple of close seconds in other flat-races, whilst
each of the jumping events cost the stable a horse. Bon
Ton, the hurdler, broke his useful leg ; Larnook smashed
his shoulder in the Steeplechase. Five well-known
jumpers were killed at Flemington and Caulfield within
15 days—Larrikin, Gratiano, Woonooke, Bon Ton, Lar-
nook ; and yet we go.deploring the “dearth of good
cross-country performers.”
* * * *
Surcingle: “Hayseed is thoroughly a ‘fiddle-
headed brute,’ as the scribes used to write, before big
heads were fashionable. As ugly and monstrous a head
as ever was seen on a racecourse, a regular hippopotamus
one, with a tiny, cunning pig’s eye, not at all like the full
one of the typical thoroughbred.”
* • • •
Surcingle : “ Why not revive the Flemington
water-jump? Only an intrigue put it down, almost in
the last generation, because the crack Babbler was uncer-
tain at water.”
* * * *
A Melb. paper says that Miller, Hayseed’s
owner, in the Grand National, had a remount jockey
ready at every fence in case of accident.
* ♦ * *
A few minutes after the steeplechaser Larrikin
was killed on Flemington course, the usual gang of race-
course ghouls stripped him of his mane, tail, shoes, and
teeth ; and they were going for the hide when Glasscock
appeared and offered up a prayer.
* • * •
On the strength of a published price-list, “on
principal events starting price limit 33 to 1, on minor
events 10 to 1,” a countryman sent to a big Sydney book-
maker a starting-price commission for the G.N. Steeple.
On the morning of the race bookie wired: “ Letter
received attention ; twelve limit.” Had the countryman
been a member of Tattersall’s Club he could have made
Mr. Bookie pay up at quoted price if his fancy had won ;
but not being a member of that select body he would
have had no redress, and bookie could just as easily have
wired his limit as 2to 1. The present writer has before
him the published list of starting prices and the bookie’s
wire, so the bill seems to be a true one.
* * * •
Early in the week Echo and The Meddler were
picked upon as the right peas for last Saturday’s Rosehill
double, and so they started favorites at 5 to 2 and 4 to 1
respectively. Echo won the Flying Handicap after a
great set-to with Sinecurist, but The Meddler failed to run
into a place in the July Handicap, and that gay old dog
Newman, after reported bad gallops during the week,
started at 5 to 1 and scored an easy win from Shaughraun.
Loch Marie and Amourette, both by Lochiel, won the
Granville Stakes and the Nursery Handicap respectively ;
Mr. S. R. Kennedy’s Bernal secured the Steeplechase;
and Standard beat Loch Marie by a couple of lengths for
the Welter Handicap. Three first favorites and two
seconds made things rather sultry for the books.
* * * »
Victoria can boast of about a dozen riders—
“gentlemen” once, now professionals—risking their
necks for the dollars.
* * * *
“Flemington” to The Bulletin:—“ On the
flat at any racecourse may be seen welshers, thieves, and
vags., men who have done time and many who ought to
be, doing time, calling themselves ‘ bookies.’ Each has
a gang of roughs on hand ready for anything. Thus the
lambs are barefacedly shorn right under the nose of the
police, yet the latter can’t see anything wrong, but will
gaol a square man making a fair tote. Why ? ”
• * • •
Townsville Daily Bulletin report of the finish
for Townsville Cup : “ Waterloo swerved badly in the run
home, crossing Checkmate just after passing the rails. It
was fully expected that a protest would be entered, but
the owner of Checkmate (Mr. W. Mooney) would not take
action, as he stated that he was satisfied the cross
did not prevent his horse winning. This was the true
sportsman spirit, and we wish we had more of his
kind racing. Mr. Mooney is the leading Brisbane book-
maker.” Waterloo, by the way, seems to have been the
outsider of the field.
* * * *
A short time ago, a well-known Q. horse-breeder
had 25 horses taken outand shot. “ A few years ago,” he
remarked, “ they would have sold at £5 apiece.” Now
they wouldn’t fetch ss. each, and are only cumbering the
earth and robbing the cattle of pasture.”
Horses are at a discount up Rockhampton way.
At a pound sale, the other day, several went for ss. each,
and a lame one only brought Is. 3d. The cute buyer
boiled him down, got 235. for the fat, and had the hide to
boot.
• . * *
Members of the V.R.C. seem'well satisfied with
the committee, as none of the six retiring members are
to be opposed this year. Mr. S. Miller, owner of G.N.
Steeplechase winner, Hayseed, is one of them.
The Queensland champion, Battalion, will be
seen out at A. J.C. Spring meeting, so Cup fanciers of this
good horse will be able to see if he has retained his form.
6 •. » * *
That smart but unlucky mare, Acmena (Martini-
Henry—Acme), opened a winning account, last week, for
Australian flat-racers by annexing the High Weight Handi-
cap at Newmarket (Eng.). As she is a good stayer, she
will probably account for some more pretentious stake ere
the end of the season.
* * * *
An unlucky horseman is Jack Cameron. He
had the mounts on Black Harry and Larrikin, favorites
for the G.N. Hurdle and Steeple respectively. Shortly
before the Hurdle, Black Harry was killed in a flat-race
and it was decided to run Larrikin for the Hurdle. Then
Cameron got his collar-bone broken while riding Con-
fusion at Moonee Valley. He managed to patch up, how-
ever, and, when going strong in the National, Larrikin
“took off” too far away and broke his neck.
• * * »
The lottery of breeding racehorses is well exem-
plified at ’Ome by St. Simon, for years at the top of the
tree of winning sires, not even figuring in the first 17 this
season. The first four places are held by descendants of
the great Stockwell, a son of the Irish horse, the Baron.
Stockwell was sold for what was considered the enormous
Bum of 4500g5., sinee then three of his descendants have
fetched £14,000 ; £30,000 ; £15,000, whilst another (Galtee
More) is priced at over £30,000. Kendal, the sire of
Galtee More, was also bought back for £lB,OOO, after
being sold for a song to go to Ireland.
* * * *
Amongst the revised rules of racing to be sub-
mitted to V.R.O. members at the annual meeting is the
salutary one that if a bookmaker have any interest in a
horse it shall be raced in the name of all persons having
any interest therein. The proposal that “ all horses
must be ridden out ” widens the path to roping, as, under
the pretence of a vigorous finish, a good horseman can
effectually stop his mount.
* * * *
Despite his ignominious performance in the
Winter Stakes, at Flemington, where he looked very
lusty, Dreamland (Trenton —Nightmare) may prove the
best of the Ballarat division for Caulfield Cup, where he is
“ chucked in ” with 7st. 91b. Wait-a-Bit will wait a bit
longer for Melb. Cup.
* * * *
“ Sweep considerations ” have caused a big entry
list for Canterbury Park Cup, N.S.W. If lucky ticket-
holders be generous, there will be an enormous field, and
Kelso, Steward, Wyalong, and Blue Vest may supply the
winner.
* * * •
The handsome win, with a 26-years’-record
score, of the Victorian riflemen in theKolapore Cup must
be eminently grateful to David Syme, who found the
money to take the team to England.
* * • •
The Australians were not thought to have
“Buckley’s show” for the Kolapore Cup rifle shoot at
Bisley last week, it being considered a certainty for the
mother country or Canada. Victoria and Maoriland,
however, made all the running, and finished with
scores of 751 and 748 respectively, the mother country
finishing next with 732. In the United Service match,
under similar conditions, a picked team of the British
army scored 743.
* * « »
Percy Cavill’s win of the world’s five miles ama-
teur swimming championship, over a Thames course, on
Saturday last, in lh. 6min. 35sec. must surely have been
with the aid of a mill-race tide. Amateur A. Ibbott swam
5 miles 66 yds. in lh. 12min. 27sec. on the same river,
with the tide, on 26th July, ’9l ; but in s till water, in '76,
it took amateur Douglass, the crack Yankee of his day,
lh. 53min. 30sec. to swim three miles, so that no criterion
of the calibre of the British amateurs, as compared with
the Australians, can be taken from the time now cabled.
In ’93 it took the English wonder, Nuttall, 26min. Bsec. to
swim a mile, with three turns, at which rate of pro-
gression, allowing he could have kept up the same speed
for five miles, it would have taken him 2h. 50sec., as
against Cavill’s lh. 6min. 35sec. With Percy Cavill in the
best of condition it is hardly likely any amateur will be
found the other side of the world to beat him a distance.
From infancy Cavill has spent as much of his life in the
water as out of it, under the very best of tuition, and
although he cannot be debarred as ari amateur any more
than Ten Eyck, the son of a professional oarsman, who
won the Diamond Sculls last week at Henley, the fact
remains that Cavill has been reared in swimming-baths
and amongst professional swimmers “ of the first water.”
* * * ♦
“ Colonials” are certainly doing well in England
on water and on land, and it now only needs Newhaven
in his Derby and Cup form to put the Australian sporting
public in the seventh heaven of delight.
* * * *
At Henley regatta (Eng.) on Saturday last, the
Diamond Challenge Sculls were won by E. H. Ten Eyck,
the 18-year-old son of F. A. Ten Eyck, a Yankee pro-
fessional oarsman who was rowing in the days of Laycock
and Trickett. The Diamond Sculls were established at
Henley regatta in 1844, in which year England was simply
invincible in pugilism and sculling, two games at which
she is now badly in the ruck.
» * * •
The semi-final match between Pirates and
Wentworths for the Sydney Cricket Ground trophy last
Saturday did not prove so close a contest as was expected.
Pirates generally, and Macnamara in particular, com-
pletely outclassed their opponents, and when, at half-
time, Pirates had scored two tries and a field goal to
Wentworth’s clean sheet, the game was a foregone con-
clusion for the Outlaws. Wentworth did better in the
second half, but Pirates more than held their own, win-
ning eventually by 17 points to 6.
* * • *
The final match, next Saturday, will be between
Randwick and Pirates, the former with an unbeaten
record, the latter with two defeats, by Randwick and
University respectively. Pirates, however, have played
themselves into form, and Randwicks will, on Saturday
next, find them a much improved team.
* * * *
S.A. football anomalies. South Adelaide and
Norwood have each defeated each other once, likewise
Port Adelaide and South. This apparently accidental
“ happy family” order of things has had a healthy effect
on the gates; and Adelaide football booms again.
* * * *
This year, for the first time, the Australasian
Axeman’s Association holds its annual meeting at Mel-
bourne during Cup week. £750 in prizes, exclusive of
trophies. Chief events : Handicap chopping match, £2OO ;
underhand chopping, £100; champion chopping (2ft.
logs), £75 ; sawing match, £6O. A big plunge, but the
Axemen hope to pull through. In The Bulletin’s opinion
their show will boom. The address of Secretary Nicholls
is Ulverstone, Tasmania.
* * » *
A certain Sydney cycle man has burst out with
a solution of the problem of increase on present possible
bicycle speed. He has dispensed with the chain, and
claims to have refused £5OOO for the N.S.W. rights of his
invention. The invention is alleged to have cost its
diviner £BOO. A match is talked of: M’Conibe to ride the
new bike against Elliott on the usual, over one, five, and
ten miles.
* * * *
Cabled that Hint, of Nottingham, has broken
the world’s cycling record for the 24 hours, covering 414
miles.
* * * *
Said that cyclist Zimmerman, with other cracks,
is preparing for another visit to Australia. Perhaps this
time he’ll come without “ the old Dutch.”

It is proposed to have a trained ambulance corps
to follow bicycle road races in Melbourne. Ridiculous!
A trained undertaker is what’s wanted.

Billmartin writes from Noumea, saying he has
quite recovered his health and is riding in great form.
He has accepted a challenge from two local riders (French-
men) to ride them over 25 miles, one up t’other down.
* * * #
It would appear that Choynski played a terrible
tattoo with his left upon Denver Ed. Smith, until the
latter, in desperation, “ butted ” Choynski with his head,
and the fight was awarded to Joseph on a foul. Followers
of the game in Sydney will remember the meeting
between Choynski and Jim Fogarty at Darlinghurst
Skating Rink. Fogarty had just previously broken a
couple of men’s jaws with his terrible right, and it was
thought that if he could by chance land upon the Hebrew
it would be all over. Choynski simply played a tattoo
with the left upon Fogarty at will, and when Jim, who
was no coward, saw he was simply being made a chopping-
block of, he pulled the gloves off and told the audience
that as he had no possible show of hitting the long
American, he did not see the fun of simply standing
there to be cut to pieces.
* • * *
Jack Griffiths, who swung clubs for 36 hours
recently, has sent a challenge to Tom Burrows. A grue-
some spectacle it will be towards the finish if the two
come together, about the only parallel being the memor-
able women’s walking-match at the old Sydney Exhibition
Building, when the exhausted maidens, supported by
whisky and their attendants, continued their weary
wobble with blistered and bleeding feet.

* • * *
Of W. Warbrick (the eminent Maori footballer,
whose portrait is printed on p. 11) “S.M.” writes to The
Bulletin W. Warbrick, born of Maori mother and Eng-
lish father, has played football for 20 years, beginning at
the age of 11. At 13, in a school match, he met his first
accident in a broken left arm ; then for 9 years he played
and remained whole, but with the forming of the 'BB
Maori team for England he began a chapter of mishaps
by breaking his nose, and in England, as back to the
Maoris, he got his collar-bone broken twice, his shin
splintered once, his left hand-bones broken, while he was
“knocked silly” once or twice a week. His bones knit,
and his flesh heals with a speed surprising to the doctors.
He fractured his larynx in Sydney football only last year,
and the hospital surgeons then shook their heads about
his chance. But, of course, he came through and played
again. He was the only Maori engaged in the first of the
matches of the hour, M.L. v. N.S.W. ; and he got his
shoulder hurt too badly in the first match to play in the
return. He says that the absence of Maoris from the
visiting team is due to the Maoris being not in the cities
but in their own settlements, where they are not affiliated
with the unions, but play football “on their own.”
Swindley, the Maori whom the Queenslanders, when in
M. last year, said was the best forward they met,
is now on one of these settlements. So sure is he that a
truly representative M.L. team could be formed of Maoris,
that he is writing to Dave Stewart, forward in ’BB Maori
team, who has money, advising him to get a Maori team
together and tour them—“the gate of M.L. v. N.S.W., on
July 3, was over £900.” The gamest footballer he ever
saw was Barlow Madigan, a Maorilander of European
parents, “ our best threequarters in England. I played
back to his threequarters, and a basket of eggs as back
would have felt quite safe behind Barlow. When we
were winning he wouldn’t trouble himself, but the
moment the battle turned he was on it. It was splendid
.to see him run, tackle, kick, and charge. He got the
ball and he didn’t then sit down with it and wonder
whether he would get his neck broken or not. But he
was off, his 12st. odd going even-time through the other
side. They gave him a gold-medal down in Auckland
for playing all through the second spell against the ’B7
English team with his collar-bone broken. He died a
year or so ago of inflammation of the lungs. The hardest
time we backs had in the English tour was against the Irish
and the Yorkshire forwards —the big, fast men who came
rushing down in a body with the ball upon their toes.
To fall on the ball, or to snatch it up and charge straight
through was the only way to defend. To play Rugby a
man must not consider his personal safety at all. The
Irish had the best of it at half-time, but in the second
spell, being as soft as the spuds they live upon, they were
run right off their legs.” Warbrick was considered by the
English to be the best back of the day ; at 31 he is not the
agile man he was at 22, when he would spring upon the
ball and save his side in the instant when all seemed over.
He has played for M.L. against England, Ireland, Wales,
N. and Q’sland ; for Q. against N.S.W. and M.L., and
Saturday, July 3, for N.S.W. against M.L. The number of
matches he has played can be estimated from the ’BB Maori
team’s record alone—plaj'ed 116, lost 21, drew 3. He has
never smoked tobacco, nor drunk grog; at Lord Shef-
field’s banquet, he decided to taste champagne, which he
had never tried. He got it down with a grimace ; his
Lordship’s champagne, to the Maori palate, “tasted just
like ink.” And, after all, he has never seen a man killed
on the football field.

A winner of .£1650 in a Tattersall sweep fell
dead upon a Melbourne platform shortly after he had
banked the money. And yet the men who draw blanks
in Tattersall’s seem to live wearily on. Some fellows have
all the luck.
* * * •
Now that “Ranjy ” is sure for Stoddart’s team,
it appears Jackson is no cert. Jackson had long had an
eye on Parliament, and, as he thinks the trip might spoil
his chances, he is inclined to cry off. They are trying
very hard to get Abel, but the little ’un has a horror of the
water trip.
* * * ♦
The Duke of Westminster, considered one of
the best judges of the ’orse at ’Ome, drafted Kendall, the
sire of Galtee More, out of his stud for a small sum, as he
did not consider the horse good enough to breed from, on
account of his stilty fore-legs.
• * * •
Cloncurry (N.Q.), on a recent Sunday, turned
out to see a quarter-of-a-mile flutter between a white
female slavey, riding straddle-legged, and a Chinese cook.
The woman won. What the Cloncurry and other Leper-
land towns want is a stockwhip muster to hunt out the
yellows, the browns, and the darker shades—the aliens to
have the benefit of a decent start from the various scratch
lines to the nearest port of shipment.

John Farrell re-issues through Angus and
Robertson, and with a new title, “ Australia to
England,” his Daily Telegraph poem for 22nd
June. S.M. Herald complains because he hasn’t
omitted two pregnant verses—
Red sins were yours : the avid greed
Of pirate fathers, smocked as Grace,
Sent Judas inissioners to read
Christ’s Word to many a feebler race—
False priests of Truth who made their tryst
At Mammon’s shrine, and reft or slew
Some hands you taught to pray for Christ
Have prayed His curse to rest on you !
Your way has been to pluck the blade
Too readily, and train the guns.
We here, apart and unafraid
Of envious foes, are but your sons;
We stretched a heedless hand to smutch
Our spotless flag with Murder’s blight—
For one less sacrilegious touch
God’s vengeance blasted Uzza white !
Of course, these are true, strong verses. But the
Herald’s attitude is always and inevitably the
attitude of Mr. Pecksniff leaning over Todgers’
bannisters and hiocuping “ Let’sh be moral !’"
—and cover things up. For a respectable
married paper, with presumably very little
to learn, the Herald is unaccountably
shocked at the sight of the naked Truth. It
resents reference to unpleasant topics just as the
grocer-deacon resents reference to his brothel-
rents —his collection half-crown doesn’t smell any
more than the Roman emperor’s historic tax. And
the Herald is greasily “ loyal ’’—loyal to a de-
corated nonentity, a fetish, a superstition; and
disloyal to manhood and womanhood, to Austra-
lia, to Right and Justice. For all these reasons
the Herald objects primly to the habit of calling
the “ dear old mother country’s ” dirty work by its
proper name. And the Herald, so it says, is
“ the leading Australian daily paper.” It means
the leaden Australian daily paper.

Ellis’s Post Office Coffee Palace is the
place to stay at in Sydney, as bushmen know and
note. Charges moderate ; table A 1; accommoda-

plan.*
The Sydney P.0.-Municipality trouble
seems to hinge upon the circumstance that, when
conference about the telephone-tunnel business
became necessary, the P.M.G. considered himself
too extensive a pumpkin to visit Ives in his
haughty civic halls, and the Mayor didn’t think
himself small potato enough to seek the foot of
Cook’s departmental throne. Perchance a parley
by trumpet at the Queen’s Statue would have
brought the dread potentates together !
“In every capital of this, the uttermost
part of your Majesty’s realm, there are bodies of
geographers, explorers, and discoverers which
have reason to bless your glorious and beneficent
reign.” Chief Justice Madden, as president of
the Vic. branch of the Geographical Society, pub-
lished the foregoing edition of the Jubilee Loyalty
Lie. The Bulletin hasn’t such a poor opinion of
Judge Madden’s brain as to suppose that he thought
he was speaking the truth. The Queen, in her
reigning capacity, is just what the British Govern-
ments make her, and no Australian explorers and
discoverers have had reason to bless any official
power, or to suppose that anybody’s reign made a
ha’porth of difference to their enterorise.

OPPOSITE C:P.Q.
MELBOURNE-
At Geraldton, N.Q., recently, Malayta
and Santo had a battle, commencing with fisticuffs
and winding up with spears, clubs, glass-bottler,
and the slaughter of two of the Santo heathen.
Europeans were edified and delighted to find their
doors burst open and hapless savages seeking
refuge from the sanguinary Malaytans among
Caucasian Lares and Penates. “The docility of
the Polynesian is remarkable.” —( Parliamentary
oration.)
Dear Bulletin, “ Melbourne ” aaks
“ Why not a statue to W. J. Clarke ? ” and points
out that the defunct baronet deserved a statue by
reason of his startling generosity. W. J. obtained
the reward of his alleged generosity when his
Gracious Queen dubbed him “Bart.” Practically,
W. J. Clarke went to London, and bid for a
baronetcy. When ho bid high enough, he was
gratified with an Imperial brand, and, having got
what he asked for, all parties should be content.
After W.J. became Sir William there was nothing
“superior” about his generosity. To erect a
statue over the ashes of the original Australian
baronet is to affirm the principle that any absurd
person who scatters a few thousands in quest of
an English gaud is deserving of the adulation of
the Australian multitude. There is one reason
why those moneyed men who are not quite so bad
as other moneyed men who might be mentioned
should have statues, but “ Melbourne ” does not
mention it —the stones will make excellent road-
metal for Posterity.—S.

Dear Bulletin, —
Ayers’ Rock, that strange, smooth, gigantio dome of
nek in the Centralian desert, so well described in Prof.
S lencer's “Larapinta Land,” will shout old Henry Ayers’
name across weird Amadeus to her sister sublimity,
Mount Oiga, long after his paltry title and ephemeral
legislatorship have lost their virility.
Why “ weird Amadeus ? There is nothing in
the least weird about the alleged lake. Lake
Amadeus is a dreary expanse of saline bog,
which has no surface water to constitute it a
lake, and has obtained a conspicuous posi-
tion on the map of Australia under exceed-
ingly false pretences. No water-courses of.any
size or importance run into it, and spinifex sand-
ridges adorn its northern side. Depressing, weari-
some, ugly, uninteresting it might be called,
but certainly not weird. Also, Ayers’ Kock
is a curious geological freak, but it is not
sublime, and it is not a dome. While
as to calling Mount Olga a sublimity ! Anyone
who does that must have “ got ’em ” bad. If such
superlative adjectives are wasted on the insignifi-
cant ridges of Central Australia., how can one
describe Mount Cook, Mount Sefton, or Mount
Egmont—one of the most symmetrical mountains
in the world ? Geographically speaking, there is
not a mountain of any importance in Central !
Australia from Spencer’s Gulf to Van Diemen
Gulf. There is a popular idea that Kosciusko is
the highest peak in Australia, and Mount Cook
the highest mountain in Australasia. The highest
peak m Australia is Mount Townsend, and the
highest mountain in Australasia is Mount Owen
Stanley in British New Guinea.—E.F.

HER TRIALS ABOUT TO OVERWHELM
HER WHEN DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK
PILLS RESCUE HER.
ENDURED YEARS OF TORTURE FROM
INDIGESTION AND DYSPEPSIA.
Mrs. Edwards, a most intelligent and kindly lady
(wife of the oaretaker of the Marrickville Town
Hall), has for a long time been a victim to in-
digestion of an aggravated character. She very
courteously received a representative, and gave an
account of her ailments and their cure. In answer
to his first question, she said :
“ What you have heard is quite true. I have
been suffering for years from indigestion and dys-
pepsia.”
“Will you be good enough to describe your
symptoms ? ”
“ Well, first of all I suffered from sleeplessness,
ringing noises in my head, and an overbearing
pain at my chest. Furthermore, my appetite failed,
and all that would rest on my stomach was soft
food such as milk and arrowroot. But what alarm-
ed me most was the way my heart would go pit-a-
pat on the very slightest exertion. I can assure
you if I walked a quarter of a mile these distressing
symptoms would come on. This frightened me
very much, for I feared I was suffering from heart
disease. As to the remedies I swallowed, why
their name is legion, but unfortunately no good
effects resulted, and I feared my troubles were
neither to be cured nor alleviated. I consequently
began to feel very low and depressed.”
“ It is no wonder you felt low and depressed,
Mrs. Edwards. Sleeplessness alone, when it be-
comes chronic, is sufficient to deprive one of reason.
Ringing noises in the head, too, you must have ex-
perienced trouble from, as deafness in some degree
is rarely unassociated with them. Severe, heavy
pain at the chest is always a marked sign of in-
digestion ; and loss of appetite, the surest proof of
something wrong, is frequently linked to it. You
must indeed have had a severe trial, but to continue
with your story.”
“ One day I read of some cures effected by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pilis for Pale People, and indeed
it proved a joyous day for me. I determined to
try a few boxes of this specific, bought them, and
commenced to take them according to the direc-
tions which I noticed were around each box. After
the first two boxes I felt a decided improvement,
and decided to continne with this medicine. As
I persevered with the treatment I made satisfactory
progress.
“ You said that your heart gave you considerable
anxiety, Mrs. Edwards.”
“ Yes, I was afraid it was diseased, and I’m
glad to find I was mistaken. It took a little time
to pull me together again, for you must remember
I had sunk very low ; but I am happy to state that
I am fairly on my feet again, and am so satisfied
that this cure has been effected by Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People that I have recommended
them to my friends. Yes, you are at perfect liberty
to publish this testimonial, for I consider it a duty
that I owe to the world at large.”
(Signed) JANE EDWARDS.
Anyone with a particle of sympathy in his com-
position will readily concede that life under the
circumstances narrated in Mrs, Edwards’ case is
a failure, and death holds little terror for one so
situated. Imagine even such an existence, and
the thought brings terror with it. It is like bear-
ing a heavy load with little strength to manage it,
consequent upon the denial of food and sleep. No
wonder the heart rebels and seems determined
upon bursting bounds.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills also cure such diseases
as rheumatism, neuralgia, partial paralysis, loco-
motor ataxia, St. Yitus dance, nervous headache,
nervous prostration, the after effects of la grippe,
influenza, and severe colds, diseases depending on
humors in the blood, such as scrofula, chronic ery-
sipelas, &c. In the case of men they effect a
radical cure in all cases arising from mental worry,
overwork, or excesses of any nature.
These pills are not a purgative. They are
genuine only with the full name, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold by
chemists, and by Dr. Williams’ Medicine Com-
pany, Sydney, who will forward, post paid, on
receipt of stamps or post order, one box for 2s. 9d.
or half-dozen for 14s. 9d. They are unrivalled as
a tonic for both sexes.*

In the prosecution of a woman in Bris-
bane (Q.) for selling half (return) railway-tickets,
Crown counsel said : “ The reason that action has
not been taken before is because women have been
principally engaged in the business.” Er, the
man wot lays his hand upon a female, save in the
way of kindness, is not worthy of the name of an
Australian civil servant.
