Dear Bulletin, —The system of starting
trains at the Redfern death-trap is not a matter
of signals only. Yesterday I saw a man leaning
out of the signal-box shouting and gesticulating
at the driver of an outgoing passenger train, who
stopped in consequence. Immediately after a
detached locomotive steamed under the Regent-
street bridge and along the line which the out-
going train was about to take.—T.D.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Victorian general election is due in Octo-
ber, so the new Parliamentary session is to last
two months only. In that brief period Isaacs
hopes to push through the Federation Bill ; a new
insolvency law; a law to abolish the unclean
dairies which spread typhoid in and around Mel-
bourne ; a measure, something after the manner of
the Companies Act, to make life assurance societies
go straight; and several smaller items. If Isaacs
manages all this in two months, then he will stand
out conspicuously as one of the strong men of Aus-
tralian politics. The N.S.W. Assembly, maun-
dering along under the feeble leadership of
Brunker and Carruthers, has been two months in
session and has just finished Clause 1 of the Feder-
ation Bill. Said clause 1 merely says: “This Act may
be cited as the Constitution of the Commonwealth
of Australia.” The Council hasn’t even got so far
as clause 1. And there are only two months left
now ! !
A Boon to Sufferers. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*
“ Medicus ” writes thus interestingly and
suggestively re Ramornie (N.S.W.) meat-pre-
serving works: —
The lately-enlarged and improved Ramornie (near
Grafton, N.S.W.) meat-works are in full blast, and,
if cattle can be secured, the season’s output will be the
largest to date. On one recent day, 168 bullocks were
killed, and the weekly average is about 900. The pro-
prietors have just signed with the great English firm of
Crosse and Blaokwell for the supply of 72 tons of meat
extract, at 4s. per lb. People who have been paying 2s.
and 2s. 6d. for a microscopic pot of “ Liebig’s,” will see
from this that there must be a pretty fair margin of profit
to the Baron’s firm, which simply buys the extract from
meat-works all over the world, and, “blending” to uniform
grade and strength with fat, sells it over its own label.
In former years the blood from the slaughtered beasts
was discharged into the River Orara, and as this stream
has but little fall from the works to where it joins the
Clarence, the refuse simply moved down and up with the
ebb and flow of the tide. As the weather grew hot and
the refuse accumulated the smell was, to say the least,
not nice ; neither was the river the sort of clear, pellucid
stream that poets rave about. This season the blood is
mixed with sawdust and sulphuric acid, and thus con-
verted into manure. The blood was the only waste pro-
duct, and now positively not an ounce of the beast goes
to waste. Many people who have lived within 20
miles of the works all their lives have never seen
them, hence have no shadow of an idea of the immense
value of the industry. The writer doubts if there
is in N.S.W. to-day a business investment that in any way
approaches Ramornie as a paying concern. The place is a
veritable hive of industry. All the timber for cases and
casks is sawn on the flat and made up on the premises by
contract. In fact everything in connection with the
operation of turning a bullock into tinned meat is done by
contract, so that the proprietors can tell to a farthing
what every tin of meat and every pound of extract costs.
Electric-light is installed throughout, and up-to-date
machinery is the rule. Many of the employes have been
years at the works—in fact, many of them were born
there, and they are steady, well-to-do men. The writer
was present last year during the visit of the Govt, inspec-
tor. As the inspector (a doctor) “cut down”— i.e., con-
demned—beast after beast, it was astonishing to see
the look on the face of the sellers of the cattle who
happened to be present. One fellow, as the inspec-
tor “cut down” the sixth out of a mob of 100 or
so, shrieked, “Great God, man, you will ruin me 1
What shall I do?” What he did do was to meet
the doctor as that gentleman returned from break-
fast, and, taking him aside, whisper as he tried to thrust
“ something that rustled ” into his hand, “ I say, can’t
you go a bit easy on those cattle ? I’ll make it square
with you.” The inspector’s “remarks” in reply were
brief, but full of vim. “ Well,” sorrowfully remarked tie
cattleman, “ anyway, keep the fiver, and if you will only
cut down one more for my brother-in-law than you did for
me, I’ll think the money well spent. The confounded fellow
has been grinning at me all the morning, and I want to
have a throw at him.”

r>«ar Bulletin, — I see that a correspondent
complains of the Sydney fruit agents. Hobart middle-
are iust as slippery. Some fruit-buyers’ weights are
percent short of true weight. The same in other
r ph I knew a man who sent several cargoes of bark to
wnhart He coifldn’t understand why it weighed so light,
fnd came up with one consignment to see fair play He
™.fnhed the bark as it was carted from the wharf and
weighed, and put a chalk mark under every dray. Each
drav-load was weighed on a weigh-bridge and one dray
weighed separately to get the nett weight That dray
was a very heavy one, the others much lighter. When
the carting was done he interviewed that bark-buyer and
afd severfl things. The buyer tried to bluff it off but
the seller took him into the yard, and showed his chalk-
marks The buyer paid hush-money. Of course, he was
a pillar of the church. They always are A favorite
dodge of some commission-agents it to sell the produce as
soon as it reaches them and report it as unsold. The
farmer then gets an advance on his produce. Some months
hence the stuff is “ sold ” and the farmer gets a little bill,
commission on selling, storage, and interest on money
advanced. The agent thus makes four profits, where he
is entitled only to one :-(l) commission on sale; (2)
storage (3) interest on advance ; (4) interest on price of
the produce which has been, for 6 months, in the agents
bank instead of the farmer’s. And yet they talk about
sleepy Hobart.—Ybllowbush. # #
His little niece in Scawtlan’ sent a Brisbane
(O ) man per parcels post, three pairs of hand-knitted
woollen socks. The P.O. demanded 9d., as Customs duty,
before delivery. Being Scawtch, the addressee replied,
declining to pay 9d., as the socks were for personal use
and not for sale, barter, or exchange, and that, had they
come out in a passenger’s luggage, they would have been
admitted free; also, that all postal rates had been fully
prepaid. The P.O. corresponded with the Customs ; the
Customs replied ; and, three weeks after, Mac. was in-
formed that 9d. was insisted on. Bein’ vera Scawtch, ye
ken, he wrote to the Home-Secretary, asking to be relieved
of the impost. The Home-Secretary sent the letter on to
the Treasury Department, and, finally, the Treasury in-
formed Mac. that it couldn’t let him off the 9d. Mac.
paid, but, when his heart’s blood had oozed out drop by
drop, till the tally of 9 was called, he joyously exclaimed
“ Hech ! mon ; ut’s scaur’d me o’ auchteen bawbees, but
Aw wunner hoo mony bawbees it’s been until they chiels
at the Post Office, the Customs, an’ the Treasury? Aboot
saxteen poon’s stairlin’, I reckon. Let’s hae a dram.”

It does not need a big stretch of imagination to
admit the possibility of a £500,000 tote on Melb. Cup, but
of this £450,000 would go to the backers of the winning
horse, £lO,OOO to charity, and £40,000 as added money to
the prize. The winning owner, without risking a shilling
in bets, could land a pile. But there’s no fizz or diamonds
in the whole £500,000 for Ikey Mo.
* * * •
Where is the political genius who will propose
to abolish all legal claims for debt on articles of con-
sumption involving an amount over £2 ? The publican
has been wisely limited as to the extent of his legally
recoverable credit, and has learned to live happily within
the scope of his powers. The fear that poor people would
starve for want of necessaries if grocer and baker were
similarly tied down is more imaginary than real. Grocer
and baker don’t go beyond £2 except when they see a
prospect; and habitually the publican exceeds his limit
where he sees a prospect. But grocer and baker can go
on to any amount, with the prospect in one eye and the
bailiff in the other, and when the prospect dissolves can
roar down like a lion on the debtor’s household, while the
publican wipes off his slate all over the limit and says no
more about it.
* * * . *
Australian banks continue their silly practice of
shipping good gold coin to England instead of worn coin.
Coin w’orn within certain limits is now being redeemed
at par by the Bank of England. By shipping worn coin
the local banks will rid themselves of a disagreeable
burden, without adding to the difficulties of exchange
operations. In Australia, the traditional policy of
penalising the last holder of a light sovereign is, of course,
still in force—and it is the banks which have to bear the
brunt of the law. Consequently, in their clearing-house
transactions they are always trying to work off their dis-
credited accumulations on one another. In Australia the
sovereign is only legal tender within a legally restricted
“remedy”— i.e., it is only a piece of bullion when it is
so many grains light. The Bank of England, acting in
conjunction with the British Government, has extended
this remedy—and it is the Australian banks’ opportunity,
if they only had the sense to grasp it. The integrity of
the currency is a responsibility which should fall on the
State, though it is but recently that Britain has recog-
nised the fact, and then only in a half-hearted way. Soon
Australia will look after its own currency, and will not be
wet-nursed by English officialdom as at present. The
new Japanese coinage law makes the State responsible
for the integrity of the metal currency.
* * * *
The monotony of organ-reoitals and services at
the Anglican Church, Charters Towers, is now varied by
the blowing and blasting and dynamiting and other things
in the Daj-Dawn Church Land Co.’s ground adjoining,
where a shaft is being sunk hard by the church building.
The Anglicans in the early days sold their underground
mineral rights to a syndicate of would-be knowing ones,
who tried in vain to coerce the Block and Wyndham
people into buying them out at fancy prices when it was
known that a rich shoot was traced under the church
land. The famous encroachment case and its unexpected
turn were the result. Now, a co. formed by the syndicate
has a shaft down 284 ft., and endless complications arising
from a co-mingling of the exhortations of the parson in
the church and the sanguinary objurgations of the brace-
man at the shaft are extremely probable.
* * * *
Arbitration proceedings between the Rock-
hampton (C.Q.) Harbor Board and the Municipal Council
cost £5898 17s. Id. (Note the penny !) A lawsuit would
probably have cost less than half. Explanation—bar-
risters were imported into the arbitration proceedings,
which thus became an arbitration plus a lawsuit. No
lawyer is wanted within a mile of an arbitration.
• * • •
The credit-foncier-life assurance business now
being done by a local co. was suggested by the contract
of the great Credit Foncier of France with an insurance
co., whereby if the borrower dies before the expiration of
the term of his loan his property becomes unencumbered
and at the disposal of his heirs. Another French insur-
ance proposal involves building-society business on analo-
gous lines.
* * * *
More than one lucky ballotress in the Richmond
Downs, Clermont way (Q.), land-selections admitted,
candidly, to the Land Court which examined into com-
plaints against bona-fides, that they had no intention of
leaving their wage-earning situations, but merely applied
for selections because —well, “because.” The girls were
on the gamble, and knew that a big squatter was ready
to purchase and pay handsomely. One girl—a milliner—
would rather that the examining lawyer “ would not
arst ” whether she wanted to get married. The Land
Court gently relieved the ladies of the land they had thus
won in the non-prohibited tote.
* * * *
One of the grievances placed before Q. Mining
Commission related to “foreign companies,” the in-
stances cited being Sydney companies. Workmen have
been diddled out of wages. It was useless for them to
sue the mining managers, as these were merely paid
servants, and the deputation asked that foreign com-
panies should each be compelled to “have a registered
office in the province, with property or cash to cover one
month’s wages.”
* * * *
The Vic. Mines Minister makes a comparison
between the capital of Yic. mines floated in London and
those dealt with locally. The average capital of local co’s
is £34,000 ; that of London ones over £lOO,OOO. Says the
Minister: “ It is quite reasonable to assume that a mine
might pay fair divs. on a capital of £40,000, used solely
for developing such mine, but would inevitably be a
failure on a capital of £lOO,OOO, £60,000 of which passed
into the hands of the brokers as commission for floating.
■ When the English capitalists recognise the force of this
assumption, and act on it, they will find that Yic. gold-
fields afford almost unlimited scope for profitable invest-
ment.” But this same old sermon has been preached for
many a year, and Bull won’t listen to it. As long as the
system lasts, working mines by co’s must be an absolute
failure as an average. When oo’s buy and work mines as
individuals would, then the industry will succeed. But,
did anyone ever see an individual mine-buyer who bought
and worked mines himself ? We often hear of him, but
where is he ? George Lansell is about as near an approach
to a mine-buyer as the writer knows of, and he seldom
buys alone.
* • * *
Dear Bulletin,— -Why cannot the Postal
Union arrange for the issue of international stamps,
which could be used to frank out-going letters, or be con-
vertible into cash, on a sliding scale of commission,
within the Union ? In these days of universal travel and
correspondence, both old and new business would benefit.
In Sydney no more than lOd.-worth of British stamps can
be obtained from the G.P.O. without extra charge ! For
any excess the commission imposed is 2d. on every sd.
worth : and private competition is prohibited by law. Is
this not tyrannical idiocy? The P.O. objects to the idea,
from a mistaken notion that it would be impossible to es-
timate the sales apportionable to each province or country,
and to adjust the international accounts; but a small
private mark on each stamp would easily secure that es-
sential.—Yours, &c., Ron.
» • * •
Kalgoorlie, so far the leading goldfields town, is
threatened with a rival in the Boulder, a new and rapidly-
growing township. Its proximity to the big mines ensures
the settlement of a large population. Kalgoorlie is
solicitous for the welfare of the young town, and offers to
build tram-lines to it from Kalgoorlie railway station, but
the Boulderites see in this proposal a plot to bolster up
Kalgoorlie at their expense, and have resolved not to
“ take any."
* * • *
The W.A. Govt, has considerably backed down
on its proposal to erect public batteries. In March the Pre-
mier said it had decided to erect mills on the different
centres throughout the province. Now the Acting-Premier
says : “ It was not the intention of Govt, to erect batteries
at any centre as permanent institutions. All contemplated
was the construction of small batteries of from three to
five heads in centres remote from existing mills. Govt,
does not intend to afford any means of relief to those
prospectors who are' now prevented from crushing by the
prices charged by private mills, and no battery at all will
be erected within radius of at least 30 miles of any works
now in existence.” What, too, has become of the pro-
posal in the same direction made last year by the N.S.W.
Mines Minister?

Strong-rooms in interprovincial steamers ridi-
culously belie their names, and it would be as well to put
up a notice asking passengers not to sneeze in the vicinity
for fear of shaking the hinges off the doors. On one
steamer, from which bullion was stolen many years ago,
it was the custom to keep the spare stock of deck mats
with the bullion, but, as this led to loss of mats, the cus-
tom was dropped.

A bigmoneyed man was lately bowled out attach-
ing already-obliterated 2d. stamps to receipts; for the better
covering of his deception he again obliterated the stamp
most effectively with a widely-barred rubber stamp. The
case was squared privately.
In the Vic. public departments this winter the
fires of the bosses are of N.S.W. coal, while the ordinary
clerks are warmed with the cheaper Vic, article.
Thus an exchange :
The Hon. George Thornton is a store-house of Australian
fact and incident. He remarked to a gentleman, the
other day, that while at school in old St. James’s, Sydney,
he saw as many as 22 prisoners in a day walk down the
steps sentenced to death. He also remarked that he
twice saw seven men standing for execution on a public
gallows in Sydney, and many times saw four or five men
five men often—on the gallows, awaiting the final dread
ceremony.
The seven men alluded to were the men hanged
for the Myall Creek massacre, and the Jewboy’s
gang. The boys of the time (1838 and 1842) could
see the hangings from the rocks behind the old
gaol in Lower George-street.
Pirimona.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PiKIMONA was a half-caste. His baptistnal name
was Philemon, but “ Pirimona ” is as near as the
Maori tongue can get to the original Greek. He
lived alone in a two-roomed frame-house, just out-
side the kainga.
He was a pattern for all good Maoris to copy ;
the “ show ” Christian of the kainga; the mis-
sionary’s pet convert.
In course of time Pirimona married. That
made two in the frame-house.
He chose well —a rangatira woman, who had
unquestioned rights to large tracts of land. But
she bore Pirimona no children.
He said it was her fault, but she denied the
soft impeachment. So matters lay dormant for
quite five years. Then Pirimona grew restive,
for the title to the land had been granted, and no
heir put in an appearance. Once every week at
least, or whenever the subject obtruded itself,
Pirimona would taunt his wife with being
pakoko. At last she became angry, and told
Pirimona that the fault was his own.
Pirimona swore big blatant English oaths —
there are none in Maori —and went away to a
kainga 30 miles off.
In a week or so he returned with two young
wahine, each carrying a big basket of kumeras
for Pirimona’s wife. These wahine were to be
handmaidens to that great lady, a,nd hoahoa for
Pirimona —if he so wished. They did all the cook-
ing and cleaning, and were very merry. That
made four in the frame-house.
But one night, first one girl slipped out of the
house and vanished into the darkness, and soon
after the other girl followed. They woke Pirimona
and his wife out of their sleep.
The two hoahoa girls did not come back that
night. But in the morning they appeared,
smiling, at the door of the frame-house, and asked
Pirimona to “come and see.”
They led him and the rangatira woman down to
the stream, and there showed them two new-born
piccaninnies lying in the thick grass, blinking at
their first sun.
Pirimona’s wife stormed and raged. But Piri-
mona only laughed, and the hoahoa girls smiled
contentedly, and the two brown piccaninnies went
on blinking at the sun till their young mothers
took them up and cuddled them to their bosoms.
Pirimona said it was whakamiharo, wonderful,
that his two sons should arrive on the samemorning.
That made six in the frame-house.
When the missionary -tohunga came to remon-
strate with Pirimona, that made seven. ,
Seven is the perfect number.
M.L.
ALFRED A. GRACE.


A MIRACULOUS CURB IN SYDNEY.
GOULD’S TINY TONIC LIVER PILLS
CURE A LADY AFTER
HOSPITALS AND DOCTORS SAID SHE WAS INCURABLE.
A Miracle in Sydney.
SUFFERED FOR THIRTY-FIVE YEARS.
TERRIBLE STORY OF A SYDNEY
LADY.
Had a disease that baffled the most skilled phy
sioians and hospital authorities —sickness com-
menced with a weakened digestion and liver
trouble—liver became congested, and kidneys
acted involuntarily. Doctors were consulted with-
out avail; said her case was hopeless—circulation
became very bad —legs began to get numb and
lifeless. At times the power to move would be
lost entirely. After suffering for 35 years and
everything failing, Gould’s Liver Pills were re-
sorted to, with the results given below.
Mrs. Gilbert, wife of Mr. J. Gilbert, of 187
Abercrombie-street, Chippendale, Sydney, who
Nas for the past 35 years been constantly under
medical treatment, and told by doctors and hos-
pital authorities that they could not cure her, but
MRS. JOHN gilbert’s PHOTO.
(Djes not look as if she was dying.)
only relieve her pains for a short time, was called
on by a representative to get patticulars of her
case.
Mrs. Gilbert proved to be a jovial, pleasant,
little lady, who readily expressed her willingness
to give the reporter all information concerning her
long siege of illness and recovery from the same.
After stating that she had been born in Gloucester,
England, and that she had been in the colonies 13
years, she proceeded with her story as follows :
About 35 years ago [Mrs. Gilbert is 55 years of age
now], I began to have some very distressing
symptoms of ill-health ; my stomach would become
distended, and a sour, bitter stuff would keep
coming up into my mouth. I had terrible pains in
my right side and back, and at times I suffered
from terrible bursting headaches. After a while
my circulation became very bad, and my limbs
would get numb and lifeless. Doctors differed as
to the cause: one said I had Bright’s disease;
another, congestion of the liver; another said it
was dropsy. Sometimes my kidneys would act
involuntarily ; and, again, the pains in my back
would become so unbearable that I could not sleep.
About this time we moved from the Old Country
to Sydney, and all the way out from England I
was sick; and instead of the voyage doing me
good I was in bed when we anchored. This was
13 years ago. Since then the sufferings I have
gone through are beyond description. The cold-
ness and loss of life in my legs continued to get
worse, and at times I would lose all power to move
them until they were thoroughly rubbed and the
circulation restored. Sometime I would be stand-
ing at the edge of a table, and start to walk away,
and my legs would utterly refuse to act ; again, I
would wake up at night, and have to call someone
to rub ray body before I could move. My digestion
at this time was in a terrible state, and I was
continually vomiting up sour bitter stuff, and
belching wind. At times, my head would feel as
if the ceiling was pressing down on it, and I felt
as if I was suffocating, and must be carried into
the fresh air; my heart would flutter, and a cold
feeling come over me, just like death. I often
thought I would be stricken with paralysis, so
numb and helpless would my limbs become. I
have consulted some of the best physicians in
Sydney and at home, have also attended"the Prince
Alfred Hospital, Sydney, but only to get ease for
a time. Patent medicines and everything I have
tried with the same results, until a few weeks ago
my son brought home some Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Liver Pills, which I resolved to try. I followed
the directions very closely, with the result I have
derived such benefit from them that I feel a new
being, am energetic, full of life, and have lost all
that drowry, listless feeling ; feel years younger,
my appetite has increased, and I fully enjoy my
meals ; and 1 may say that I feel like a new woman
through and through. Would I object to have
this published ? No. 1 should like to let every
sufferer in the world know what I owe to Gould ; s
Pills.

Constipation More Terrible
than the Black Blague .
Centuries ago, people used to fear what they
called the pestilence. “ Black Death ” was the
most terrible thing in the world to them. They
feared it as the people now fear the Cholera and
Yellow Fever. And yet there is a more terrible
thing than any of these. It is the commonest
trouble that human beings suffer from. It is so
common that nine-tenths of all the sick-
ness in the world is traceable to it. It is
merely that simple, common thing—constipation.
There is no telling what it may lead to, but it is
sure to lead to something bad. Its immediate
effects are unpleasant in the extreme. It makes
people listless, gives them headaches, makes the
digestion poor, causes dizziness, loss of appetite,
loss of sleep, foul breath, and distress after eating.
All this merely because Nature has been imposed
upon, and has been refused the little help she
needed.
This little help is furnished by Gould’s Tiny
Tonic Liver Pills. You take them until you get
yourself into perfect working order, and then you
stop. You don’t have to take any more, Pills unless
after a while you do • something that deranges
your digestive system again. We are not guess-
ing about these facts, and we don’t ask you to
take our unsupported word for it. Doctors have
found that they are true, and have testified to
them. Let the druggist who says that something
else is “ iust as good ” sell that something else to
somebody else. Don’t let him trifle with your
health for the sake of a little more profit to himself.
Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver Pills are a specific for
billiousness, constipation, headache, indigestion,
piles, face pimples, fullness after eating, female
ailments, pale and sallow complexion, general
weakness, debility, kidney and liver troubles, and
a number of other disagreeable ailments that are
the results of improper food and neglect.
If your chemist or dealer does not stock Gould’s

ceipt of stamps or post order for 2s. fid., or half-
dozen for 13s. fid., by addressing the Australian
branch of
E. A. GOULD, SONS, & CO.,

Condemned to Death.
Mr. Hutchinson, of King-street, Newcastle,
chemist and druggist, was called on by a repre-
sentative of a Sydney paper to get some in-
formation regarding his marvellous recovery
from a long and severe illness. Mr. Hutchin-
son said, m answer to our inquiry, that he
was fifty-two years of age on January last, and
that during the last nine years he had been a great
sufferer from liver trouble, which got so bad that
his skin became perfectly yellow, and he was so
weak that he could scarcely walk. He suffered
also from terrible pains in the shoulders, and had
awful splitting headaches. At times he would
become terribly depressed in spirits, and felt that
he was at death’s door. Mr. Hutchinson continued
this way for years, and notwithstanding the fact
that he tried every possible remedy, and consulted

The Gallants of England.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
t During the Jubilee celebrations an attempt was made
to muster the survivors of the armies of the Crimea and
Indian Mutiny scattered throughout England. The
Prince of Wales reviewed the old soldiers, and presented
each with a Jubilee pipe and a small quantity of tobacco.
Much surprise was expressed when it was discovered that
nearly all the men answered from workhouses, poor-
houses, &c.”]
O, remnant of regiments long scattered
By death in the din
Of battles where squadrons were shattered—
Roll up and fall in !
The deeds that you did shed their lustre
On England, and she —you can trust her
To give fair reward. To the muster—
Roll up and fall in !
Survivors of red Balaclava,
Of fierce Inkerman;
Of those cooped in Lucknow to starve, or
Who cleared the Redan —
Stand forward ! The nation you bled for,
And faced fire and bayonet and lead for—
The nation you piled up your dead for
Calls, “Muster, who can ! ”
What matter though feebly you falter,
Who once marched so trim ;
No feebleness surely shall alter
Your glory, nor dim
The mem’ry of deeds that were never
Surpassed since the world was, nor ever
Shall be while the sword strikes to sever
Man’s life and man’s limb !
With slow, halting footsteps and weary
Sad faces, they come
From workhouse and poorhouse and dreary
Disease-stricken slum—
A handful left over from slaughter
When blood was poured out like waste water ;
W hat fortune of war gave these quarter
When death struck those dumb ?
“ The Prince to review and reward you.”
(Your work bears its fruit.)
Remember your might with the sword you
Once wielded. “Salute!”
Then take your reward from a land full
Of gold—’tis a pipe and a hand full
Of ’bacca diluted with sand. Full
And plain lies your route !
“ Dismiss ! ” (if you like, to the devil),
Depart whence you came ;
Get hence to the poorhouse’s revel
And there bless the name
Of the Prince and the nation you bled for,
And faced fire and bayonet and lead for—
The workhouse is what you were bred for—
Your meed is your fame.
Upper Derwent (Tas.).
W.A.A.
A Tale of Jubilee .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fp ear Bullktin, —A man came into our ward last night,
and, after they had eased his pain, this is the story he
told.— Hermes.]
Lobd bless you ! Charity is mostly advertise-
ment, and nearly all the Jubilee grub went to
them as wanted it least. Anyone can live well
on charity in Sydney if they only likes to crawl
enough fur it. If yer don’t crawl yer can damn-
well starve.
“ I bin down on me back ten weeks with some-
thin’ wrong with me stumick—can’t keep any
food on it; look at me legs, like pipe-steins.
Terrible, ain tit ? Well, while I was downed, the
wife bucked round ; did scrubbing, washing,
needle-work, and anything she could get, to keep
us—there’s three kids, you know. But ’twas no
go. She’d pawned everything we could get ’em
to take ; we’d done-in two landlords, and then I’m
blessed if we could make tucker of it. So
she says to me, ‘ It’s no good ; I must go and see
some of the people that gives out charity.’
“So she goes to boss Creer, but Joey he ain’t
giving out charity to women, only to men as could
work for it; and he scratches his nose and, says he,
‘l’m sorry; but I can’t do anything; yer’ll have
to go to the Benevolent Asylum, and they’ll help
yous there.’
“So she paddles round to the Benevolent, but
there was such a dam crowd of cows there that
she couldn’t stand the slack, and chucked it and
comes home. Well, we had no tucker, and it was
after 4 o’clock in the afternoon, and no chance
of gettin’ any till next morning; and the kids—■
poor little devils ! —-was howlin’ with hunger.
“It was a miserable night, and next morning the
wife gets up early and goes to another charity
place and asks the bloke wot runs the show for
somethin’ to eat. And he says, polite-like;
‘ Madam, I carn’t do anythin’ for yous unless you
brings me a reference.’
“ Says she : ‘ I’ll soon do that.’
“ And home she comes and gets a-searchin’ in
an old box for the reference from her last place
afore I married her. She was a cook and laun-
dress afore we got spliced. Well, she gets the
reference and away she goes, but when she got
back the bloke was out, and, what was worse,
wouldn’t be back till two o’clock.
“So she waits till two, when the bloke comes
back, and she steps in and gives him the reference,
and he reads it and, says he : ‘ Here ; what’s all this
about ? I don’t want a cook and laundress ! ’
“ Says she : ‘ Please, sir, it’s the reference you
told me to get.’
“ ‘No! no ! me good woman ! I didn’t mean
this. You must get a recommendation from a
supsgriber to this society.’
“ ‘ Where'll I find ’em ?’ says she.
“ Says he, ‘ That, madam, it ain’t my business
to inform you.’ So she comes away and gets
pitching about it to some other women, and they
tells her ’bout a doctor and a lawyer what wuz
supsgribers. So she starts off to the doctor’s, and
tells the gell at the door, and the gell takes her
into a great parlour and sez to her, ‘ S’pose you
write a little note, missus ; he’s bein’ consulted by
a lot of ladies, and if you wrote a little note p’r’aps
you’d get it quicker.’
“ So she wrote a little note and the gell takes it
in, but, strike me dead ! he never took no notice,
but jistwent on with the ladies, one after another,
bowin’ and scrapin’ and openin’ the door for ’em
hisself. And he kept my poor wife waitin’ an
hour an’ twenty minutes before he reads the note,
and then he roars out to the servant gell, ‘ Show
this yere pusson out an’ tell her to do some work
or go to the Benevolent Asylum.’
“ So the gell shows her out and, says she, ‘ Mis-
sus, I’m mighty sorry I haven’t any money in my
clothes. ’
“So then she goes round to the lawyer sup-
sgriber, and a clerk sees her an’ she pitches him
her tale and asks him for a reference. And he
goes in to the boss and tells him. And the boss,
he says : * Dam un ! don’t let un in here; let un
go and work.’
“And when my missus gets into the passage
she’s like to drop, and down comes a walloping big
shower of rain, and she waits in the passage for it
to clear off. In pops a crimson hay bag all rags
and dirt. She dives into the lawyer’s office and
squeals out:
“ Shelp me Gawd ! I haven’t had a bite for
four days. It hain’t myself, gents, it’s my poor
little childer hi thinks on. Doee now, sir, think
of me poor little babbies at home. Now, doee ! ’
“ ‘ I haven’t got a ticket left,’ shouts the lawyer
bloke ; ‘ take this and clear out, you drunken
wretch ! ’
‘‘‘Now, doee, sir; make it half-a-crown; a
shillin’ don’t go nowhere with bread thruppence a
loaf; an’ I want meat, an’ bread, an’ spuds, an’
wood, an’ sugar, an’ ’
“ ‘ Well, take it, then, and go to the devil; and
next time you come here I’ll run you in ; by
heavens I will! ’
“ ‘ Thankee, sir ! Yer a kind gentleman, sir,
and I hope the Lord will bless ye, sir. Couldn’t
ye just give me a little note to the ’ciety now
an’ ’
“ ‘ Ring up the police-station ! ’ shouts the
lawyer.
“ Then the old haybag scoots out larfin’.
“ He gave her harf-a-crown to get drunk on,
and wouldn’t give my poor old woman a sprat or a
line to save her and her kiddies from starvation.
Damn him !
“ You see they tell a decent pusson to go and
work, ’cause they don’t go in with their clothes
torn, and their faces grimed, and a stream of tears
running off the end of their nose.
“ How did my wife get on ? Well, I’m telling
you as fast as I can. When the old fowl comes
out of the lawyer’s my missus steps back like and
gets close to a door on the other side of the pas-
sage—she’s always very neat and tidy, my missus ;
you’ll see her on visitin’-day. Well, when she gets
close to this other door—it was another lawyer’s
office —out comes a tall, dark young fellow with a
moustache, and he says :
“ ‘ Good afternoon, madam ! Did you want to
see me ? ’
“ ‘ No, I was tryin’ to get a reference to the
Charity Society, but the gentleman who gives the
orders refused me, and now I’m waitin’ for the
rain to stop.’
“ ‘ Come inside and p’r’aps I can get yous one,’
says he.
“ Lord ! but he was a real gentleman, that chap ;
he takes her inside, and makes her sit down and
tell him about herself and the kids and me, and
then he says:
“‘Mrs. , I sympathises with you very
much.’ And he writes out a letter to take to the
charity chap, and he gives her harf-a-crown and a
lot of tram-tickets to take her home quick, and
then he gives her another letter for me to take to
a doctor what attends hisself.
“ And so she come home with some tucker 1 and
Lord 1 didn’t we eat! ’specially the kids; and
then I takes the note to the dootor, and he sends
me to the horspital, and here I am—the wife’s got
a bit of tucker on hand, too.”
Westralian Hallways .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The through express (which runs ten miles an
hour when plenty of firewood can be picked up
along the line to keep the fire going) pulled up
suddenly and heads went out of the windows
just as quickly. The guard walked along to the
engine, and a pale young man in our carriage
said he thought the pooh-hoo valve had become
complicated with the last coupling.
Then the guard came along and explained mat-
ters to the live-stock contents of each carriage. It
appears the engine had run short of water, and so
could not get up steam; as there was no water
within 30 miles, he hoped the passengers would
be good enough to produce all their drinkables,
otherwise they would have to “get out
and push.” They responded liberally, and
83 bottles of ale and porter, 7/> bottles of whisky,
17 bottles of other intoxicants, and half a bottle
of water (contributed by a clergyman) were
poured without a groan into the tank of the thirsty
engine. After 45min. delay, the express started
again and went quickly, but unsteadily, until
Diedwithfever Junction was reached. Here the
local constable, who was on the platform, viewed
the engine very suspiciously as she staggered into
the station, and, after smelling her all round for
some minutes, decided that she was unfit to be at
large, and ran her in for being drunk and dis-
orderly. This caused another delay of two hours,
when an Afghan and two camels were engaged to
take the train along until we got another engine.
Things went very well until we came to the hill
where the gold wasn’t found (rather vague when
we’re talking about W.A.), and as there were no
brakes on the carriages, away we went at about
103 miles a hour, killing the Afghan and the
camels in the first 30 yards; we went down the
hill and up the opposite hill, then down the hill
and up the opposite hill, backwards and forwards
until there was a dead stop in the gully. Then
the passengers all got out and walked to the ter-
minus, eight miles off, arriving to time 3.40 p.m.
by the time-table, but 24 hours late; everyone
cursing because of the lost grog.—A. A. A.
During a Cingalese law-suit in Mackay the
P.M. thus addressed the interpreter : “ You savee
speak ’em all the same this fellow ? ” Whereupon
the man of tongues, stately and dignified, an-
swered : “ I speak pure English, but I do not
understand the sort of English your worship
speaks.”

Mr. Herbert lias been an officer in the U. S. naval service for the past 16 years.
His duties have taken him all over the world, and he has been on a constant go for
several years, through the Dardanelles, around the famous rock of Gibraltar, in the
Mediterranean, through the Suez Canal, about the South Sea Islands, in Hawaii,
round Cape Horn, to South America, Africa—everywhere, almost, under the sun he
has cruised as a navy officer. On his return from one of liis voyages the strain told
on him. His blood was in a very bad condition, and his stomach was weakened,
and, as he says, he was “ knocked out.” Mr. Herbert says:
‘‘Although I look very hearty now, four years ago my health and physical con-
dition were anything but good. "Then it was that I found the constant knocking
about had at last told on me. My blood was very poor and my stomach as bad. I
had been cruising about in the service for a long time without any restand without
ceasing from my duties. Frequent changes in water and climate, such as I experi-
enced, will weaken and debilitate the strongest of us, and I was no exception. In
fact, I was completely run down, and when I came home my family insisted on call-
ing in the doctor. He prescribed for me and did all he could, but nothing came of
it, and I grew worse. As my blood seemed chiefly the cause of the trouble I thought
I would give Ayer’s Sarsaparilla a trial, seeing it advertised to cure such cases. I
took a bottle in an aimless sort of a fashion, but when I found it helped my stomach
and was commencing to clear my blood, too, I thought thefe might be some real
merit in it, and accordingly began on a second bottle. Well, to make a long story
short, the third completely cured me and restored me to my old-time health. My
blood was better than ever, and my stomach did not bother me in the least. Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla cured me. Now I take it whenever I feel that my system needs it, and
I do not consider myself equipped for a cruise anywhere without a bottle of Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla. It has, I assure yoii, my hearty indorsement and recommendation.”
